==PAGE:3==
Abstract
This work presents an experimental analysis of a DTU 10MW wind turbine on a ﬂoating ten-
sion leg platform at scale 1/60 in simultaneous wind and waves. The tension leg platform was
designed speciﬁcally for the DTU 10MW wind turbine, and a simple and compact design was
chosen with the aim of suggesting a cost-eﬀective solution. The wind turbine, the foundation
and the experimental environment were Froude scaled, while the wind turbine rotor was per-
formance scaled to give the right thrust. The model was tested with three diﬀerent mooring
conﬁgurations in a range of constant wind speeds, and in regular, irregular and focused waves.
The mooring line tensions, the model displacements and accelerations as well as the shear force
between the tower and the nacelle were measured, and this constitutes the basis for the analysis
of the dynamic system.
It is shown that directionally spread waves increase the transverse motion of the model and
reduce the in-line tilt motion. The in-line motion of the model is signiﬁcantly damped for
co-directional wind and waves. A decrease in this eﬀect is observed when the wave heading
angle is changed relative to the wind. Further, an asymmetry in the mooring system relative to
the wave heading angle signiﬁcantly increases the motion perpendicular to the wave heading.
Finally, it is shown that the inclination of the mooring lines leads to a considerable reduction
of nacelle motion and peak mooring line tensions, whereas it increases in-line tilt motion.
In conclusion, these experiments demonstrate the ability of the designed tension leg platform
to support the DTU 10MW wind turbine under the experimental conditions tested. It is shown
that this setup can be used to give important insight into the dynamic characteristics of ﬂoating
wind turbines.

Acknowledgement
The MSc project was carried out at the Department of Wind Energy at the Technical University
of Denmark and at DHI (Danish Hydraulic Institute) Denmark from October 2014 to March
2015, and is supported by the INNWIND.EU project.
The scope of the present work involved a large number of people at DTU Wind Energy and
DHI, and without their help it would not have been possible.
First of all we would like to thank our supervisors Senior Researcher Robert Flemming Mikkelsen
and Associate Professor Henrik Bredmose for their help and support throughout the project.
Robert, your ideas and personal engagement in the design and construction of the wind tunnel
is really appreciated, and we also would like to thank you for your help with the design of the
blades and the nacelle for the model. Henrik, we would like to thank you for being a very
valuable sparring partner in the development of the numerical model and in discussion of the
results. Furthermore you work on developing a code for wave generation is also valued.
Moreover, we are very thankful for the great collaboration we have had with DHI. More specif-
ically we would like to thank our supervisor Research Engineer Nicolai Francis Heilskov, Hy-
draulic Engineer Bjarne Jensen for guidance during the experiments and help with developing
the wave generation code, technicians Poul Hansen and Sune Berthelsen for their daily assis-
tance during the experiments, Senior Consultant Jens Kirkegaard for the eﬀort in providing
wind anemometers for measurements of the wind ﬁeld, Alex Poulsen from the Instrumentation
Department for his assistance in cabling of the strain gauge and the wind anemometers and
help in making the servo motors operational, Henrik Kofod, head of Ports & Oﬀshore Tech-
nology for arranging the open house at DHI. A special thank goes to Jeng Hei Chow, Ph.d.
student at DHI in Singapore for his comprehensive assistance in setting up and conducting the
experiments.
Robert Laugesen & Anders Mandrup Hansen
DTU, Kongens Lyngby
March 2015

Chapter 1
Introduction
1.1
Further oﬀshore, larger turbines
The wind power industry is constantly pushing the limits for traditional bottom ﬁxed founda-
tions, moving them further oﬀshore into deeper waters with increased MW-capacity, and this
development is expected to continue [10]. To date, the foundation with far the largest market
share is the monopile, which my be attributed to its simplicity, and thereby favourable produc-
tion costs. The ﬂexibility of the monopile, however, puts a depth limit to this structure, since
the natural frequencies of the wind turbine and foundation will approach the range of wave and
rotor excitation frequencies when the water depth is increased [11]. Hence, the depth limit of
the monopile is by [10] and [11] regarded to be in the order of 30-40 m.
The jacket foundation does not have this issue and is expected to grow in market share over the
coming years, taking oﬀshore wind into deeper waters. The depth limit of jacket foundations
is by [10] regarded to be around 60 m, while [11] estimates that they can be feasible in up to
100 m water depth, even though they might not be cost-competitive at this depth.
Floating foundations have the potential of becoming competitive at a water depth of 50-60 m
according to [10], but cost estimates by [12] show that some ﬂoating foundation solutions even
become competitive with ﬁxed foundations at 30 m depth. However, ﬂoating foundations are
still within an R&D state, and are not expected to become commercial before 2020 [10]. To
date, only a few ﬂoating foundation prototypes have been tested, but no ﬂoating wind farms
have yet been erected, even though there are a few smaller projects in the planning. The date
of plug-on is unknown to the authors.
1.2
Dominating ﬂoating foundation concepts
In the recent years, increasing attention has been drawn to the area of ﬂoating wind turbines
(FWT), since they oﬀer an opportunity for installing oﬀshore wind turbines in intermediate
and deep water. There are many suggestions for ﬂoating foundation concepts, among which
there is great variety. The four dominating concepts, which will be detailed in the following,
are the spar bouy, the semi-sub, the barge and the tension leg platform (TLP). Most proposed
designs are intended to support a single conventional horizontal axis wind turbine (WT), and
only these will be considered in the following.
1.2.1
Spar buoy
The spar buoy, shown in ﬁgure 1.1a, is a single column cylindrical structure which is ballast
stabilized. It contains a buoyancy tank, and below that a ballast tank, which creates a righting
moment and high inertial resistance to roll and pitch motion [13]. The self stabilised foundation
leaves a possibility for towing the foundation and turbine to its position after assembly, which
1
==PAGE:20==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
(a) Conceptual sketch.
(b) Hywind towed to installation site.
Figure 1.1: The worlds ﬁrst full scale ﬂoating wind turbine, Hywind. Pictures are from Sta-
toil.com [1]
may easy installation. The large draught, however, sets a signiﬁcant minimum depth both for
the installation site and the towing route. The large but simple structure of the spar buoy
results in intermediate production costs, but in turn, the simple catenary mooring system is
relatively low cost, easy to install, and insensitive to bottom conditions [12].
The worlds ﬁrst full scale ﬂoating wind turbine, Hywind, was installed by Statoil in 2009 oﬀ
the South-West coast of Norway, combining a spar-type foundation and a 2.3MW WT. This
prototype foundation has a diameter of 8.3 m, a displacement of 5300 m 3 and a draft of 100 m,
which naturally limits the application of this concept to water depths signiﬁcantly larger than
100 m. Further plans include the installation of the Hywind Scotland Pilot Park in waters
exceeding 100 m depth, with a total capacity of 30MW delivered from ﬁve 6MW wind turbines
[1].
1.2.2
Semi-submersible
The most common semi-sub, comprises three large columns, which are interconnected by a
lattice structure, and is sometimes also referred to as a Dutch tri-ﬂoater. The WT is either
positioned on one of the columns (e.g.
WindFloat), or in the center of the structure (e.g.
the Fukushima ﬂoating turbine [14]). This foundation is stabilised by having a large water
plane area stiﬀness resulting from the column separation [15]. Like the spar, the semi-sub is
self-stabilized giving the same advantages of towing and price of the catenary mooring system.
However, the semi-sub does not require the same water depths which means that it can be
installed and towed trough intermediate water depths.
The relatively high complexity and
large volume of the foundation result in high production costs that are signiﬁcantly larger than
those for the spar buoy [12].
A 2.0MW wind turbine was in October 2011 installed on a semi-sub foundation under the
name WindFloat oﬀthe coast of Aguçadoura, Portugal, by Principle Power [2], see ﬁgure 1.2.
This was the ﬁrst installation of a full scale wind turbine on this type of foundation. The
foundation features a ballast water trim system which mitigates the mean aerodynamic thrust
and so-called water entrapment heave plates mounted at the bottom of the columns which
dampen the motion in heave, see ﬁgure 1.2a It is suggested to install ﬁve 6MW turbines in
350 m water depth oﬀthe coast of Oregon, US, on this type of foundation, but no time frame
for this project has been published [16]. In 2013 a 2MW downwind wind turbine was installed
2
==PAGE:21==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
(a) WindFloat concept sketch.
(b) WindFloat installed at Aguçadoura,
Portugal.
Figure 1.2: A 2MW wind turbine mounted on the WindFloat semisub foundation. Pictures are
from principlepowerinc.com [2]
on a semi-sub foundation along with a ﬂoating substation as a demonstration project before
the planned installation of the Fukushima oﬀshore wind farm [14].
1.2.3
Barge
The barge foundation is self-stabilized by a distributed buoyancy which results from a large
water plane area [13], which also enables towing of the structure. This foundation has a low
draft and applies a catenary mootring system like the spar and the semi-sub.
The french
company IDEOL has developed a barge concept to support a wind turbine, which is claimed
to be competitive with traditional oﬀshore wind turbines from 35 m water depth, see ﬁgure
1.3. This foundation features a moon pool which dampens the motions of the platform, and
Figure 1.3: Illustration of the IDEOL barge foundation. Picture from www.offshorewind.biz.
a dynamic catenary mooring system by which the foundations in a wind farm can move along
3
==PAGE:22==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
mooring lines to optimize the wind farm layout. It is planned to install a 2MW wind turbine
on this foundation by the end of 2015 as a demonstration project, before installing a pilot wind
farm in 2017-2018 [17].
1.2.4
TLP
The tension leg platform (TLP), is a single column structure for which stability is achieved by
virtue of excess buoyancy and tensioned mooring lines, so-called tendons. This means that the
foundation is restrained in heave, pitch and roll motion while it is pliant in surge, sway and
yaw. The bulk of the foundation volume is often fully submerged while the part of the structure
that pierces the water surface is more slender, leading to smaller variations in the buoyancy and
reduced hydrodynamic loading. Some suggestions for TLP designs include ballast to increase
stability, but with limited or no ballast the TLP foundation has the potential of being a very
compact structure compared to the aforementioned spar buoy, semi-sub and barge foundations.
In the work of [12], the costs of three variants of the TLP foundation are estimated assuming
large volume production. The analysis ﬁnds that the production costs are signiﬁcantly lower
than those of the Hywind and WindFloat concepts and even lie considerably below the pro-
duction costs of monopile and jacket foundations for 30 m water depth. The mooring system
for a TLP is somewhat more expensive than the classical catenary mooring system used for the
other foundation types, but nevertheless the comparison estimates a signiﬁcantly lower capital
expenditure per MW for the TLPs than for the Hywind and WindFloat concepts.
In fact
this work estimates that the TLP in 200 m water depth can even become compatible with a
monopile in 30 m water.
Blue H tested a 3/4 scale prototype of a so-called Submerged Deepwater Platform (SDP) 6
months at sea, from 2008 to 2009, see ﬁgure 1.4a. This foundation is a multicolumn structure
but has a taught line mooring system, and is hence similar to the TLP concept. To the authors’
knowledge this is to date the only test of a TLP-like foundation in open water. Future plans
include the construction of a demonstration unit to support a 5MW turbine [3].
The Glosten Associates have developed a compact TLP foundation under the name PelaStar
to support a wind turbine. This concept is sketched in ﬁgure 1.4b, and consists of a cylindrical
lower and a upper column or transition piece which extends through the water surface to the
tower bottom. The foundation was initially designed with three spokes or pontoons [18], but
this has been changed to ﬁve as seen in the sketch. These spokes also represent a substantial
volume and thereby give a signiﬁcant contribution to the buoyancy. Given the compact design,
the foundation is not self-stabilized and can therefore not be towed after installation of the
wind turbine. For the purpose, the Glosten Associates have developed a concept support barge
which can attach to the foundation and after installation of the turbine along quayside, the
barge attached to the wind turbine can ﬂoat to the installation site. The PelaStar foundation
has been designed to support a 6MW wind turbine in the Celtic sea[4], but it is unknown to
the authors whether and when this demonstration project will be executed.
4
==PAGE:23==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
(a)
The Blue H SDP at its test position in
southern Italy.
(b) The PelaStar TLP concept foundation
with 5 spokes.
Figure 1.4:
Proposed TLP concepts.

www.pelastar.com [4] respectively.
1.3
Related experimental work
Concurrently with the increasing interest for ﬂoating wind turbines in the industry and the
aforementioned demonstration projects that have been developed over the recent decade, an
increasing interest for this ﬁeld in the academic world is observed. Here numerous ﬂoating
foundation types are tested, numerically and experimentally, and this work will found the basis
for a maturation of the technology.
The FWT is aﬀected by simultaneous wind and wave
loads, and therefore the structure has a complex dynamic behaviour, which is best modelled
numerically by coupling of advanced hydrodynamic and aeroelastic codes. In the veriﬁcation
of these coupled codes, model tests play a great role, and therefore work within both of these
ﬁelds is needed. This section describes some of the experimental studies concerning ﬂoating
wind turbines that have been carried out over the last decade
In 2005, Skaare et al. [19] tested a 1/47 scale model of Hydro Oil & Energy’s ﬂoating wind
turbine concept, Hywind, in combination with a 5MW wind turbine at Marintek’s ocean basin
in Norway. The model was well instrumented measuring mooring line tensions, nacelle accel-
eration, shear force between tower and nacelle along with rotor speed and blade pitch angle.
A motor was used to control the blade pitch angle, incorporating the motions of structure in
the control algorithm. The wind ﬁeld was generated by a system of fans, and a relatively high
turbulence intensity of 20% is reported. The experiments were used to validate the numerical
modelling of the Hywind spar buoy which was carried out by a coupling of the hydrodynamic
SIMO/RIFLEX code and the HAWC2 aeroelastic code.
Roddier et al. [20] reported tests on a 1/105 scale model of the WindFloat semi-sub foundation
in the UC Berkley ship-model testing facility in 2010. These tests were conducted as part of the
development of the aforementioned full-scale WindFloat foundation. The foundation and the
wind turbine tower was constructed in acrylic, and a foam disc with a diameter of one third of
the rotor diameter was mounted on the hub to mimic the thrust of a rotor. To ensure the right
gyroscopic eﬀect, an electrical motor was mounted behind the disc to drive an aluminum bar.
The wind ﬁeld was generated by a system of ﬁve fans, but no attempts to improve the wind
5
==PAGE:24==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
ﬁeld quality were made, and it is argued that the disc evens out turbulent ﬂuctuations in the
jet. The experimental results of this work were used to validate the hydrodynamic algorithm
in a sophisticated aeroelastic-hydrodynamic model and includes them in an overall technical
feasibility study of the WindFloat.
One of the most comprehensive model tests in waves and wind to date was carried out by
the DeepCwind consortium, University of Maine and National Renewable Energy Laboratory
(NREL) at Maritime Research Institute Netherlands (MARIN) in 2011. The papers of [21]
and [22] report the results while [23] reports the underlaying design considerations. These tests
combined a 1/50 scale model of the NREL 5MW reference wind turbine with three diﬀerent
foundations, see ﬁgure 1.5a. Scaling of the wind turbine and foundation is based on a conserva-
tion of the Froude number as it is tradition in hydrodynamic model experiments. At this scaling
ratio, it is diﬃcult to match mass properties at the same time as maintaining a reasonable level
of instrumentation, and in this work the WT turned out to be 16.6% heavier than the scaling
methodology dictates, which can be considered satisfactory. The tower was constructed from
speciﬁcally sized aluminum tubing to match the ﬁrst bending natural frequency. The blades
were scaled geometrically leading to an under-performing rotor due to the large dissimilitude
in Reynolds numbers of the model and the prototype wind turbines.
The foundations counted a spar buoy resembling the Hywind, a three column semi-submersible
where the wind turbine is placed in the center, and a TLP which resembles the three-legged
PelaStar concept. These structures were generic, and special eﬀort was not put in optimizing
the foundations as they were intended to exhibit general characteristics of the three foundation
types.
The wind turbine model was tested in a combined dynamic wind ﬁeld with a turbulence intensity
in the order of 4% and a series of irregular wave climates in a water depth equivalent to 200 m
in full scale. However, due to the geometrical scaling of the rotor, the tests were run at the
double wind speed to generate the correct aerodynamic thrust.
This problem was addressed in a later follow-up study by the University of Maine and MARIN,
which is documented in [24]. Here two performance-scaled rotors in 1/50 scale and 1/130 scale
were tested on a ﬁxed foundation, and their ability to emulate the performance of the NREL
5MW turbine was investigated. It was found that the 1/50 and 1/130 scale rotors performed
similarly, and it was shown to be possible to tune the right thrust by a blade pitch adjustment
of a few degrees. Further, the power coeﬃcient is markedly improved compared to the geomet-
rically scaled rotor, but as noted in the paper, it is not possible to obtain the right thrust and
torque at the same time, because the lift/drag ratio of full scale turbines cannot be obtained
in model scale.
At DTU Wind Energy a 1/200 scale model of the NREL 5MW reference wind turbine was
tested in simultaneous wind and waves on a TLP foundation in 2012, see ﬁgure 1.5b. The
wind turbine rotor was performance scaled for maximum power in model scale to mimic a full
scale design case, but in the evaluation of this project it was realized that the rotor in stead
should be scaled to deliver the right thrust. Nevertheless, this study was the ﬁrst to report
model tests of a ﬂoating WT in combined wind and waves with a redesigned rotor for the low
Reynolds numbers. The rotor was mounted on a cylindrical TLP foundation with four slender
spokes, all constructed in carbon ﬁbre reinforced polymer, to limit the mass of the model. The
limitation of mass at this very small scale experiment turned out to be impossible to meet,
and the total mass of the ﬂoating wind turbine turned a factor 2.5 of the mass speciﬁed by
the applied Froude scaling. As a consequence, the ﬂoater volume was increased and the spokes
were extended to increase stability. The instrumentation of the model was kept simple with
two accelerometers, one in the ﬂoater and one right under the nacelle and a generator which
could be controlled by a simple electrical circuit to take an approximate rotational speed. The
wind turbine was tested in a wave ﬂume, in which a two-dimensional wave climate could be
generated, and the model was tested in a number of intermediate to very harsh irregular sea
6
==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION

semi-sub) of the DeepCwind study.

(b) The 1/200 scale model TLP
wind turbine at DTU Wind En-

experiments. Picture from
[26].
Figure 1.5: Scale model wind turbines from previous studies.
states as well as regular waves with the same wave heights. The wind ﬁeld was generated by a
so-called open wind tunnel with exit dimensions of 0.8 m×0.8 m, which was constructed specif-
ically for the experiment. The resulting wind ﬁeld had a minimum wind speed of 1.7 m/s and
a relatively uniform wind proﬁle with a turbulence intensity of ∼5%. The blades were pitched
to match the scaled thrust. Under these conditions the FWT was tested with four diﬀerent
mooring conﬁgurations, which counted two conﬁgurations with four vertical mooring lines, one
with four mooring lines inclined towards the tower, and one with eight tendons restraining yaw
motion. To the authors’ knowledge this work is the ﬁrst to investigate the eﬀect of diﬀerent
mooring conﬁgurations experimentally.
In 2012, a 1/40 model scale tests of the NREL 5MW reference machine mounted on the MIT/N-
REL TLP and the OC3-Hywind spar, were conducted at the Danish Hydraulic Institute, and
results are reported in [25]. The MIT/NREL TLP is a relatively large structure which is heavily
ballasted to maintain self-stability in the ﬂoat-out of the foundation and WT in mild weather
conditions. This concept has an abrupt change in diameter in the mean water level, which is not
7
==PAGE:26==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
seen for other TLP concepts, see ﬁgure 1.5c. The foundations and the tower were constructed
in a plastic material and the foundations were ballasted with lead to give the correctly scaled
mass of the foundation. The rotor was geometrically scaled from the NREL 5MW reference
turbine and the blades were constructed in ﬁber glass. As the tests did not include a wind
ﬁeld, the blades were pitched 30 degrees, and a motor was used to drive the rotor at constant
angular speed in a propeller-mode. This generated the right gyroscopic eﬀects and a thrust
force contribution, which however, was not enough to match the scaled thrust at rated wind
speed. Hence, a line, which passed through a pulley to suspend a mass, was connected to the
nacelle to supplement with a mean trust contribution. The model was instrumented with a
six-component force gauge at the tower base and a four-component force gauge right under
the nacelle and mooring line force gauges. Further, accelerations were measured at diﬀerent
levels of the tower, and the rigid body motions were tracked using an optical tracking system.
Tests were conducted in operational environments with and without wind and in a number of
extreme conditions, where regular and irregular waves were used in both cases.
A recent and very comprehensive experimental work was carried out in October 2014 at Ecole
Centrale de Nantes, France, (LHEEA), where a 1/60 scale model of the OC4/DeepCWind semi-
sub was tested as part of the INNWIND.EU project, see ﬁgure 1.5d. Preliminary results from
these tests were accessed through [26] and [27]. The tested foundation is a three column semi-
sub, where the turbine is placed in the center of the structure. In model scale, the columns were
constructed from PVC tubes and they were connected by carbon ﬁbre tubes, which resulted in a
relatively stiﬀstructure. The model was ballasted with a number of dumbbell discs which could
be adjusted to tune the center of gravity. The foundation was tested with two rotor concepts,
counting a performance scaled rotor based on a 10MW upscale of NREL 5MW, and a software
controlled ducted fan installed on top of the tower. The latter is incorporated in a real time
simulation of the platform motion and thus the eﬀect of the aerodynamic damping is included.
Further, turbulence and gusts can easily be tested by adjusting duct fan thrust accordingly,
and misalignment of wind and waves can be investigated by turning the fan. However, the use
of a fan does not scale the aerodynamic torque and gyroscopic momentum correctly [26]. The
performance scaled rotor was designed to deliver the right thrust, but as in the study of [24]
it was realised that it is not possible to match the aerodynamic thrust and thereby the power.
The nacelle was heavily instrumented with individual blade pitch actuators, strain gauges on
blades and shaft and a control system which makes this one of the most advanced model wind
turbines that have been tested on a ﬂoating foundation. The rotor was operated in a wind
ﬁeld generated by a movable wind generator with a wind speed range of 0.5-15 m/s and an exit
area of 3 m×3 m, which had a turbulence intensity below 5% and a wind ﬁeld with reasonable
homogeneity.
1.4
Motivation
In the light of the industry’s tendency to increase the size of traditional bottom ﬁxed oﬀshore
wind turbines to reduce cost of energy, ﬂoating foundation concepts should also be designed to
support these larger wind turbines. The installation of traditional oﬀshore WTs with a rated
power of up to 8MW are already in the planning, and for R&D projects, like those considering
ﬂoating turbine concepts, there is a need to look beyond the current standards. These large
turbines and their foundations can be modelled numerically, but using only this approach will
not reveal whether the models are accurate enough, if they capture all the relevant phenomena
and where they should be modiﬁed. Model scale tests can, if designed and executed properly,
answer some of these questions.
To date, however, most of the reported experimental work on ﬂoating wind turbines has been
based on a downscaled model of the NREL 5MW reference WT at scaling ratios varying from
1/40 to 1/200. Hence, there is a need to conduct model tests with models of larger turbines
8
==PAGE:27==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
in the order of 10MW, such as the DTU 10MW reference WT, to mature the technology for
future applications.
However, tests with a performance scaled DTU 10MW rotor have not yet been reported. Per-
formance scaling of the rotor is essential to represent primary aerodynamic eﬀects of the full
scale turbine. Further, the TLP foundation shows great potential of becoming one of the most
cost competitive technologies for FWTs when a compact design with little or no ballast is
chosen. A TLP has not yet been designed and tested experimentally with a scale model of a
10 MW wind turbine.
Finally, the eﬀect of inclined mooring lines on a TLP has only been reported in very small
scale in [15] on a model with limited instrumentation, which however showed interesting eﬀects
on the dynamics of the structure. There is a need to test this eﬀect in a more sophisticated
experimental setup with more extensive instrumentation.
The present work pursues these issues, from the design and construction of the experimental
setup to the execution of the experiments.
1.5
Project scope
The present work suggests a design for a compact TLP foundation to support the DTU 10MW
reference WT, and the WT and foundation are constructed and tested in model scale 1/60. The
structure and the experimental environment are Froude scaled, while the rotor is performance
scaled to give the right aerodynamic thrust. Hence, this work is the ﬁrst to performance scale
the DTU 10MW rotor, and the ﬁrst to specify, scale and test a ﬂoating foundation design for
a 10MW WT. The design of model foundation and anchoring mechanism allows for the test
of diﬀerent mooring conﬁgurations, including an option for inclination of the mooring lines.
The wind ﬁeld is created by a wind generator, designed and constructed as part of the project,
which can deliver a constant wind ﬁeld. A collective pitch mechanism in the rotor facilitates the
operation of the model turbine like the full scale turbine under constant conditions. The model
is tested in regular waves, irregular sea states, focused waves and white noise wave signals.
Hence, the present study facilitates the investigation of
• The eﬀect of diﬀerent sea states
• The eﬀect of extreme waves
• Description of the wind damping eﬀect
• Misalignment of wind and waves
• The eﬀect of the mooring system
The consideration of these eﬀects is fundamental in the design of a real FWT prototype, and
they must be modelled numerically to get approval of a suggested design. The present experi-
mental investigation can take part in the validation of the numerical modelling of these eﬀects.
This task however, is not within the scope of the present project.
1.6
Report content
Chapter 2 deﬁnes the choice of prototype foundation concept to set the scene for the present
analysis. Further, a brief description of wind and wave conditions along with joint probability
distributions for wind and wave climates are given to serve as a basis for the design study and
the deﬁnition of the environmental conditions in model scale.
In chapter 3 a three degree of freedom rigid body model of the ﬂoating WT is derived, along
with a ﬁnite element model to describe the interaction of the platform pitch and tower bending
9
==PAGE:28==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
modes. Further, it is described how a WAMIT simulation is set up to estimate the hydrody-
namic load on the TLP. These numerical tools will found the basis for the choice of the TLP
design.
Chapter 4 introduces the DTU 10MW WT, and describes how the ﬁnal foundation geometry
is chosen based on the simulation of two simple load cases. Subsequently the full prototype
ﬂoating TLP WT is deﬁned with three diﬀerent mooring conﬁgurations, and the response to
the load cases is modelled and presented.
Chapter 5 introduces the principles of dynamic/elastic scaling, and describes the design and
construction in model scale of the wind generator, the performance scaled DTU 10MW rotor,
the nacelle and WT tower as well as the TLP foundation.
Chapter 6 deﬁnes the experimental setup, and describes the applied instrumentation. In addi-
tion, wind and wave climates for the model scale tests are deﬁned and the test programme for
the experiments is introduced. Further, the data processing is described, including descriptions
of ﬁlters and the identiﬁcation of uncorrelated response peaks.
In chapter 7 the model scale wind ﬁeld and wave climates are measured and characterized
without the presence of the WT model.
Chapter 8 describes the validation of the scale model WT, and gives the results of decay tests
and response amplitude operators based on white noise surface elevation.
Finally, the selected results from the model scale tests in simultaneous wind and waves are
presented in chapter 9.
The conclusions of the present work are found in chapter 10.

==PAGE:29==
Chapter 2
Prototype concept and
environmental conditions
This chapter motivates the choice of ﬂoating foundation type and suggests a conceptual design,
on which the rest of this investigation will be based. Further, a brief description of oﬀshore
wind and wave climates and their probability distributions are given, to serve as a basis for the
design study and model experiments of the present work.
2.1
Prototype concept
The governing challenge of today’s wind power industry is to lower the cost of energy in order
to decrease dependency on government subsidies.
If the industry of ﬂoating wind turbines
(WT) will grow and mature like the industry for ﬁxed foundation WTs has done over the last
decades, it will most likely meet the same challenge. In the study of [12] it was estimated that
TLP type foundations are among the most competitive ﬂoating foundation designs of today,
mainly driven by low production costs which make it up for slightly higher installation costs
due to expensive anchoring. Hence, the TLP foundation shows the potential of becoming one
of the foundation types with the greatest abilities to reduce the cost of energy. Further, it is
noted in [28] that the anchoring system of TLPs may be better suited for shallow waters than
the catenary mooring system of a semi-sub, and that the limited platform motions of the TLP
may reduce turbine and power cable fatigue loads.
Motivated by the economical and dynamic advantages of the TLP foundation, this concept was
chosen for the present study to support a conventional upwind horizontal axis WT.
For the TLP foundation, a simple geometry was chosen, as a detailed design of this structure
is not considered in the present work.
This further eases construction of the model scale
foundation for the experiments, and simpliﬁes the numerical modelling of the ﬂoating wind
turbine.
The foundation consists of a cylindrical transition piece (TP), a cylindrical ﬂoater and slender
spokes, as sketched in ﬁgure 2.1 along with the geometrical parameters that deﬁne the design.
The TP has diameter DTP and height hTP , the ﬂoater has diameter Df and height hf and
submergence s while the spokes have length L, measured from the center of the ﬂoater, and a
square cross section with side length hs.
The TLP is sketched with four tendons, but the number of spokes, ns, is not considered ﬁxed.
The FWT is designed for a water depth of h = 180 m, to match the basin water depth of 3.0 m
for the 1/60 scale model tests.

2.2
Coordinate system
The global (x, y, z)-coordinate system is located at the position of the unloaded structure,
with z = 0 in the mean water level (MWL) and the z-axis pointing upwards. The x-axis is
coincident with the primary wave direction, as sketched in ﬁgure 2.2. Rigid body displacement
in the positive directions of the x, y and z-axes, are denoted surge, sway and heave, while
rotations about the same axes are denoted roll, pitch and yaw in accordance with general
maritime terminology. The surge, sway, heave, roll, pitch and yaw coordinates are denoted
ξ1, ξ2, ξ3, ξ4, ξ5 and ξ6. Further, a body ﬁxed coordinate system, (xb, yb, zb), is deﬁned such
that it coincides with the global coordinate system, when there are no external loads on the
structure.

2.3
Knowledge of the environment
Both from a structural design perspective and from an economical perspective, knowledge of the
environmental conditions are of great importance to the development of oﬀshore wind farms.
Joint wind and wave measurements form the basis for design load cases of the individual WTs,
and wind measurements give a more accurate estimate of the annual year production, which is
essential to investors. Typically, the planning of an oﬀshore wind farm is based on many years
of measurements of signiﬁcant wave height, peak wave period, wave direction and spreading,
mean wind speed and direction and turbulence intensity. Based on these measurements, joint
probability distributions can be generated to deﬁne the design load cases.
These measurements are seldom published, and since wind farms with traditional bottom-ﬁxed
WTs are installed in relatively shallow water, they would not be representative of the conditions
that a FWT meets, when installed in deeper water. Measurements also form the basis for the
environmental conditions of FWT numerical studies, e.g. [29] and [28]. In [29] a preliminary
loads analysis of a FWT on a barge platform is carried out for a reference site in the relatively
extreme environment North-East of Shetland Islands. The joint probability distribution of wind
speed, signiﬁcant wave height and mean wave period was derived from wave data measured from
1973 to 1976 by a wave buoy radar wind measurements from 1985 to 2007. In [28] a dynamic
analysis of a FWT on a TLP platform is carried out based on the joint distributions of wind
speed, signiﬁcant wave height and peak period ﬁtted to 26 years of measurements by [6] for a
13
==PAGE:32==
CHAPTER 2. PROTOTYPE CONCEPT AND ENVIRONMENTAL CONDITIONS
location in the Northern North Sea.
For the design and test of the FWT model in the present study to be as realistic as possible,
the joint probability distributions of the environmental conditions should also be based on
measurements. It was chosen to apply the distribution given in [6], which is sketched in section
2.6 after a general characterizations of wind and wave climates.
2.4
The wind climate
A wind climate can be regarded as a three dimensional velocity vector ﬁeld with velocity
components in all three coordinate directions. Each of these velocity components can be deﬁned
as the sum of a short-term mean wind speed, U, and a ﬂuctuating component, U′ :
U = U + U′
(2.1)
The short-term mean, U, is typically based on a measurement with a duration of 10 minutes
[30, ch.
2.3.3], during which the conditions of a wind climate can generally be considered
constant.
However, U based on one hour measurements occur, and standards such as [31]
include empirical conversion formulas between the ten minute and the one hour mean wind
speeds.
The most basic parameter to characterize the ﬂuctuations in a wind ﬁeld, is the turbulence
intensity, deﬁned as
TI = σU
U
(2.2)
where σU is the standard deviation of the wind speed, which also typically has a duration
of 10 minutes. The turbulence intensity depends on the presence of local disturbances, but is
frequently in the range 0.1-0.4 where the lowest turbulence intensities often occur at the highest
wind speeds. The turbulence intensity also decreases oﬀshore and with increasing height [30,
ch 10.4.3].
The ﬂuctuations can also be characterized by their integral length scale, which is a measure
of the size of the turbulent eddies in the ﬂow. This quantity is determined as the mean wind
speed times the integral of the wind measurement autocorrelation to its ﬁrst zero crossing [30].
Finally, the wind ﬂuctuations can be characterized by their frequency content, which is well
presented in a spectrum.
Due to friction, a boundary layer in the lowest part of the atmosphere is created, which naturally
depends heavily on the presence of obstacles, which can be interpreted as a surface roughness.
Hence, the mean velocity depends on the elevation above ground, or the water surface, and is
commonly described by the power law
U(z)
U (zref) =
 
z
zref
!γ
(2.3)
where zref is a reference height at which the wind speed U (zref) is known, and γ which often
is set to 0.14 for oﬀshore applications [31].
The Weibull distribution is often applied as the probability distribution of the mean wind speed,
and is given by
f(U) = 1 −exp
 
−
 
U
βU
!αU !
(2.4)
where f is the cumulative probability and αU and βU are shape and scale factors respectively
[30].

2.5
Ocean waves
The analysis and description of a sea state is often carried out based on three hour measurements
of the surface elevation [31], in which the conditions of a sea state generally are regarded as
constant.
The most straight forward analysis to make, is to analyse the time series of the surface eleva-
tion, as exempliﬁed in ﬁgure 2.3. Here the surface elevation is divided into individual waves
in-between the zero-downcrossings of the surface elevation with heights H1, H2, ..., HN and
corresponding periods T1, T2, ..., TN. The individual waves could also be deﬁned in-between
Figure 2.3: Surface elevation with indication of zero-downcrossings. Figure is from [5]
subsequent zero-upcrossings, but the down-crossing division is preferable when studying wave-
structure interaction, since wave accelerations in the wave propagation direction are maximum
close to the zero-upcrossing, which by this convention is centred in the wave.
The wave heights in a fully developed sea are with a fair approximation Rayleigh distributed
[32], described by the cumulative distribution function
F(H) = 1 −exp

(2.5)
where Hs is the signiﬁcant wave height [5]. The signiﬁcant wave height is one of the most
important parameters to describe a sea state, and is deﬁned as the mean height of the one
third largest waves. It is also a wave height that comes close to the height estimated visually
by experienced oceanographers [32].
A typical sea state with a duration of three hours, typically contains in the order of Nw = 1000
waves, and the most likely maximum wave height, ˜Hmax can thus be determined from the
Rayleigh distribution by
F(Hmax) = 1 −1

purposes.
A second, and very useful way of characterizing a sea state, is to analyse it in the frequency
domain.
This is most commonly done by computing the variance spectrum of the surface
elevation, deﬁned as

2.6
Joint distribution for wind and waves
In the work of [6] a joint probability distribution of the mean wind speed at 10 m above MSL U10,
the signiﬁcant wave height, Hs, and the peak period, Tp, is suggested, based on measurements
from the Northern North Sea covering the years 1973-1999.
The joint probability density
function, fU10HsTp(U10HsTp) is assumed to be represented by
fU10HsTp(U10HsTp) = fU10(U10)fHs|U10(Hs|U10)fTp|HsU10(Tp|HsU10).
(2.16)

fHs|U10(h|u) is the conditional distribution for Hs, given U10, and fTp|HsU10(t|h, u) is the condi-
tional distribution for Tp, given U10 and Hs. The two latter distributions are based on 3-hour
wave time series. In [6], fU10(u) is described by the two parameter Weibull distribution (2.4),
repeated here for convenience
fU10(U10) = 1 −exp

2.7
Environmental contours
The joint distributions of wind speed, signiﬁcant wave height and peak period are well presented
graphically by plotting the environmental contours, along which the joint probability of a
given combination of the environmental parameters is constant. As oﬀshore wind turbines are
17
==PAGE:36==
CHAPTER 2. PROTOTYPE CONCEPT AND ENVIRONMENTAL CONDITIONS
designed for events with a 50-year recurrence period [31, ch.6.4], the 50-year environmental
contours were plotted to capture the extreme design events. This method is independent of
the structure and does not directly give the 50-year response, but this can be found as the
maximum response on the 50-year environmental contour [34].
The present procedure for deriving the environmental contours, follows the description of [34]
which is given for a two-parameter joint distribution, but here it is extended to a three parameter
space.
First, a physical three-dimensional space, spanned by the three parameters, U, Hs and Tp is
considered, in which a surface exists that is deﬁned by a joint probability of
pf = Tss
Tr
.
(2.23)
Here Tss is the duration of the environmental condition, and Tr is the return period of the
event.
Secondly, a three-dimensional standard normal space is considered, which is spanned by the
variables ˆU, ˆHs and ˆTp and deﬁned by the mapping
FU10(U10) = Ψ(ˆU10)
FHs|U10(Hs|U10) = Ψ( ˆHs)
FTp|HsU10(Tp|Hs, U10) = Ψ( ˆTp).
(2.24)
Here FU10, FHs|U10 and FTp|HsU10 are the cumulative distribution functions (CDF) of the
marginal and conditional probability functions fU10, fHs|U10 and fTp|HsU10, and Ψ is the stan-
dard normal CDF.
In the standard normal space, the environmental contour surface consists of all the points
(ˆU10, ˆHs, ˆTp) that have the distance ς to the origin [35], where ς is deﬁned by
Ψ(ς) = 1 −pf.
(2.25)
In the three parameter space this is a sphere centred in the origin and with radius ς as sketched
in ﬁgure 2.4, and it can be expressed by


ˆHs
ˆTp
ˆU10



(2.26)
This body can be mapped back into physical space using (2.24), which creates the contour
surface in physical space.
Contour lines for Hs and Tp for a given wind speed U10, can be determined using this method
by mapping a circle deﬁned by (2.26), where
γ ∈[0; 2π],
ψ = arccos
ˆU10

from the standard normal space, as marked in red in ﬁgure 2.4, to physical space.
The 50-year contour lines for Hs and Tp were determined in this manner for a range of speeds
Uhub at hub height using the power law (2.3) to compute U10 from Uhub. The sphere radius,
ς, is determined by (2.25), where Tss = 3 h and Tr = 438 · 103 h, which yields pf = 6.85 · 10−6.
These contour lines are shown in ﬁgure 2.5 along with the target prototype scale sea states of
this present study, see section 2.8.

2.8
Selected environmental conditions
For the experiments of the present work, ten environmental conditions (EC) were chosen as
a combination of Hs, Tp and wind speed at hub height Uhub. These are given in table 2.1
in full scale and model scale respectively, where the conversion from full scale to model scale
follows the scaling of length, time and velocity, described in section 5.1. For convenience, the
environmental conditions are referred to as EC1, EC2,...,EC10. The peak periods for EC1, EC3,
EC5, EC6 and EC7 were chosen equal to the peak periods of sea states 1,2,3,4 and 5 in the
INNWIND.EU experimental project conducted at Ecole Centrale de Nantes, France, in October
2014, and documented in [27]. To increase the resolution under operational conditions, EC2
and EC4 were added with peak periods in-between those of neighbouring ECs. The associated
wave heights for EC1-EC7 were determined by requiring a 5% wave steepness based on Hs and
the deep water wave length, L0, determined from the peak period. This yields
Hs = 0.05L0 = 0.05g
2π T 2
p .
(2.28)
Equivalently, the wind speeds for EC1, EC3, EC5, EC6 and EC7 were chosen equal to the wind
speeds for sea states 1,2,3,4 and 5 in the aforementioned INNWIND.EU experimental project,
and the wind speeds for EC2 and EC4 were chosen in-between. The EC10 was chosen as the
50-year sea state with the maximum signiﬁcant wave height, identiﬁed as the top point of the
contour body in ﬁgure 2.5. The parameters deﬁning EC8 and EC9 were chosen in-between the
corresponding values for EC7 and EC10. The implementation of these environmental conditions
is detailed in section 6.2, which describes the test programme.

Chapter 3
Approximate design models
This section outlines some numerical tools which were applied for design purposes in the present
work, including a 3 degree of freedom (DOF) model to determine the response of the rigid body
FWT in surge, heave and pitch, a WAMIT simulation to determine the hydrodynamic properties
of the foundation, and a ﬁnite element model (FEM) to estimate the coupled pitch and tower
bending frequency. The 3DOF model and the FEM were set up from scratch as part of the
design process and it is stressed that these are not state of the art, but meant for design of the
foundation and the WT tower, in a study who’s primary focus is experimental. This section
was separated out from the actual design considerations, which follow in chapter 4, to make it
easier to digest.
3.1
FWT rigid body 3DOF model
3.1.1
Problem sketch
A simplistic dynamic model that includes the ﬂoater, transition piece and the wind turbine is
derived. The model only accounts for planar displacement of the structure in the x, z-plane,
hence reducing the full six DOF problem sketched in ﬁgure 2.2 to a two-dimensional problem
including ξ1, ξ3 and ξ5 DOFs.
The problem is sketched in ﬁgure 3.1a, which shows the structure in an externally unloaded
state, where the structure and the tendons are vertical, and in a loaded state where the structure
is displaced in ξ1, ξ3 and ξ5 with a resulting elongation of the tendons.
The sketch in ﬁgure 3.1a further shows the principal geometrical quantities of the wind turbine,
the TP and the ﬂoater with spokes and tendons, which will be considered in the model.
The distance δ, sketched to the left in this ﬁgure, leaves an option for inclination of the tendons
towards the tower. For δ = 0 the tendons are vertical. The model is derived based on a vector
formulation to simplify the equations. A representation of a vector, br, in the body coordinate
system is given the index b, and is related to the representation of the same vector in the global
coordinates, r, by the coordinate transformation matrix, Tb, which accounts for the rotation
in pitch.

3.1.4
In-line hydrodynamic load
The hydrodynamic load on the submerged part of the structure is determined using the Morison
equation, which gives the in-line load, qhydro, per unit length of the structure as
qhydro(x, z, t, D) = 1
2ρwCDD (u −ub) |u −ub| + ρCm
D2π
4

4

(3.19)
24

CHAPTER 3. APPROXIMATE DESIGN MODELS
where u and ˙u are ﬂuid velocity and acceleration respectively and ub and ˙ub are the local body
velocity and acceleration of the FWT, respectively.
As the FWT is very stiﬀin pitch, it is fair
to assume that
ub ≈˙ξ1.
(3.20)
Further, it is convenient at this stage to take out the body acceleration term, because when
setting up the equations of motion the body accelerations must be collected in the same term.
At this point an added mass term will be added to the inertia term, and the hydrodynamic
force can be kept as it is. Hence, from now on the deﬁnition
qhydro(x, z, t, D) = 1
2ρwCDD


|u −˙ξ1| + ρCm
D2π

4

(3.21)
is applied, but it is remembered that the body acceleration term must be added in the equation
of motion.
The coeﬃcients, CD and Cm, are the drag and added mass coeﬃcients, which
primarily depend on the Reynolds number, the KC number and the surface roughness [33].
The ﬁrst term of (3.21) is the drag, the second term is the hydrodynamic mass force and the
third term is the Froude-Krylov term. The second and third terms are often referred to as the
inertia terms. By evaluation of the Keuglean-Carpenter (KC) number, it can be estimated
whether the load is inertia or drag dominated. The KC number is deﬁned as
KC = umaxT
D
,
(3.22)
where umax is the maximum velocity and T is the period of the oscillatory ﬂow, while D is
the diameter of the structure. In [33, ch. 4] it is shown that the ﬂow is inertia dominated for
0 < KC ≪20 −30, while it is drag dominated for KC > 20 −30. For a wave with height and
period corresponding to signiﬁcant wave height and peak period for EC6, umax = πH
T = 2.9 m/s,
assuming linear deep water wave theory, and T = 15.4, while D = DTP = 9.0 m, gives KC ∼5,
which clearly shows that the ﬂow is inertia dominated.
The in-line force on the structure exerted by the waves, can be estimated by integration of the
Morison load over the height of the structure

3.1.8

3.1.9
Dynamic 3DOF rigid body model
First, a single DOF dynamic model was derived to model the response in surge to the impact of
a wave. However, as it was realized that the tendon tensions depend strongly on the pitch angle,
and only weakly on the surge displacement, it was decided to set up a dynamic 3DOF model.
This enables a more complete insight to the eﬀect of a wave in the tendon tensions, although it
must be stressed that the present model is still very simplistic. To solve equations (3.48) and
(3.49) using an ode45-solver, the body accelerations must be determined in every time step.
These can be determined using fsolve as for the static model, however, computational time
can be saved by linearising the left hand sides of equations (3.48) and (3.49) to determine the
accelerations explicitly.

3.2
WAMIT simulation
The following brief descriptions of the theory behind WAMIT and the setup of the software is
based on the WAMIT 7 user manual, [7].
3.2.1
Theory
WAMIT is a radiation/diﬀraction program that uses a panel method to analyse the interaction
of plane harmonic waves with one or multiple oﬀshore structures in ﬁnite or inﬁnite depth. The
analysed bodies or structures can be located anywhere from the bottom to the free surface and
can either be moving freely, restrained by an external stiﬀness or ﬁxed in one or more of the six
degrees of freedom. WAMIT applies a Cartesian (x, y, z)-coordinate system which is sketched
in ﬁgure 3.3, and is identical to that of the present report, deﬁned in section 2.2. As ﬁgure
3.3 indicates, a separate body coordinate systems can be deﬁned if this is convenient, and the
waves propagate with an angle, β, to the x-axis.
The software is based on a potential formulation of the ﬂow ﬁeld, which can be introduced
whenever the ﬂow is assumed to be incompressible, inviscid and irrotational. How this leads
to the potential formulation will be outlined in the following. We start by deﬁning the velocity
vector, u
u =


u
v
w

,
(3.59)
where u, v and w are the velocity components in the x, y and z-directions respectively. It
is fair to assume that the ﬂow is incompressible as long as ﬂuid particle velocities are small
compared to the speed of sound in the ﬂuid, which holds for most applications of free surface
30
==PAGE:49==
CHAPTER 3. APPROXIMATE DESIGN MODELS
Figure 3.2: WAMIT global and body coordinate systems and indication of wave angle, β. The
sketch is from the WAMIT 7 manual [7].
hydrodynamics. For an incompressible ﬂow, conservation of mass is identical to conservation
of volume, which can be expressed as the zero divergence of the velocity ﬁeld [36].
∇· u = ∂u
∂x + ∂v
∂y + ∂w
∂z = 0
(3.60)
The divergence can be understood as the local volume expansion of the ﬂuid, which must vanish
for volume to be conserved.
Viscous forces are only signiﬁcant in boundary layers, and at relatively high Reynolds numbers,
these are very thin. Given that marine structures often are very large, the Reynolds numbers
are high and hence the bulk of the ﬂuid can be treated as inviscid,[37, p. 6]. From Kelvin’s
theorem it follows that when an inviscid ﬂuid is acted upon only by conservative forces, no
shear stresses are introduced in the ﬂuid, and hence if the ﬂuid is initially irrotational, it will
remain so,[36]. Mathematically, the irrotation condition is expressed as zero rotation of the
velocity ﬁeld.
∇× u =


∂u
∂y −∂v
∂x
∂v
∂z −∂w
∂y
∂w
∂x −∂u
∂z

= 0.
(3.61)
It is shown in [37, p.
106] that the condition of irrotational ﬂow, is a prerequisite for the
velocity ﬁeld to be expressed as the gradient of a scalar function of the three coordinates,
which is denoted the velocity potential, Φ.
u = ∇· Φ =


∂Φ
∂x
∂Φ
∂y
∂Φ
∂z


(3.62)
From mathematics, [38, p. 200], it is known that the rotation of the gradient of a scalar function
is zero, meaning that when the velocity ﬁeld is represented by the gradient of the potential, it
will be irrotational. Insertion of the velocity potential (3.62) in the continuity equation (3.60)
yields
∇2Φ = ∂2u
∂x2 + ∂2v
∂y2 + ∂2w
∂z2 = 0,
(3.63)
which is the Laplace equation and expresses continuity in terms of the velocity potential. The
potential is assumed harmonic in time, which allows for the deﬁnition of Φ as
Φ = Re

(ﬁnite depth)

For ﬁnite depth, this condition deﬁnes zero velocity normal to the sea bed, corresponding to
an impermeable boundary. In inﬁnite depth, the velocity potential and thereby the velocities
vanish for z approaching minus inﬁnity. In addition, φ, satisﬁes the free surface condition,
∂φ

(3.66)
This is a combination of the kinematic surface condition, which speciﬁes the vertical velocity
equal the surface elevation diﬀerentiated once with respect to time, and the dynamic surface
condition which sets the pressure to zero on the free surface. Since the potential is linear in the
wave amplitude, the principle of superposition applies, and the complex potential can therefore
be decomposed into the radiation and diﬀraction components, φR and φD, respectively.
φ = φR + φD
(3.67)
The radiated velocity potential can be further decomposed into six potentials, φj, j = 1, 2, ..., 6,
that each correspond to the wave ﬁeld generated by unit amplitude body motion in calm water
in the j’th degree of freedom [37, p. 287].
φR = iω

(3.68)

of the corresponding velocity, which is transferred directly through the linear diﬀerentiation
operator to the velocity potential. In addition to the conditions (3.65) and (3.66) the radiated
potentials must satisfy the kinematic condition, which in [37, p. 287] is formulated as
∂φj

φj. This condition equates the ﬂuid velocity to the velocity of the body on the body surface.
The diﬀracted wave potential, which represents the incident waves and their interaction with
the ﬁxed body [37], can be decomposed into an incident wave component and a scattered wave
component.
φD = φ0 + φS
(3.70)
The scattered wave potential is the disturbance of the incident wave potential caused by the
ﬁxed body. The normal derivative of the diﬀracted wave potential must vanish on the surface
of the body, since the body is regarded as ﬁxed, which sets up a boundary condition for the
scattered potential as formulated in [37, p. 288].

The outlined wave-body interaction problem is solved using a three dimensional panel method,
which is outlined in [7]. This brief description of the wave body interaction theory, on which
WAMIT is based, and the lack of description of its numerical implementation, reﬂects the use
of WAMIT as a tool in the design phase of this experimental project.
3.2.2
Simulation setup
WAMIT has two principle subprograms, POTEN and FORCE. POTEN solves for the radiation
and diﬀraction potentials, while FORCE computes the hydrodynamic added mass, damping and
stiﬀness coeﬃcients, along with the motions of the body and pressures and velocities on the
body surface [7]. To run the WAMIT software, a number of input ﬁles to control the simulation
are required. The three most important will in brief be touched upon in the following. For
further details, please consult [7].
Geometric data ﬁle (.gdf)
The Geometric data ﬁle holds the coordinates of the panel
vertices that deﬁne the body, and has option for treating the x = 0 and/or the y = 0 planes as
symmetry planes. Supervisor Henrik Bredmose was kind to provide a piece of code that could
deﬁne these coordinates for the TLP foundation without spokes, given TP and ﬂoater diameters,
the ﬂoater height, the submergence of the ﬂoater and an approximate panel dimension. Given
the rotational symmetry of the structure, both the x = 0 and the y = 0 planes were treated
as symmetry planes. The panels of the ﬁnal geometry are plotted in ﬁgure 3.3, which due to
the two symmetry planes only shows a quarter of a the foundation. It was decided to apply
a guiding panel width of Dfπ
50
∼1.1m for the ﬁnal design. A more thorough approach to the
WAMIT simulations would include a convergence study with an increasing number of panels.
However, for the present design purpose, this discretization was applied because the geometry
seemed relatively well resolved.
Potential control ﬁle (.pot)
The potential control ﬁle holds the input parameters for the
POTEN subprogram. This includes the water depth, h, which can also be set to inﬁnite, wave
periods, T, and wave heading angles, β, as the most important. In the present study, h = 180m,
β = 0 and a range of periods between 100s and 0.5s were set.
33

CHAPTER 3. APPROXIMATE DESIGN MODELS

Force control ﬁle (.frc)
In the force control ﬁle, the input variables for the FORCE sub-
program are deﬁned. These are parameters such as the the ﬁxed or free modes, vertical center
of gravity, the radii of gyration, heading angles for the evaluation of the Haskind relations, and
a number of points in which the surface elevation or particle velocity is to be evaluated. In
the present WAMIT simulation, the surge, heave and pitch modes were free as these were of
primary interest in the design phase, while the sway, roll and yaw were ﬁxed. In the standard
set-up of WAMIT, the analysed body is ﬂoating on the free surface, the only stiﬀness of the
system is the hydrostatic one, and the body mass is determined as the mass of displaced water.
However, in an alternative setup, WAMIT oﬀers options for deﬁning the mass matrix and spec-
ifying external stiﬀness and damping matrices, which will be added to the WAMIT calculated
hydrostatic stiﬀness and hydrodynamic damping, respectively. The full mass matrix is deﬁned
in [7, eq.(3.3)], but for the system reduced to three DOF, rows and columns 2, 4 and 6 cancel

3.3
Tower ﬁnite element model
To illuminate any coupling between the tower bending mode and the pitch motion of the model
a Finite Element Method (FEM) is implemented to model the structural response of the FWT.
FEM modelling is particularly applicable for structures with a complex geometric shape and
heterogeneous mass and stiﬀness properties. The FWT structure can be modelled as an assem-
bly of cylindrical beam elements with diﬀerent cross sections, to which closed-form solutions
of the natural frequencies and eigenmodes do not exist, and hence FEM approximation is re-
quired.
In FEM modelling the ﬁrst step is the discretization of the primary structure into ﬁnite el-
ements, for which the local mass and stiﬀness matrices have been derived analytically. The
elements are connected to each other in nodes where the local displacement and rotation of the
elements must match, and this requirement is governing for the assembly of the global stiﬀ-
ness and mass matrices, K and M, from the local stiﬀness and mass matrices of the elements.
Natural frequencies and mode shapes are determined by solving the generalized eigenvalue
problem
ω2Mu = Ku
(3.77)
where ω is the natural frequency and u is the mode shape vector. For the present analysis, beam
elements are chosen, due to the interest in modelling transverse vibrations of the structure, and
structural damping is not accounted for in this FEM model.
A 2D model of the FWT is considered and thus each beam element holds four degrees of
freedom (DOF), where a linear and a rotational degree of freedom is assigned to each node.
The transverse deﬂection u(x, t) for a Euler-Bernoulli beam with constant values of Young’s
modulus E and second moment of inertia I is, governed by [39].
∂4u(x, t)
∂x4
= 0
(3.78)
which has the solution:
u(x, t) = c1(t)x3 + c2(t)x2 + c3(t)x3 + c4(t)x4
(3.79)
where ci(t), i = 1.., 4 are constants of integration. For the beam elements (3.79) must satisfy,
[39]:
u(0, t) = u1(t)
ux(0, t) = u2(t)
u(l, t) = u3(t)
ux(l, t) = u4(t)
(3.80)
where ux denotes diﬀerentiation with respect to x. The constants of integration are determined
by insertion of (3.80) into (3.79)
u(x, t) =

3.3.1
Discretization of the model
With the discretization of the FWT shown in ﬁgure 3.4 it is sought to obtain elements that are
comparable in size and suﬃciently long to justify the use of Euler-Bernoulli beam theory for the
individual beam elements. At the same time a suﬃcient subdivision of the model is necessary
to get a decent resolution of the modes. A division into 20 elements was considered appropriate.
The discretized model consists of the ﬂoater, the transition piece and the tower. The modeling
of the nacelle and rotor is treated in section 3.3.3.
The mass and the second moment of intertia for the tower elements, are interpolated from the
distributions of these quantities for the applied turbine, see section 4.2. The distributed mass of
the ﬂoater and the transition piece is 280 kg/m3 as introduced in section 4.3.2. The foundation
is assumed rigid and hence it was assigned with a stiﬀness in the order of 1000 times that of
the tower.
3.3.2
Addition of tendon stiﬀness
The stiﬀness of the mooring system was implemented by linearisation of the tendon restoring
forces as it was done for the WAMIT simulations, see section 3.2, and this external stiﬀness is
added in the point of ﬂotation. Accordingly, the transition piece must be divided into elements
that allows a node to be located at the point of ﬂotation. These external stiﬀness is added to
the elements of the global stiﬀness matrix that govern the motion of the node at the point of
ﬂotation (PoF).

3.3.3
Addition of nacelle and rotor mass
The additional mass of the RNA, mRNA can be accounted for in two ways. It can either be
distributed in the top element of the tower, or be added to the node at the tower top as lumped.
The ﬁrst method would account for a change in the inertia, but since the extension of the blades
is already left out of this analysis, it was decided to pursue with the latter strategy, since the
convergence of the solution would otherwise be highly dependent on the size of the top element.
Equation (3.86) exempliﬁes how the lumped mass is added.

(3.86)
The mass of the spokes is not included, but the contribution is assumed to be small compared
to the ﬂoater.
3.3.4
Addition of added mass
The added mass contribution is modeled in a very simplistic way. An added mass coeﬃcient of
Cm = 0.7, which is considered appropriate for a TLP foundation, is used to compute the added
mass for the ﬂoater and the part of the TP which is located beneath the still waver level. The
37
==PAGE:56==
CHAPTER 3. APPROXIMATE DESIGN MODELS
added mass is added to the local mass matrices of the ﬂoater and the mentioned section of the
TP. The added mass from the spokes is considered negligible in the FEM model.
3.3.5
FEM model of reduced tower
As described in section 4.3.1, the tower of the applied DTU 10MW WT is reduced in length to
maintain hub height while establishing a freeboard of 20 m for the TP. Hence, a FEM model
is prepared to investigate the eﬀect of the tower reduction on the bending natural frequency.
Instead of the ﬂoater and the transition piece a clamped boundary condition was implemented
at the bottom of the tower.
The clamped boundary condition is enforced by imposing no
rotation and translation of the node at the tower bottom. By removing the ﬁrst two columns
and rows of the global mass and stiﬀness matrix this condition is fulﬁlled. In this simpliﬁed
FEM model the tower is discretized into 12 elements, corresponding to the same number of
elements in the tower of the full FEM model.
38
==PAGE:57==
Chapter 4
Prototype design
This chapter outlines the design of the prototype TLP FWT, which later will be scaled and
constructed in model scale. First, two simple load cases are deﬁned which the TLP FWT must
be able to withstand, for the design to be considered appropriate. This is followed by a brief
description of the DTU 10MW WT, which is applied for the present study. Next, the ﬂoater
design is chosen based on numerical simulations using the 3DOF model from chapter 3 and
the load cases and two simple performance requirements. Finally the response of the chosen
prototype TLP FWT is modelled using the numerical tools presented in chapter 3.
4.1
Load cases
The standard [31] lists a minimum number of design load cases for which a conventional bot-
tom ﬁxed wind turbine should be designed. These include normal and fault design situations
at diﬀerent levels of severity of the wind speed, turbulence intensity and sea state and extreme
changes in wind direction, as well as transportation, installation and maintenance design situ-
ations. Some of the design load cases should be analysed for fatigue loads while others should
be analysed for ultimate loads.
It is likely that a corresponding set of design load cases should be considered in addition to
a set of load cases regarding the ﬂoating structure and the mooring system, when designing
FWTs at full scale. However, it is far beyond the scope of the present report and the capability
of the simplistic numerical code, to do a thorough analysis of a single of the load cases deﬁned
in [31], but they are taken as inspiration for two simpliﬁed load cases used in the present study.
These are both of the ultimate load type, and count the response to an extreme wave and an
extreme aerodynamic thrust. They are described in the following.
Load case 1 (LC1)
Load case 1(LC1) takes as starting point design load case 6.1c in [31],
which deﬁnes a combination of a 50-year steady wind, the maximum wave in a 50 year sea
state, extreme currents and extreme water elevation. Under these conditions the WT should
be parked. The present numerical model does not include the aerodynamic drag on the structure
above water since this eﬀect is assumed to be very small. Further, a description of currents and
water elevation due to tides was not a part of the environment adopted from [6], and was not
sought elsewhere. Therefore these eﬀects were also not modelled. Left is the eﬀect of the of the
maximum wave in a 50-year sea state, which has a signiﬁcant wave height of Hs50 = 14.33 m.
This value can be determined as the top point of the contour body in ﬁgure 2.5. The expected
largest wave, is generally given by
H50 = 1.86Hs50 = 26.65m
(4.1)

noted that at the maximum wave height, χ = 1, meaning that the velocity and acceleration
ﬁelds are correct at this point.
Load case 2 (LC2)
The second load case takes as a stating point design load case 1.3 in [31]
which is a combination of extreme turbulence and a normal sea state during power production.
As the current model does not account for turbulence eﬀects, the steady thrust will be based

Ueff = (1 + TI)U

4.2
Wind turbine deﬁnition
The WT of the proposed prototype design takes as a starting point the DTU 10-MW reference
wind turbine (DTU 10MW RWT), which is to be published in [9] and is described in [8]. These
should be consulted for a detailed description of the DTU 10MW RWT.
This reference turbine was deﬁned by researchers at DTU Wind Energy as an extension to the
Light Rotor project which was executed in coorporation with Vestas, and had as main objective
to optimize the stiﬀness and performance of blades to facilitate the construction of larger rotors.
It was decided in addition to the rotor to deﬁne a tower and drive train, to have a complete
description of a 10MW wind turbine which is publicly available and has suﬃcient detail to be
directly applied in simulation models [8]. The DTU 10MW RWT was designed as an oﬀshore
WT for class IEC class 1A, which implies a design 10 minute mean wind speed of 50 m/s at
hub height and a 10 min mean turbulence intensity of 16% at a wind speed of 15 m/s at hub
height [40]. Some primary parameters for the DTU 10MW RWT are given in table 4.1.
To accommodate the requirement of a light and stiﬀrotor, a high area moment of inertia of
the blades was desired. This was obtained by selecting airfoils with a high relative thickness
ranging from 24.1% at the tip up to 60% towards the blade root before obtaining a cylindrical
shape. This design was chosen despite the fact that the relative thickness traditionally goes to
zero at the blade tip [8].
Based on simulation of the rotor in the HAWCStab2 software, operational parameters were
determined for the DTU 10MW RWT, which are relevant for the present study, since it is
desired to operate the model turbine like the prototype. These data presented in ﬁgure 4.1,
comprising the rotor rotational speed, the blade pitch, rotor mechanical power and turbine
thrust along with the corresponding power and thrust coeﬃcients deﬁned as
CP =
8Pmech
ρairπD2
RU3
(4.10)
and
CT =
8fT
ρairπD2
RU2 .
(4.11)
These ﬁgures are presented for a range of wind speeds between the cut in wind speed and the cut
out wind speed. From the ﬁgure, it is observed that the WT is operated at constant rotational
speed for 4m/s ≤U ≤7m/s, at constant tip speed ratio, TSR, which implies a constant blade
pitch, in the range 8m/s < U ≤11m/s and at constant power for 12m/s < U ≤25m/s. The
maximum thrust is reached at 11m/s where fT = 1.50 MN and CT = 0.814.
The wind turbine tower, which has a total height of 115.63 m is deﬁned as conic shell-structure
with an outer diameter varying linearly from 8.3 m at the bottom to 5.5 m at the top, see
ﬁgure 4.2a. It comprises 10 sections with distinct wall thicknesses, varying from 3.8cm for the

area moment of the tower as a function the height above the tower base.
The tower material is deﬁned as steel S355, and an additional 8% is added to the material
density to account for the presence of secondary structures in the construction, [8].
A fundamental design criteria for ﬁxed and ﬂoating wind turbines is to avoid resonance in the
structure arising from the rotation of the rotor. Unbalances in the rotor, for instance due to a
mass unbalance or a discrepancy in the pitching angles of the blades, will result in an excitation
frequency which is equal to the rotational speed of the rotor. This frequency is known as the 1P
frequency and for the DTU 10MW RWT it lies within the range 1P=[0.1Hz 0.16Hz]. Another
dominating excitation frequency is the rotor blade passing frequency, which is 3 times the
rotational speed for a 3 bladed turbine, and is denoted 3P. For the DTU 10MW RWT the 3P
frequency is within the range 3P=[0.3Hz 0.48Hz]. Obviously the fundamental tower bending
mode of a wind turbine should not have a natural frequency that coincides with the 1P and 3P
frequencies. This is fulﬁlled for the DTU 10MW RWT which has a fundamental tower bending
frequency of 0.25 Hz.
