CHAPTER 4. PROTOTYPE DESIGN
4.3
Foundation design
In line with the challenges of reducing the cost of energy which the wind industry is facing
nowadays, it was decided to aim for a compact foundation design, potentially leading to a
reduction in material costs and thereby a reduction in cost of energy.
4.3.1
Design requirements and guidelines
To produce a realistic design for the ﬂoating TLP foundation, some requirements and guidelines
from TLP platforms in the oﬀshore industry are applied along with general requirements for
oﬀshore structures. However, when considering the design of a TLP for a FWT, it must be
kept in mind that the economical and environmental consequences of a platform wreck, are
much larger for an oil and gas platform than for a TLP supporting a FWT. Consequently,
the design of an oil and gas TLP is likely to be associated higher safety factors than it can
be expected from a commercial TLP design for a FWT. Thus, the design of the present is
on some parameters chosen to be less conservative than recommended in oil and gas platform
litterature. Only the geometry and mass properties of the structure will be considered in this
study, leaving out the large task of a detailed structural design for the foundation. Further,
the stability of the structure in the ﬂoat-out phase was not considered in the present study.
It is imagined that the construction of a special installation barge like the one suggested in
[4], which provides stability to the structure in this phase, could become relevant in large scale
projects. Changes in the MWL due to tides are disregarded in the present design, but must
naturally be considered in a commercial design, such that the foundation is stable and the
minimum submergence and freeboard are not exceeded at low and high tides.
Floater submergence and TP freeboard
The ﬂoater top must be prevented from piercing
the free surface which would result in large variations in the buoyancy force and thereby the
stability of the foundation. Likewise, no water is allowed on the platform at the connection
between the TP and the wind turbine tower. As the FWT is designed for a 50 year storm, this
means that the ﬂoater must be submerged below the trough and the TP must extend above
the crest of the maximum wave in the 50 year 3h storm. This wave is plotted in ﬁgure 4.11.
Taking into account a setdown in heave due to a displacement in surge/sway of approximately
1 m, the freeboard must be larger than 16 m, and the ﬂoater submergence must be larger than
11 m.
Maximum oﬀset
The maximum oﬀset in surge and sway due to both the static and dynamic
forcing must preferably not exceed 6-8% of the water depth, according to [41]. For small draft
compared to the water depth this corresponds to the tendons having an angle of ∼3.5−4.5 deg
with vertical. It is also stated, however, that mechanically the tendon connectors cannot exceed
10deg. Due to the aforementioned expectation of reduced safety factors for the design of TLPs
for FWTs, it was chosen to apply the mechanical limitation of the tendon connectors as the
maximum angle of the tendon with vertical. Hence, the present study applies a less conservative
criterion than it was suggested in [41].
Tendon tension limits
In a previous study carried out by the authors and described in [42]
and [15], events of slack tendons were observed. As stated in [15], this must be avoided in a
commercial design due to the undesirable resulting snap loads in the tendons. It was decided
to require all tendon tensions within the span
τi =]0τ0; 2τ0[,
i = 1, 2, .., nt
(4.12)
where τ0 is the tendon tension in the equilibrium state, and nt is the number of tendons.
45
==PAGE:64==
CHAPTER 4. PROTOTYPE DESIGN
Natural periods
As it is a general criterion for oﬀshore structures, the natural frequencies
of the combined foundation and turbine structure are required not to collide with the wave
frequency spectrum and the 1P an 3P frequencies of the rotor. This is required to avoid ﬁrst
order wave and rotor imbalance excitation of the structural eigenmodes. It is suggested in
[28, ch. 4.1.3] to require a surge natural period above 25s and roll/bending and pitch/bending
periods below 3.5s.
4.3.2
Structural design
A detailed structural design which eventually could give an estimation of the production costs
was not considered in this work. However, a few structural considerations that help deﬁne the
primary properties of the foundation were made.
To minimize the size of the TLP foundation, it was chosen not to ballast the structure as it has
been done in previous studies [43]. For oﬀshore TLP platforms ballast is used to even loads
between tendons [41, ch. 7.6.2], but this is not considered an option for the TLP foundation
supporting the FWT. The signiﬁcant overturning moment from the wind, which gives great
diﬀerence between mean tensions in the up- and downwind tendons, would require a substantial
amount of ballast to even out the tendon tensions. Hence, the mass of the foundation was
assumed to be proportional to the foundation volume, which is in line with [28], and eases the
estimation of the center of mass. While [28] applies diﬀerent eﬀective steel densities ranging
from 157-224 kg/m3 for the diﬀerent components of the foundation, it was in this work chosen
to use the same density of
ρs = 280kg/m3
(4.13)
throughout the structure. This remarkably higher density was chosen, since it was found im-
possible with the available materials in model scale to come close to the downscaled foundation
mass based on the densities suggested in [28]. Hence, following the recommendations of [28]
would in the present study reduce the model resemblance of the deﬁned prototype. Admit-
tedly, this is not the most stringent approach, and in future studies more eﬀort could be put
into ﬁnding light weight materials that could meet downscaled prototype requirements.
The tendons should be dimensioned such that they do not yield upon a certain design load. In
[28] it is suggested to let the tendon area, At be deﬁned by
2τ0
At
≤σy
SF ,
(4.14)
where σy is the yield stress of the tendon and SF is a safety factor. This equation says that
the tendon with a safety factor, SF, will not yield when the tendon tension is 2τ0. The present
design applies SF = 2, which according to [28], leads to a conservative design, and it uses the
sign of equality in (4.14) to deﬁne the tendon area.
At = 4τ0
σy
(4.15)
It was chosen to apply steel pipe tendons, as it is common in the oil and gas industry, and
which is also suggested for TLPs supporting FWT by [46]. In addition, this work states that
it is beneﬁcial for the tendons to be neutrally buoyant, which for steel pipes implies a wall
thickness to diameter ratio in the order of 3%. For the oil and gas industry these thin-walled
pipes made from high quality steel, have yield stresses up to σy = 552 MPa, according to [44,
p. 217], which was applied in the present analysis. To deﬁne the tendon stiﬀness, EAt, Youngs
modulus, E = 210 GPa was used.
46
==PAGE:65==
CHAPTER 4. PROTOTYPE DESIGN

would be a comprehensive task to cover this 9-dimensional design space, it was decided to ﬁx
some of the parameters. The TP freeboard was ﬁxed, such that it is not exceeded by the crest
of the 50 year design wave, and hence hTP becomes redundant if s is known. The depth, h,
was ﬁxed to 180 m in full scale, corresponding to the 3.00 m water depth of the basin in model
scale. The FWT mass, mFWT , comprises the mass of the WT and the foundation, where the
ﬁrst is deﬁned by the DTU10MW reference turbine, and the other is deﬁned by the structural
design of the foundation and assumed to be proportional to the foundation volume as stated
in section 4.3.2. The tendon stiﬀness is deﬁned from the initial tendon tension, τ0, by (4.15),
which depends on the volume of the foundation. Further it was chosen to apply
Df

4.3.4
Choice of foundation geometry
To propose a design for the TLP foundation, the response of a range of foundation geometries
deﬁned by combinations of the three non-dimensional design parameters ˜hf, ˜s and ˜L, was
simulated using the dynamic model derived in section 3.1.9. As it was considered out of the
available time frame for the design phase to run a WAMIT simulation for each geometry variant,
the linear damping terms in B were disregarded and the added mass, A was approximated by
a volumetric scaling of the added mass determined from a single WAMIT simulation. The
added mass corresponding at the wave frequency was then used in the dynamic model. The
response to the two the environmental conditions described in LC1 and LC2 was modelled.
Based on these simulations the maximum absolute displacement in surge is presented in ﬁgure
4.3 together with the buoyancy ratio, and the global minimum and maximum tendon tensions
are presented in ﬁgure 4.4. Both are presented as contour plots of the design parameters ˜s, ˜hf
and ˜L, ˜hf respectively.

lines of the buoyancy ratio are straight lines with an inclination corresponding to the ratio
between the cross sectional areas of the TP and the ﬂoater in the ˜s, ˜hf contour plots. This
ratio is D2

increase the stiﬀness in surge. For LC1, which only has wave forcing, ˜s is observed to be the
most eﬀective parameter to reduce surge displacement. An increase in ˜s will move the ﬂoater,
which represents the bulk of the foundation volume, down with a resulting decay in the velocity

The same contour plots for LC1 and LC2 were generated for the tendon tension and are pre-
sented in ﬁgure 4.4. For LC1 it is observed that the minimum tendon tension is generally more
critical than the maximum tendon tension, in the sense that it is closer to slack than the max-
imum is to the double initial tension. The submergence ˜s is clearly seen to be the parameter
with the weakest inﬂuence on the tendon tension for this load case, and ˜L is the parameter
48
==PAGE:67==
CHAPTER 4. PROTOTYPE DESIGN
with the strongest inﬂuence. A longer spoke means a longer arm for the righting moment and
hence the peak tendon tensions are closer to the initial value. For LC2, an increase in ˜s and ˜hf
means an increase in the moment arm for the overturning rotor thrust moment, at the same
time as they will increase the buoyancy and thereby the righting moment. This explains why an
optimum point is observed in left side of the ˜s, ˜hf-plot. As it was the case for LC1, an increase
in the spoke length ˜L also for LC2 brings the tendon tensions closer to the initial tension.
A foundation design approximately at this "optimum" point of ˜s, ˜hf in LC2 was chosen combined
with a spoke length of ˜L = 1.78 as marked by the blue star. The simulations of LC1 show that
the tendons are brought close to slack for this conﬁguration, though without becoming slack.
Hence the model suggests that the present design will go through the 50-year wave without
experiencing snap loads in the tendons.
The submergence for the chosen design is also right below the trough of the 50-year design
wave which is at z = −0.6Df as shown in ﬁgure 4.11, which means that the design requirement
of submergence of ﬂoater beneath the free water surface was fulﬁlled along with the guideline
of constructing a compact foundation. The dimensional parameters characterizing the chosen
foundation design are given in table 5.4 .

4.4
Prototype FWT properties and performance
4.4.1
Deﬁnition of structures 1-3
In a previous study of the authors, documented in [42] and [15], a great eﬀect on the response
of a 1/200 scale model of a 5MW wind turbine was observed, when introducing an inclination
of the tendons. Further, TLP foundations with both three and four spokes are reported in the
litterature, see e.g. the numerical study [28] and the experimental work [21] and [25]. Hence, it
was chosen to deﬁne three diﬀerent structures with diﬀerent mooring conﬁgurations, sketched
in ﬁgure 4.5, based on the chosen ﬂoater and transition piece design.
Structure 1 has four spokes, and four vertical tendons, and thus the mooring conﬁguration
resembles that of the experimental studies [15] (conﬁgurations A and B) and [25] and
that of the numerical studies of [45] and [28] (baseline designs 1, 4 and 5). The spokes
are oriented such that the wind direction is right between the two upwind tendons. Since
this mooring conﬁguration is simple and often observed in the literature, this structure
50
==PAGE:69==
CHAPTER 4. PROTOTYPE DESIGN
δ
l0
Structure 1
Structure 2
Structure 3
Figure 4.5: Sketch of the three applied structures. Left: structure 1, center: structure 2, right:
structure 3
is considered the base case of the present experimental study.
Structure 2 has four spokes and four tendons that are inclined towards the tower with an
angle of 9 deg, which means that their intersection is located 43 m above the hub level.
This inclination was chosen based on some practical limitations in model scale, and no
further analysis of an optimum angle has been considered. The tendon inclination entails
a rotation of the WT tower against the ﬂoater displacement, which for the deﬁned DOF
means that negative pitch is accompanied by positive surge and vice versa, while positive
sway is accompanied by positive roll.
This will be observed as a 180deg phase shift
between the surge and pitch motion, while sway and roll will be in phase. Having inclined
tendons, this structure resembles tendon conﬁguration C in [15], which was observed to
have a strong coupling of pitch and surge and to have a beneﬁcial dynamic behaviour in
harsh sea states. Based on these ﬁndings, it was chosen to test the inclination of tendons
in this more sophisticated experimental setup. The spokes of this structure are oriented
as for structure 1, such that the wind direction is right between the two upwind tendons.
51
==PAGE:70==
CHAPTER 4. PROTOTYPE DESIGN
Structure 3 has three spokes, where one is parallel to the wind direction, and this structure
is moored by three vertical tendons. Hence, this mooring conﬁguration resembles that of
baseline designs 2 and 3 in [28]. This structure was chosen, since it is recommended in
[46] to have three spokes for easier installation.
4.4.2
WAMIT simulations
To determine the primary hydrodynamic properties of the three chosen structures, they were
analysed using the WAMIT software, described in section 3.2. Since the spokes are not modelled
in WAMIT, the foundation geometry in these simulations of the three structures are equal, and
thus the simulations of the three structures only diﬀer in the external mass and external stiﬀness.
Consequently, the added mass, the damping and the wave excitation forces are equal for the
three structures, since these quantities are speciﬁc to the foundation geometry. The diagonal el-
ements of the added mass and damping matrices are presented in ﬁgure 4.6 in their dimensional
form, while the wave excitation forces Xi, normalized by the wave amplitude a,
˜Xi = Xi
a
(4.18)
are presented in ﬁgure 4.7.
It is observed that the damping and the wave excitation forces peak at 0.10-0.15 Hz, which is
followed by a rapid decay such that the damping and excitation force in the surge direction
have almost vanished at 1 Hz while this happens at approximately 0.4 Hz in the heave and
pitch degrees of freedom. The peaks are centred approximately at Df
λ = 0.2, which according
to the linear dispersion relation corresponds to a frequency of
f =
√kg tanh kh
2π
= 0.13Hz.
(4.19)
Hence, this is the point where diﬀraction eﬀects according to [33, Ch. 6] become signiﬁcant. As
the ratio of Df
λ increases, corresponding to increasing wave frequency, the pressure variations
over the geometry even out each other, and consequently the wave excitation force vanishes.
It is observed, that the added mass has a peak and a dip around this frequency as well, and
thereafter takes a constant value. In surge, the damping and the wave excitation force show
a range of "dips" for f > 0.4Hz. This has in a previous WAMIT analysis been experienced to
vanish when the number of panels that discretize the geometry is increased.
Based on these data, the undamped natural frequencies of the rigid body modes can be deter-
mined by solving the well-known eigenvalue problem
(M + A) ω2 = C
(4.20)
where M and A are the mass and added mass matrices, and C is the stiﬀness matrix, holding
both the hydrostatic and external stiﬀness contributions. The resulting natural frequencies are
given in table 4.2 for the three prototype structures. Structures 1 and 3, have equal surge and
heave natural frequencies, while a minor discrepancy is observed for the pitch natural frequency.
Structure 2, however, has a slightly higher surge natural frequency and slightly lower heave and
pitch natural frequencies. Hence, changing the number of tendons from four to three does only
increases the pitch stiﬀness slightly, while an inclination of the tendons, increases the surge
stiﬀness, while the heave and pitch stiﬀnesses are decreased.
It was discussed whether the mean displacement due to an aerodynamic thrust force would
aﬀect the systems stiﬀness signiﬁcantly. To investigate this, the structure’s displacement was
determined for a range of thrust forces, and the natural frequencies were computed based on
linearized stiﬀness terms at these equilibria. For the three structures, the deviations of the
natural frequencies, fi, from the corresponding natural frequencies, fi0, in the initial position

are presented in ﬁgure 4.8 for a range of thrust forces. It is shown that for structure 1 and 3 the
surge frequency for 0 < fT < 1 MN experiences a small decrease and increases for fT > 1 MN.
The pitch frequency decreases with increasing fT . It is noted however, that below rated thrust,
the change in the natural frequencies is within 0.1% and hence, this is not considered to have
any signiﬁcant eﬀect on the ﬂoating WT response.
The magnitude of the RAOs computed in WAMIT are presented in ﬁgure 4.9, where the left
column holds the RAOs for structure 2, and the RAOs for structures 1 and 3 are shown together
in the plots in the right column, since they are very similar.
The surge RAOs for the three structures are very similar and all show clear peaks at their
respective natural frequencies. It must be kept in mind when studying the values of the RAOs
that these are the results of the linearized problem, which is fair approximation when the
displacements are small.
The heave RAO’s are as well very similar for the three structures. Clear peaks are observed at
the heave natural frequencies at ∼1.3 Hz and at the frequency where the wave excitation force
has its maximum.
From the pitch RAOs it is observed that waves at the surge natural frequency are the most
eﬀective to excite pitch motion of the structures. This is especially pronounced for structure 2,
which is explained by the pitch motion being induced as a result of the inclined tendons.

In the ﬁrst mode in ﬁgure 4.10a the FWT is translating back and forth with no deﬂection of the
tower. This mode can recognized as a pure surge motion, and the associated natural frequency
of 0.027 is consistent with the surge natural frequency deduced in section 4.4.2.
In the second mode the tower itself deﬂects in its ﬁrst bending mode while the entire FWT
pitches and translates in surge. In this mode the nacelle and ﬂoater are out of phase, and the
corresponding natural frequency of this mode is 0.30 Hz, which is just at the lower limit of the
3P range. Besides, the natural frequency of the second mode is adequate far away from any
wave frequencies.
The third mode is also a coupled surge, pitch and ﬁrst tower bending mode, but in the third
mode the ﬂoater and the nacelle are in phase, causing an increase in the natural frequency to
1.8. This frequency is completely out of the 3P range
In closing the FEM model shows that the frequencies that arises from the coupled tower-
pitch motion of the FWT seems to be around 9 % lower than the ﬁrst tower bending natural
frequency.

4.4.4
Response to LC1
The response of the three prototype structures to the estimated maximum wave in the 50-year
sea state (LC1) was modeled by means of the dynamic model described in section 3.1.9. The
added mass and damping were imported from the WAMIT results, at a frequency corresponding
to the wave frequency which for the present load case is
1
13.4s = 0.075 Hz. From ﬁgure 4.6 it
is observed that this choice of damping is too high, for the free oscillations at the structure’s
natural frequencies.
Figure 4.11 shows the surface elevation for LC 1, which is a ramp up of a series of stream
function waves. The wave group is placed in the beginning of the time series to make room for
the free oscillations in the second half of the simulation.
The surge, heave and pitch response along with the tendon for structure 1 is presented in ﬁgure
4.12.
The surge motion is seen to be in phase with the surface elevation, and the largest displacement
from the equilibrium position is observed in the upstream direction at the passing of the deepest
wave trough.
As a consequence of the stiﬀmooring system, which gives a strong coupling between surge and
heave, the structure also experiences the largest set-down in heave at this event.
The pitch motion time history, strongly resembles that of the surge motion, meaning that they
are in phase and that the peak displacements have the same relative magnitude. The high
frequency oscillations for t > 100 s are likely due to a damping eﬀect which is not accounted
for, and are therefore not considered physical.
These oscillations are also observed in the tendon tensions, and should neither be considered
physical here. The most critical tendon tensions are encountered at the coinciding maximum
surge and pitch displacements from the equilibrium in the upstream direction. Here the upwind
tendons encounter a tension of ∼1.7τ0 while the downwind tendon tensions are at ∼0.1τ0.

Figure 4.11: Surface elevation for 50-year design wave with a wave heigt of 26.65 m.
For structure 2, the response to the same event is presented in ﬁgure 4.13. The surge motion
for structure 2 is observed to be very similar to that of structure 1. The set-down in heave,
however, is slightly larger than for structure 1, which can be explained by a signiﬁcant increase
in pitch motion. Further, it is observed that the pitch motion is approximately 180 deg out of
phase with the surge motion, which means that the structure pitches in the negative direction
when the surge is positive, as it is described in [15] for the equivalent tendon conﬁguration C.
The tendon tensions for this structure reach peak values of ∼0.5τ0 and ∼1.5τ0 both obtained
in the upwind tendons. However, these peak tensions are far less critical than the ones obtained
for structure 1, which is a clear advantage of this structure.
From ﬁgure 4.14 it is seen that the response in surge, heave and pitch of structure 3 to this event,
is very similar to that of structure 1. However, the tendon tension in the upwind tendon reaches

The response of the three structures to this load case is presented in ﬁgures 4.16 to 4.18. The
observations from LC1 regarding the phase between surface elevation, and the response in surge
heave and pitch, also apply in these simulations for all three structures.
However, the mean rotor thrust, which is now present results in a mean displacement in surge
pitch and heave.
Structures 1 and 3 are displaced ∼0.52Df downstream in mean, with a
resulting mean set-down of ∼0.017Df and a positive mean pitch of ∼1.1deg. Structure 2 is
only displaced ∼0.12Df in surge, and experiences a mean set-down in heave of ∼0.0015Df
and a negative pitch of ∼0.6deg. The mean displacements for structure 2 are hence remarkably
smaller than for structures 1 and 3.
Further, an increased mean tension in the upwind tendons and a decreased mean tension in
the downwind tendons is observed as a result of the aerodynamic thrust. An evidently larger
mean teansion in the upwind tendon is observed for structure 3 with three tendons.

Chapter 5
Downscaling and construction
5.1
Dynamic-elastic scaling
When setting up a model experiment, the aim is to have dynamic and elastic similarity between
the model and its prototype. The motion of a rigid body in a ﬂuid, is generally governed by
inertial forces, gravitational forces and viscous forces, and to obtain full dynamic similarity, the
ratio of these forces must be conserved. This can be expressed as a preservation of the Froude
number deﬁned as the ratio of inertial forces to gravitational forces
Fr =
Inertial force
Gravitational force =
u
√gl
(5.1)
and the Reynolds number which is deﬁned as the ratio of the inertial forces to the viscous
forces:
Re = Inertial force
Viscous force = ul
ν ,
(5.2)
where u is a characteristic velocity, l is a characteristic length, g is the acceleration of gravity
and ν is the kinematic viscosity. Generally it is not possible to preserve both in model tests
which represent a signiﬁcant change in the length scale, which is clearly observed by considering
the ratio of the Reynolds number to the Froude number,
Re
Fr =
√g
√
l3
ν
.
(5.3)
As a consequence of the constant Re and Fr, this quantity must also be preserved, requiring
either a substantial change in the acceleration of gravity or the ﬂuid viscosity, but neither are
considered possible for model tests [37, p. 5]. Surface waves are primarily governed by Fr, and
hence it has become tradition to preserve this number in model tests of oﬀshore structures and
ﬂoating bodies, and this methodology will also serve as a starting point for the scaling of the
wind turbine model in the present work.
Further, for a ﬂexible structure, it is desired to scale it elastically, such that the natural fre-
quencies of the model scale like the frequencies of the environment.
This section follows the authors’ own work published in [15] and is consistent with Bredmose
et al. [47] and Martin et al. [23], although the latter does not take into account the diﬀerence
in prototype and model scale water density.
The geometric scaling factor, λ, is deﬁned by

The rotor aerodynamics are mainly governed by Re, and due to the large dissimilitude in Re,
a geometrical scaling of the rotor will not give the correct aerodynamic performance of the
blades. This will lead to a reduction in the aerodynamic thrust and mechanical power, where
especially the ﬁrst is of great importance to the overall dynamics of the structure. Hence, there
is a need for a redesign of the rotor in model scale, which is able to deliver the right thrust at
the low Re. This was also carried out as a part of the present work and is detailed in section
5.3. To maintain mass and gyroscopic eﬀects, this must be combined with a distribution of
mass as determined from the prototype distribution by (5.5), and ideally the stiﬀness must be
scaled to give the right natural frequencies determined by (5.10).

5.2
Wind generator design
The wave basin at DHI is not equipped with a wind generation system capable of covering
the rotor area of the ﬂoating turbine, and hence it was necessary to design and build a wind
generator from scratch. Given that the wind turbine is tested in a wave basin it is necessary to
construct an open wind generator, yet an open jet allows the outer streamlines to bend more
freely around the rotor plane, which actually simulates a real wind ﬁeld better.
DHI were in possession of six AVL-710 fans, which formed the basis for the design of the wind
generation system. It was decided to have an outlet of 4m x 4m, to cover the rotor area since
the wind turbine is located some distance from the wind generator, and the area of the uniform
wind proﬁle decreases as the wind turbine gets further away from the outlet, causing a higher
degree of turbulence at the boundaries, potentially impacting the wind turbine rotor.
Instead of designing one big wind generator, the generator system was designed as six identical
units with outlets of 2.00 m x 1.33 m. Several beneﬁts arose from this strategy. First of all it
gave the opportunity of testing a single wind generator once it was built, making it possible to
measure and decide upon whether the sought wind ﬁeld was obtainable before the construction
of the remaining units. Moreover the crane at DHI could not lift more than 1000 kg, and
with a weight of 100 kg per fan this would put an impracticable limit on the weight of the
remaining construction. A sketch of the single wind generator is shown in ﬁgure 5.1, whereas
ﬁgure 5.2 shows the ﬁnal setup for the wind generation system, where the six individual units
are combined to form a uniform wind proﬁle of 4 x 4 meter.
The limited space available for the wind generator at the wave basin set out the need for a
compact solution which was achieved by introducing a corner section where the ﬂow is turned
90◦. Additionally the corner section was utilized for expanding the ﬂow, which otherwise would
require a diﬀuser section. A compact diﬀuser given the space available would most likely lead to
ﬂow separation and backﬂow due to the relatively sudden expansion. Such phenomena causes
energy loses and induces turbulence to the ﬂow.
Guide vanes were mounted diagonally in the corner section to turn the ﬂow 90◦without sig-
niﬁcant energy loses, and an additional beneﬁt of the guide vanes is a reduction in large scale
turbulence.
In order to homogenize the ﬂow prior to the corner section a settling chamber with dimensions
of 1.33 m x 1.00 m was installed. The settling chamber was ﬁtted with two screens (see section
5.2) and downstream of the corner section with an expansion ratio of 1:2 two additional screens
71
==PAGE:90==
CHAPTER 5. DOWNSCALING AND CONSTRUCTION
Figure 5.1: Sketch of a single wind generator
Figure 5.2: Sketch of the complete wind generation system
72
==PAGE:91==
CHAPTER 5. DOWNSCALING AND CONSTRUCTION
Figure 5.3: Exploded view of a single wind generator
were also installed. The axial fan induces a strong rotational velocity component to the ﬂow,
and the purpose of the screens is to ﬁlter out this turbulence and to smear out the ﬂow. The
ﬂow must have a certain length to develop across once it has passed the screens, due to the
small scaled turbulence introduced by the wires in the screen. Therefore the outlet is situated
some distance away from the last screen. It was decided not to mount a contraction at the
outlet of the wind generator, since this would require an outlet of the corner section of at least
5 x 5 meter, which would make the construction too large for the basin building. However the
lack of a contraction section which in particular reduces turbulence at the boundaries will imply
the need for careful considerations regarding the remaining sections of the wind generator.
An exploded view of the wind generator is presented in ﬁgure 5.3.
Screens
A frequently employed setup for decreasing turbulence and even out the ﬂow is the implemen-
tation of screens and/or honeycombs. According to [49] screens are much more useful than
honeycombs for smoothing the ﬂow, but it must be noted that whereas screens are excellent
at decreasing the longitudinal component of turbulence and to homogenize the mean velocity,
honeycombs are much more eﬃcient at removing lateral and vertical components of turbulence
[50].
Screens should be mounted at the widest sections of the wind tunnel where the dynamic pres-
sure is at its minimum, however due to the compact size of the wind generator this requirement
could not be fulﬁlled entirely. According to [49] the energy loses caused by screens is quite
small and is far outweighted by the improvement in velocity distribution.
Four diﬀerent types of screens are installed, and they are all listed in table 5.2. The pressure
drop over the screens was estimated using the correlations given in [51, p. 522], based on the

signiﬁcant eﬀect on the volume ﬂow delivered by the fan.
Guide Vanes
An abrupt corner as the one planned to be constructed calls for guide vanes to reduce energy
losses. According to [49] a corner section without any guide vanes may have a loss of 100%
of the velocity head, whereas properly designed vanes may reduce this loss to only 15% of the
velocity head.

The scale of the turbulence was the governing parameter for the size (chord) of the guide vanes
which was ﬁxed at c = 0.180m. The relation between the chord of the guide vanes and the
length of the diagonal S = 2.24m can be used to estimate the necessary number of vanes. For

are just indicative.
In [49], a leading edge angle of 4◦to 5◦and trailing edge angle of zero is suggested for minimum
pressure loss. However these guidelines are valid for a Reynolds number of 500000, which is
way above the Reynolds number of 20000 expected for this wind generator.
Furthermore it is suggested to install horizontal vanes to form a honeycomb, but this has been
discarded due to increased complexity of the setup.
5.2.1
Fans
Six AVL-710 axial fans were employed for wind generation, and in ﬁgure 5.5 a performance
diagram for a single fan is shown.
Figure 5.5: Performance diagram for the AVL-710 axial fan
The area enclosed by the black curves is the operating range of the AVL-type fans. The exact
performance curve for the AVL-710 fan is diﬃcult to decide on, but from ﬁgure 5.5 it is evident
that regardless of the position of the performance curve for the AVL-710, it will be able to deliver
at least about 5.7 m3/s at a reasonable static pressure. The static pressure is of less interest,
75
==PAGE:94==
CHAPTER 5. DOWNSCALING AND CONSTRUCTION
since the total pressure drop across the screens, which is considered the largest contributor to
pressure loss, is about 18 Pa as stated in table 5.2.
The rated speed of the fans is 1420 rpm at 1.5 kW, but the fans are equipped with variable
speed drives, which makes it possible to adjust the ﬂow rate and hence the wind speed.
In the fan casing, ﬂow straighteners are located downstream of the fan, which removes some of
the swirl generated by the rotating motion of the fan. Furthermore the fans all have a smooth
inlet section which improves the incoming ﬂow and reduces noise.
5.2.2
Construction of Wind Generator
To get all dimensions right, the wind generator section was drawn in Solidworks and the outcome
of this work is shown in ﬁgure 5.1. The frame of the wind generator units was constructed in
30x30x3mm steel angle bars and the walls were made from 9 mm plywood which was chosen
due to its relatively low density, high stiﬀness and easiness of processing. The thickness of the
plywood sheet holding the fan was increased to 18 mm to ensure adequate rigidity. The fan
was attached to the 18 mm plywood sheet using 16 M10 bolts.
RHS steel beams were used for the screen frames, and the screens made from stainless steel
were stretched around the screen frames and clamped to the frame using pop rivets.
The guide vanes were cut from a 1 mm aluminum sheet with a width of 200 mm. Ideally the
vanes should have the form of a quarter of a circle, but due to the limiting size of the rolling mills
available, it was decided to bend the aluminum plates to approach a circular shape. Several
lifting yokes were welded to the frame, such that the wind generators could be lifted during
assembly of the complete wind generator system. Furthermore supporting RHS beams of 1/2
m were welded to the top and bottom of the corner section to facilitate the junction of two
wind generator units such that they could be lifted together. This was necessary at DHI, since
the crane could only move along one axis.
5.2.3
Preliminary measurement of wind ﬁeld
A measurement of the wind ﬁeld was conducted in order to investigate the quality of the ﬂow,
in particular the uniformity and turbulence intensity in the ﬂow. Four multi-channel omnidirec-
tional velocity sensors with a sample frequency of 5 Hz were used to measure the mean velocity
and the turbulence intensity of the wind ﬁeld. The velocity sensor is formed as a spherical
sensor of 3 mm in diameter made of enameled copper and connected to the rest of the sensor
by a tiny metal rod. The copper sphere is electrically heated and when it is located in a ﬂow, it
will be cooled, causing a detectable change in voltage which can be converted to a ﬂow speed.
The reason for using a hot-sphere anemometer instead of a hot-wire anemometer, which has
a much higher sampling frequency in the range of hundreds of Hz, is that this device is more
accurate at low wind speeds.
The four sensors were equally spaced in a vertical rack covering half of the height of the wind
tunnel outlet. The rack was mounted on a small trolley which had the ability of traversing
in the lateral direction of the wind tunnel. The trolley speed was chosen as the largest speed
at which the sensors only measured noise when traversing in a no-wind condition. When the
trolley had traversed the outlet once, the four sensors were moved to a new position on the
rack, such that the remaining part of the outlet could be covered.
Hence the measurements consist of 8 horizontal lines with numerous measurement points. The
sensors were located a distance of 1 m downstream of the outlet. Measurements further down-
stream would have been desirable, since the wind turbine will be located more than 1 meter away
from the outlet, but this was not possible due to limited space. The setup is shown in ﬁgure 5.6.
The eﬀect of the following four conﬁgurations were tested:

(b)

• A: No screens

The reason for not having a conﬁguration of two ﬁne screens was due to the rather high costs
for this type of screen.
5.2.4
Results
A central moving average was imposed on the data with a sample width of 5 s to determine
mean velocity and turbulence intensity. Unfortunately it turned out that one of the probes were
not measuring correctly. During the experiments it was not clear whether it was an error in
the sensor, the software or a bad calibration. Due to limited lending time of the equipment the
source of error was not found. Hence the results will clearly show a probe which is measuring
velocities that deviate substantially from the remaining probes. However it is assumed that
the relative diﬀerences in velocity for this particular probe for diﬀerent conﬁgurations is usable
for determination of the most favourable screen conﬁguration. In continuation hereof it must
be stressed that these initial measurements of the wind ﬁeld primarily serves as the basis for a
qualitative analysis for the choice of screens.
Contour plots mapping the mean velocity for the four diﬀerent screen conﬁgurations are shown
in ﬁgure 5.7. The cross section and contour plots of the turbulence intensity are shown in ﬁgure
5.8. The left side of the individual plots faces the fan.
Figure 5.7 clearly show that the implementation of screens has a signiﬁcant inﬂuence on the
velocity distribution. The ﬂuctuation in the ﬂow has decreased and a more uniform ﬂow is

5.3
Rotor design
Froude number similitude entails a reduction in velocity in model scale as the model dimensions
are smaller than the dimensions of the prototype. Hence Reynolds number similitude cannot
possibly be achieved, and in fact the Reynolds number will be signiﬁcantly lower than the
full scale Reynolds number. Consequently the ﬂow regime around the turbine blade will be
poorly replicated in model scale if the blades are geometrically scaled. Since the lift and drag
coeﬃcient (CL, CD) for an airfoil are highly dependent on the ﬂow conditions around the airfoil,
[52], the wind turbine performance of the model is expected to deviate from the full scale and
a geometrically scaled rotor. The lift and drag coeﬃcients are essential components of the lift
and drag force as stated below
FL = 1
2cCLρaV 2
FD = 1
2cCDρaV 2
(5.25)
where c is the blade chord length, ρ is the density of the air and V is the relative velocity seen
by the airfoil section.
The thrust force FT and the force component contributing to the rotor torque FQ are dependent
on the lift and drag force in the following way
FT = FL cos φ + FD sin φ
FQ = FL sin φ −FD cos φ
(5.26)
where φ is the angle between the rotor plane and the incoming wind passing the blade, as
sketched in ﬁgure 5.11. Since φ usually is relatively small in magnitude for a high TSR-rotor
as the one considered here, the lift force is the main contributor to the thrust force. At this
φ
α
θ
V
FQ
FD
FT
FL
φ
U
Figure 5.11: Sketch of a 2D airfoil
stage it is evident that a signiﬁcant change in the lift and drag coeﬃcient of a geometrically
scaled blade will violate the scaling of thrust and torque (power), since CL and CD scale with
the ratio of the prototype to model water densities, which is close to 1. This is demonstrated
for CT in section 5.1.
One way to come around this is to implement an alternative airfoil proﬁle which is more
suitable for low Reynolds number ﬂows, combined with an increase of the chord, such that the
dominating scaling parameter for the blade is CLc instead of c. By scaling CLc correctly the lift
force will be scaled correctly and consequently the correct thrust force FT and CT will follow,
which is deﬁned as
CT =
FT
1
2ρAU2
(5.27)
where A is the rotor area and U is the free stream velocity. The drawback of shifting to a low
Reynolds number airfoil is an increase in drag which preclude the ability to scale the torque
and hence the power and achieve a satisfying CP , which is deﬁned as:
CP =
P
1
2ρAU3
(5.28)
82
==PAGE:101==
CHAPTER 5. DOWNSCALING AND CONSTRUCTION
According to [23] the thrust force is typically an order of magnitude greater than the overturning
moment due to the rotor torque, and hence the thrust force will have a much greater impact
on the global dynamics of the model. Therefore it was decided to aim for a thrust-matched
rotor design. Moreover, eﬀort was put into matching the tip speed ratio correctly and scaling
the mass of the blades.
The work of designing the thrust-matched blades was conducted by Robert Mikkelsen based on
aero-servo-elastic computations in FLEX 5, and in [53] the overall design criteria for the thrust
matched blades are listed, which are
• Match axial blade loading Fz and thrust coeﬃcient CT
• Single point design, V = 10 m/s for full scale
• Preserve design TSR
• Power as good as possible
• Preserve chord distribution scaled with the same factor for all radii
Maintaining the TSR in the Froude scaled wind ﬁeld ensures that any excitation frequencies
arising from rotor imbalances (1P) or aerodynamic interaction with the tower (3P) are scaled
correctly.
Usually 1P is not present in numeric codes but will be present in experiments due to uneven
mass distribution, discrepancies in pitch angle etc.
Furthermore a retained TSR and a scaled distribution of mass in the blades is a necessity for
modeling the gyroscopic eﬀects correctly.
As stated in [53], the need for an alternative low Reynolds number airfoil to form the basis for
the thrust matched turbine lead to a manual search for proﬁles with the aerodynamic properties:
• Linear behavior in attached ﬂow across the Reynolds number range
• Continuous trends for drag
• High lift-drag ratio
• “Soft” stall behavior
• Limited or no hysteresis
The SD70xx series, [54],[55],[56] was chosen for further analysis due to its favorable properties
at low Reynolds numbers. The airfoils in this series are much slimmer compared to the high
Reynolds number airfoils of the 10 MW DTU reference wind turbine.
Airfoil data down to Re = 60000 was available, but since initial computation suggested that
the Reynolds number would be as low as Re = 25000 in model scale, it was decided to conduct
measurement of a SD7003 airfoil at DTU. In collaboration with Robert Mikkelsen a 2D blade
of the airfoil was drawn, measuring 50mm in the chord and 500 mm in the span, and this test
blade is shown in ﬁgure 5.12.
The 2D wing was CNC milled from a block of aluminum, and grounded to obtain a smooth
surface.
The 2D blade was tested in the red open loop low speed wind tunnel at DTU. The test section
of the wind tunnel is 500mm x 500mm in cross-section and 1300mm long, and the turbulence
intensity is below 0.1%.
The 2D blade was tested at seven diﬀerent Reynolds numbers: 15000, 20000, 30000, .., 70000,
which corresponds to wind tunnel speeds in the range from 4.5m/s - 21m/s. The 2D blade
was mounted in a 1 DOF force transducer enabling the measurement of lift, while a rack of
pressure taps downstream of the airfoil measured the wake and hence the pressure loss across
83
==PAGE:102==
CHAPTER 5. DOWNSCALING AND CONSTRUCTION
the airfoil, which can be converted to a corresponding drag. Figure 5.12 shows the test setup.
Measurements were carried out at the minimum turbulence intensity of around 0.1 % and with
added turbulence generated from three thin wires mounted upstream of the testsection. With
the wires the level of turbulence intensity can be increased up to 1 %.
The turbulence intensity in the wind ﬁeld is not identical to the turbulence intensity experienced
by the blades, since the blades have a speed themselves, and consequently the turbulence
intensity decreases as the rotational speed increases.
Figure 5.12: Test setup in low speed wind tunnel for lift and drag measurement of a 2D SD7003
airfoil.
At every Reynolds number the lift and drag is measured in the range of angle of attack (α or
AOA) from −5◦to 18◦. According to [52] the lift and drag for wind turbine is only functions
of the AOA and the Reynolds number.
The test procedure is automatized such that the AOA is initially set to 0◦. Then the AOA is
increased stepwise of 1◦to 18◦followed by a stepwise decrease to −5◦, and ﬁnally the AOA
stepwise returns to 0◦. The drag and lift was measured for 10 seconds at every integer angle,
which is equivalent to letting the air molecules pass the chord in the order of a 1000 chord
lengths.
The reason for monitoring the lift and drag as the AOA is increased and decreased is to enable
the detection of hysteresis.
In ﬁgure 5.13a the lift and drag coeﬃcients are plotted as a function of the AOA. All ﬁgures in
this section have been reemployed in agreement with Robert Mikkelsen.
The results for Re = 15000 and Re = 20000 are omitted since these results turned out to be of
insigniﬁcant relevance.

Figure 5.13: Measured airfoil characteristics of the SD7003 airfoil for Reynolds numbers of 30k,
40k, 50k and 60k. Selig data applied for 100k and 200k.
From ﬁgure 5.13a it is evident that in the attached ﬂow region the relationship between the lift
coeﬃcient and the AOA is linear up till around CL = 0.9 for the tests conducted at DTU. As
the AOA is increased further stall begins, which leads to a decrease in the lift coeﬃcient and a
rapid increase in the drag coeﬃcient.
The SD7003 airfoil stalls quite abruptly, which is common to thin airfoils with a high curvature
around the leading edge, [52]. The diﬀerence in stall behavior for the Selig blade can partly be
attributed to the increase in Reynolds number. However the change in stall behavior between
the DTU blade at Re = 60000 and the Selig blade at Re = 100000 is so diﬀerent that an
increased turbulence level in the wind ﬂow in the Selig experiments or an increased surface
roughness for the Selig blade might be a contributory explanation for the more mild stall be-
havior for the Selig airfoil. With a higher degree of turbulence in the passing ﬂow, separation
at the upper side of the airfoil can be postponed and can set in gradually, since a turbulent
boundary layer is more energetic and thinner compared to a laminar boundary layer with the
result that the boundary layer sticks better to the airfoil, [57]. The drawback of stimulating a
85
==PAGE:104==
CHAPTER 5. DOWNSCALING AND CONSTRUCTION
turbulent boundary layer is an increase in the skin friction and thus drag.
The Selig airfoil measures 305mm in the chord, and this diﬀerence in model shape could also im-
pact the measurements. Moreover the chord/tunnel aspect ratio is 1:4 in the Selig experiments
whereas the ratio i 1:10 in the red wind tunnel at DTU.
Additionally there are no clear signs of nonlinear eﬀects such as hysteresis. A slight discrepancy
is observed for the CL curve at Re = 30000 in the range from α = [0 2], but it is considered of
no importance.
The relationship between the lift coeﬃcient and the drag coeﬃcient is outlined in ﬁgure 5.13b.
From this ﬁgure it is evident that the drag possesses a continuous trend which was a desired
aerodynamic property stated previously.
Figure 5.13c which represents the CL/CD ratio as a function of the lift coeﬃcient CL, is essential
in the choice of the design lift, since the lift coeﬃcient correlated to the peak of the CL/CD
curve is considered the optimum value for CL.
The maximum lift/drag ratio increases slightly with CL as the Reynolds number is increased,
but a design lift of approximately CL = 0.6−0.7 satisfy the condition of maximum lift/drag ratio
for a large range of Reynolds numbers expected to be present in model scale, while maintaining
a suﬃcient safety margin to stall.
The next task is to decide upon the length of the chord which must match with the design CL
in order to scale c · CL correctly. Additionally the chord is a decisive parameter with respect
to the Reynolds number and in order to ascertain a suﬃciently high margin to stall, the chord
must be increased around 60-80% to match the design lift.
However, increasing the chord implies the need for more material and hence an increased weight
of the blade. Furthermore, an increase in chord would make the blade more sensitive to changes
in the pitch of the blade, impacting the global dynamic response of the model.
Even though the slope δCL
δα is alike for the full scale blade and model blade the relative larger
chord of the model blade will result in greater changes in the load and increase in aerodynamic
damping for the model blade for an equivalent change in the AOA. This sensitivity towards
pitch is naturally an issue impacting the control characteristics of the model, but since the main
target is to match the static thrust force, this eﬀect is condoned.
As stated earlier, the new blade design does not allow a match of the torque, due to the larger
drag of the slimmer SD7003 airfoil compared to the prototype blade, and hence the maximum
CL/CD ratio of the model blade will consistently be lower. According to [15], typical values of
the CL/CD ratio is in the order 100-150 for full-scale wind turbine blades, while ﬁgure 5.13c
shows that only a maximum CL/CD ratio of 20-40 is obtainable.
A series of trail computation was conducted by Robert Mikkelsen to investigate the impact on
the Reynolds number and the lift coeﬃcient for diﬀerent chord elongations. A mutual agreement
of moving forward with a chord increase of 75 % was made. In ﬁgure 5.14a the scaled chord
distribution of the model blade is shown, and in ﬁgure 5.14b the distribution of the Reynolds
number across the blade span is shown. Finally the distribution of the lift and drag coeﬃcient
is shown in ﬁgure 5.14c.

By inspection of ﬁgure 5.14b it is evident that the Reynolds number is larger than 30000 from

of the blade design is handled separately in [53] since the airfoil proﬁle must converge into a

in ﬁgure 5.14a, which apart from near the hub is preserved as stated previously as a design

To put it brieﬂy, two additional airfoils from the SD70xx series, more speciﬁcally SD7032 and

point is that knowing the chord, thickness and lift distribution, the twist may be derived using
the airfoil data and linear interpolation. With a ﬁnal design in place, which is presented in
ﬁgure 5.16a a negative mold was designed by Robert Mikkelsen, see ﬁgure 5.16b and is described
in detail in [53].

Even though a single point design has formed the basis for the design process of this blade, it
is expected that the CT curve is well captured in the variable speed range and less accurate
above rated wind speed. In [58] it is shown how the CT curve of a thrust-performance blade
can be changed by pitching the blades. Hence the CT coeﬃcient can be matched at multiple tip
speed ratios, with a built in pitch control, which is implemented in this project and described
in details in section 5.4.
5.3.1
Casting and assembly of blades
The negative blade mold was CNC milled from a piece of polyurethane elastomer known as
’CibaTool’. This material was chosen due to its excellent machinability and ability to maintain
its shape. The demand of low mass for the blades while maintaining suﬃcient strength, dura-
bility and stiﬀness, made carbon ﬁber an obvious choice.
It was decided to aim for a lightweight design with one layer of weaved carbon ﬁber (ρ =80g/m2)
aligned with the span of the blade, followed by a unidirectional carbon ﬁber band (w =25 mm,
ρ =295g/m2) also aligned with the span of the blade to increase the spanwise stiﬀness. The
next layer of carbon ﬁber with a ﬁner weaving is oriented at an 45◦angle with respect to the
span. The weaved carbon ﬁbers have a density of approximately 93 g/m2.
Slow curing epoxy resin was used as adhesive and hardener. The amount of epoxy resin should
match the mass of the carbon ﬁber.
The ﬁrst step is to hone the mold and consecutively apply wax, see ﬁgure 5.17a. By polishing
the mold with wax, a thin layer of wax in the order of a few micrometers is established and
prevents the epoxy resin from sticking to the mold.
Then epoxy resin is applied to the mold, the ﬁrst layer of carbon ﬁber is laid up in the mold
and more epoxy resin is applied, see ﬁgure 5.17b. The unidirectional band is laid up from the
root of the blade to the tip, and on top the carbon ﬁber oriented 45◦is laid up, see ﬁgure
5.17c. Another layer of epoxy resin is applied, to prevent any dry spots, since the strength of
the carbon ﬁbers only is obtained if the ﬁbers are plastered with epoxy.
In ﬁgure 5.17d the remaining layers needed before the cast is complete are shown. Besides the
carbon ﬁber layers, a layer of white release ﬁlm in nylon, a layer of blue ﬁlm with numerous
holes, and a layer of white suction felt is applied. The purpose of the suction felt is to ensure
that a vacuum is present across the entire blade section.
Next a sheet of tight and durable plastic wrap encases the mold and putty is used for tightening
the plastic wrap to the plate on which the mold is situated.
88
==PAGE:107==
CHAPTER 5. DOWNSCALING AND CONSTRUCTION

Figure 5.16: Final design for the performance scaled blade
Finally a vacuum is created and after 24 hours of hardening the two sections of the blade is
taken out of the mold. Any excess material is cut away and the suction and pressure sides are
ready for assembly. A blade partition is cut from a plate of foam which in advance had been
reinforced with a layer of carbon ﬁber on each side. The blade partition is inserted at 30 % of
the chord from the leading edge.
A slow curing epoxy is used to joint the suction and pressure side. Tiny beams of foam at the
inner side of the leading edge of the blade ease the connection.
A cylindrical plug to be inserted at the root of the blade was made from a 18 mm board of
balsa wood, which has very low density. The balsa wood plug is also reinforced with a layer of
carbon ﬁber on each surface.

Figure 5.17: Casting steps for model blades
A diﬀerence in the range of 10 g between the blades was detected once the blades were assembled.
In order to balance the mass and the center of mass for the individual blades, a piece of brass
band aligned with the airﬂow was attached to the pressure side of two of the blades in order
to minimize the disturbance of the ﬂow, such that they would match the heavier blade in mass
and center of mass.
One of the ﬁnished blades is shown in ﬁgure 5.18.

==PAGE:109==

(d) Close up view of vortex generators
Figure 5.18: Assembled blade
In ﬁgure 5.18a the center of mass is indicated by the small silver triangles.
As stated earlier, the airfoil proﬁle is thicker at the inboard part, and combined with a lower
Reynolds number the risk of ﬂow separation is higher in this region of the blade. Therefore it
was decided to attach vortex generators to the inboard part of the blade to delay ﬂow separation
and thus aerodynamic stall.
5.4
Nacelle and hub design
The nacelle of the model wind turbine is much more simple in its composition compared to a full
scale nacelle, and the number of components is reduced signiﬁcantly for the model. However the
main function of the nacelle is the same, namely housing the generator and the pitch actuator.
It was decided not to strive for a streamlined nacelle design, since the Froude scaled wind only
induces a small drag on the nacelle. Instead the main focus was to match the weight of the
model nacelle with the scaled weight. The complete nacelle and hub is illustrated in ﬁgure 5.19.
The pitch control system on the model wind turbine implies added complexity of the wind
turbine design. The basic idea in the pitch mechanism for the model is to convert the rotary
motion of the pitch actuator into a translatory motion which again can be converted into an
angular displacement of the blades.
By introducing a gear the generator does not have to be concentric with the wind turbine shaft,
and instead the pitch actuator can be axially aligned with the hub. This design is preferable
since a minimum number of joints is advisable in the pitching mechanism in order to minimize
play.
Obviously, the hub rotates, while the pitch actuator must be stationary. If the pitch actuator
would also have to rotate this would put a challenge on the cabling and most likely the eﬀect
91
==PAGE:110==
CHAPTER 5. DOWNSCALING AND CONSTRUCTION
Figure 5.19: Complete nacelle and hub
of any rotor imbalances grows.
Usually a pitch mechanism can be designed in two diﬀerent ways. Either the pitch can be
controlled from the center of the hub, which is a common technique for propellers for ships and
smaller airplanes. Alternatively the pitch angle is regulated directly at the root of the blade.
The ﬁrst method entails a robust and simple design, whereas the latter method requires a more
complex design, but with the beneﬁt of a reduced play, and usually it is possible to design the
latter system more lightweight. The latter mechanism was selected.
The pitching mechanism is illustrated in ﬁgure 5.22. The pitch actuator is connected to a ball
screw spindle which is ﬁtted with a spindle carrier. The spindle carrier is fastened to a hollow
tube and at the other end of the hollow tube a ball bearing comprises the link between the
hollow tube and the so-called pitchblock. The screw spindle can unhindered slide inside the
tube.
The pitchblock has three grooves belonging to one blade each. Inside the grooves a small brick
(pitchbrick), connected to the periphery of the bearing cup, glides in relation to the pitchblock.
The blades are connected to the bearing cup and hence a back and forth motion of the pitchblock
is converted into an angular displacement of the blades. A close up of the pitchblock, pitchbrick
and bearing cup interaction is shown in ﬁgure 5.20.
The bearing cups are fastened to the main shaft via ball bearings and the main shaft also makes
up the supporting structure for the pitchblock. At the tip of the main shaft a supporting plate
strengthens the hub.
The main shaft is ﬁtted with a ball bearing and this ball bearing is ﬁtted to a bush, such that
the entire hub is connected to the support plate of the nacelle, which comprises of an aluminum
plate where the generator and pitch actuator also are mounted.
The pitch actuator is located a distance from the support plate, in order to make room for the
translatory motion of the spindle carrier. Four support rods are used to hold the pitch actuator
92
==PAGE:111==
CHAPTER 5. DOWNSCALING AND CONSTRUCTION
Figure 5.20: Blade pitching mechanism
in place, and moreover four smaller support rods prevents the spindle carrier from rotating.
The support rods are all fastened to the support plate at one end and connected to a motor
plate at the other end. The purpose of the motor plate is to comprise an attachment point for
the spindle carrier rods which must be tightened to ensure that the rods do not deﬂect, since
this would inhibit the motion of the spindle carrier.
The support plate is pinned to the base plate of the nacelle, which is mountable to the tower
via a strain gauge. The angle between the frame plate and the base plate is 85 ◦in accordance
with the actual shaft tilt angle for the prototype. In fact the design of the prototype prescribes
that the rotor is forward coned an angle of -2.5◦, but due to a substantial increase in design
complexity by implementing this, it was decided that the inclination of the shaft would be
suﬃcient, to avoid the blades from hitting the tower.
At rated wind speed, the hub of the model is rotating at 71 rpm, and therefore a gear ratio
of 1:25 must be employed in order to increase the rotational speed of the generator to 1770
rpm. A large helical gear ﬁtted to the main shaft and a smaller helical gear fastened to the
generator shaft constitutes a 1:5 gear ratio. Helical gears, which are of the oblique-toothed
type, have higher friction than regular spur gears due to a larger contact surface between the
gears.
However, they run more smoothly and quietly, inducing less vibrations to the wind
turbine, and hence the disruption of other measurement devices is reduced. The explanation
of this is that the angled teeth of the helical gears engage gradually compared to the teethes of
spur gears, which engages abruptly.
The helical gears are made from nylon, which is appropriate for gear pairs, where the power
being transmitted is relatively low.
Between the small gear and the generator a planetary gear increases the rotational speed with
a another factor of 5. The planetary gear is a compact gear solution with the great beneﬁt
of having aligned axes of the input and output shaft. If the generator would have to run at
lower rpm the weight of the generator will be higher due to the need for additional poles and
similarly the price will go up.
5.4.1
Selection of material
The scaled mass of the hub and the nacelle is 0.476 kg and 2.014 kg, respectively. The target is
to scale the hub and nacelle correctly, but since the gyroscopic eﬀects of the hub is very limited
compared to the gyroscopic eﬀects from the blades, a target mass for the combined nacelle and
hub of 2.49 kg is aimed at instead.
Initially the generator and the actuator for the pitch control was selected, since these two units
were expected to be major contributors to the total mass. Two identical servomotors were
chosen since the controlling unit of this particular servo motor is well known. The servomotors
can be remotely controlled. Information on the servo motor is listed in table 5.3.
With 1.29 kg of mass in disposal for the remaining parts of the nacelle and hub, great care had
93
==PAGE:112==
CHAPTER 5. DOWNSCALING AND CONSTRUCTION

(b)

94
==PAGE:113==
CHAPTER 5. DOWNSCALING AND CONSTRUCTION
Figure 5.23: Assembled nacelle and hub
to be taken with respect to the selection of materials.
It was decided to use aluminum for the majority of the parts, due to its high mechanical strength
in proportion to its weight. For the support rods for the pitch actuator and the spindle carrier,
extra stiﬀness was demanded, and hence stainless steel was used. Furthermore, stainless steel
was used for the bushes connecting the pitch actuator to the generator and the spindle carrier
to the hollow tube. Brass was used for the bush due to its relatively low hardness, but with
otherwise high stiﬀness. This makes it ideal for an assembly where two parts are joint together
by a squeezing technique.
A material with a low friction coeﬃcient was sought for the pitchblock, given that the pitchblock
will glide along the main shaft.
POM (Polyoxymethylen), having a relatively low friction
coeﬃcient, seemed as a proper material, as it is also lightweight.
Deep groove double row ball bearings are used in the bearing cups, in the pitchblock and in the
connection between the support plate and the main shaft. The double row ball bearing does
not allow any inclination between the parts which naturally is a demand for the main shaft and
the blades to prevent the joints from being wobbly.
A photo sensor was installed enabling a detection of the location of the spindle carrier. In this
way a zero setting of the pitch is possible, since the pitch actuator is unable to remember its
position once the power is shut oﬀ.
The ﬁnal design of the nacelle and hub turned out to weight 3.48 kg, which corresponds to an
exceedance of 40 %. Figure 5.23 is a photo of the assembled nacelle and hub. The cables from
the nacelle is guided inside the tower to the bottom of the tower where it is assembled with
cables from the ﬂoater. The construction drawings for the nacelle and hub is found in appendix
E.
5.5
Wind turbine tower
Given the simple geometry of the tower it should be a straight forward task to construct a
tower in model scale. However, apart from the dimensions and weight, a key parameter to
match is the bending stiﬀness, since the bending stiﬀness is governing for the natural frequency
of the tower. Matching the natural frequency for the tower is essential, since the structural
frequencies of the tower can be excited by the 1P or 3P frequencies. Moreover extreme waves
can excite a response of the tower, a phenomena known as ringing.
Due to the ﬂoating support of the tower which is relatively soft, it is expected that the tower
will only be excited at its ﬁrst natural frequency in its most fundamental mode shape. For the
original full scale tower the ﬁrst natural frequency is 0.25 Hz.
95
==PAGE:114==
CHAPTER 5. DOWNSCALING AND CONSTRUCTION
The tower on the TLP is reduced to 95.6m, and in section 4.4.3 it is shown that this reduction
of the original tower height leads to an increase in the ﬁrst tower bending natural frequency to
0.33 Hz, but at the early stage of the design phase it was decided to aim for the same natural
frequency of 0.25 Hz as for the original tower, which corresponds to 1.94 Hz in model scale.
In october 2012 an experimental study of a 5 MW TLP model was conducted at DHI, [43].
The properties of the wind turbine tower used in [43] is quite similar to the target values in
this project, with respect to mass and height. This tower is not conical as the full scale tower,
and it is also undersized in diameter, but this is considered of secondary importance, since the
cross sectional geometry of the tower primarily has an impact on the aerodynamic drag on the
tower. The height of the model tower is 1.61 m, which is only a deviation of 1 % from the
target value, but as it will be outlined later the distance to the nacelle will be increased by an
additional 72 mm, due to the installation of a strain gauge for monitoring of thrust.
In section 8.2 the results of a pure tower decay test are presented, and from these tests the ﬁrst
natural frequencies in the x- and y-direction are extracted
ftower,x = 2.0Hz
ftower,y = 2.4Hz
(5.29)
Given that the natural frequency in the x-direction is only diﬀerentiating by 3 % from the
target natural frequency of 1.94 Hz, it was decided to move forward with the tower from DHI.
An advantage of this tower is that the tower frequency will certainly not be excited by the 3P
frequency. In 5.24 the 1P and 3P spans are plotted along with the wave energy spectrum for
all the environmental conditions introduced in section 2.8.

Figure 5.24 demonstrates that the model tower with a frequency of 2.0 Hz in the x-direction
(0.268Hz in full scale) does not coincide with any critical excitation frequencies.

a rough estimate of the coupled natural frequency of the tower bending mode and the pitch
motion as being roughly 10-20 % below the tower natural frequency, based on the results from
the FEM model, shows that the coupled natural frequency of the tower and the pitch motion
should not conﬂict with the peak frequency of the sea states or the 1P frequency.
5.6
Floater construction
With the overall ﬂoater design in place, the next task is to design the individual parts of the
ﬂoater.
The design is simple in the sense that it basically consists of the ﬂoater tube the
transition piece and the spokes. Discs at the top of bottom of the ﬂoater tube and transition
piece serve as mounting surfaces for the spokes, the tower and for linkage between the ﬂoater
tube and the transition piece.
96
==PAGE:115==
CHAPTER 5. DOWNSCALING AND CONSTRUCTION
In ﬁgure 5.25 the assembled ﬂoater is shown with 3 and 4 spokes, respectively. The construction
drawings of the individual pieces is found in appendix F.
(a) 4 spokes ﬂoater
(b) 3 spokes ﬂoater
Figure 5.25: Assembled ﬂoater
To ensure that the ﬂoater is tight, grooves are cut at the circumference of all the discs to make
room for an O-ring. A groove is cut and an O-ring added at the connecting surface between
the top disc of the ﬂoater tube and bottom disc of the transition piece tube. It is ensured that
the O-rings are always located between the screws and the airtight interior of the ﬂoater and
transition piece, since water can easily sink in through the screw holes. Moreover heavy duty
grease is used to seal the ﬂoater.
A system for easy changing of the spoke conﬁguration is sketched in ﬁgure 5.26. The spokes
are made of 20x20x2mm RHS proﬁles in stainless steel which each are welded to its own 3mm
plate of stainless steel (spokeplate). Five holes in the spokeplate enables an easy mounting of
the spokeplates to the bottom disc of the ﬂoater. The holes in the bottom disc of the ﬂoater
are drilled such that either 4 spokes perpendicular to each other or 3 spokes with an angular
distance of 120◦can be mounted. Eyebolts are mounted at the tip of the spokes such that the
tendons can easily be attached to the ﬂoater. At the top of the transition piece an aluminum
ring is mounted. Another aluminum ring is free to rotate inside the ﬁxed aluminum ring. The
tower is mounted on the inner ring, and once the screws in joint between the tower and ﬂoater
are tightened, the inner ring cannot rotate. In this way it is possible to adjust the yaw angle
of the wind turbine.
A tube of acrylic glass with a diameter of 300 mm and a thickness of 6 mm was used for the
ﬂoater, while a tube of acrylic glass with a diameter of 150mm and a thickness of 3 mm was
used for the transition piece. The discs at the bottom and top of the ﬂoater tube and transition
piece tube were made from a 20 mm PVC plate. PVC is a rather low weight solid material
and by using M8 bolt and drilling the holes 15 mm in depth the assembled ﬂoater should be
97
==PAGE:116==
CHAPTER 5. DOWNSCALING AND CONSTRUCTION
4 Spokes
3 Spokes
Figure 5.26: Mounting of spokes
suﬃciently robust. A disc of acrylic glass was mounted at the intersection between the ﬂoater
tube and the transition piece, constituting a platform for later installation of accelerometers.
By having a transparent ﬂoater it is easy to check for leakages and to check that the instru-
mentation is oriented correctly. Stickers are added to the transition piece in order to identify
the point of ﬂotation. Screws of brass are used for assembly in order to minimize corrosion.
5.6.1
Tendon material
It was decided to use kevlar rope as tendons for the model, due to its high tensile stiﬀness. The
kevlar rope is 2.5 mm in diameter, made of Dyneema ﬁbers® and has a breaking load of 569
kg. The stiﬀness of the tendons were determined from a simple setup in which the tendon was
elongated by adding known masses in the range from 5 - 35 kg. The setup is depicted in ﬁgure
5.27a,
98
==PAGE:117==

(a) Experimental setup

Loading

(b) Elongation of the tendon as a function of loading
Figure 5.27: Determination of tendon stiﬀness
From ﬁgure 5.27b it is evident that the elongation of the tendon is diﬀerent, dependent on
whether the tendon is being loaded of unloaded. As the tendon is unloaded the tendon does
not return to the same state as during loading, thus remaining a little longer. First when all
the weights are removed the tendon returns to its original length. This is actually an example
of hysteresis, which is caused by dissipation of energy due to internal friction.
It can be deduced that the tendon does not obey Hooke’s law perfectly, which is given as
σ = Eϵ
(5.30)
where σ is the tensile stress, E is Youngs modulus and ϵ is the relative deﬂection computed as
∆L/L. Nevertheless it was decided to apply Hookes law to compute EA. A linear ﬁt between
the tensile stress and the elongation was computed for the loading case and the unloading case
and an average of the slopes was used as an estimate for E. The EA-value of the model tendons
is computed to 8.11 · 104 N.
5.7
Model speciﬁcations
The assembled TLPWT is shown in ﬁgure 5.28.
In table 5.4 the corresponding key speciﬁcations for the prototype wind turbine, the ideally
Froude scaled model and the actual model TLP wind turbine are collected.
By inspection of table 5.4 the diﬀerence in mass of the nacelle and hub for the Froude model
scale and the actual model scale really stands out, with an exceedance in the actual mass of
40%. Moreover the mass of the ﬂoater is too large for the actual model scale, especially for the
conﬁguration with 4 spokes. However as outlined in section 6 the z-gauges for monitoring of
the tendon tension each weigh 250 g, and consequently are decisive for the excess mass.
This increase in the mass of the entire model, naturally leads to a decrease in the natural fre-
quencies in all modes of motion. Especially the drastic increase in mass of the nacelle, results in
an increase in the mass moment of inertia which most likely will lead to a considerable decrease
in the pitch and roll natural frequencies. Moreover the increased mass of the nacelle and hub is
expected to induce a destabilizing eﬀect in the response as the model rolls and pitches. Yet, the
ﬁne compliance for the manufactured model blades must be highlighted. Given the complex
shape, large dimension and low target mass, it is quite impressive to be within 5% of the target

Chapter 6
Experimental setup and execution
6.1
Experimental setup
The experiments were conducted in the deep water wave basin at DHI Water & Environment
in Hoersholm, Denmark. The length of the basin is 20m, the width is 30m and the depth is 3m
throughout the entire basin, with the possibility of increasing the depth at the center of the
basin to 6 or even 12m.
The wave maker is of the hinged type and consists of 60 individually controlled ﬂaps, all
operated by hydraulic pistons. The wave maker is capable of generating unidirectional regular
and irregular waves with a heading of up to Θ = 30◦. Furthermore the wave maker is able
to generate multi-directional waves. Opposite of the wave maker a passive wave absorber is
located, with the aim of dissipating wave energy and reduce wave reﬂection. At the sides of the
wave basin small stripes of permeable mat functions as wave absorbers to reduce the reﬂection
from oblique waves.
A bridge, with the ability to move in the transverse direction of the basin, serves as the work
station from where the wave makers are controlled. Additionally all the equipment for data
acquisition is located on the bridge.
In ﬁgure 6.1 a top view of the wave basin is sketched. Four meters from the wave maker the
mooring platform is immersed. The platform is build of wooden beams and weighed down
with bricks of lead. Numerous eyebolts are pinned to the platform constituting the anchoring
points for the three diﬀerent mooring conﬁgurations. The tendons are lead through the anchor
points, then trough eyebolts located at one common edge of the platform and then gathered
on the bridge where the tendons are fastened. The individual tendons are coiled around their
respective anchor point in an attempt to constrain the tendons to be elastic only from the
model to the platform, while still being adjustable from the bridge.
The wind generator is located on top of the wave maker.
It was necessary to construct a
supporting structure of wooden beams in between the wave maker pistons, such that the wind
generator can have a stable platform. In ﬁgure 6.2 the experimental setup is sketched. Due to
the concrete borders of the wave basin and the wave paddles it was not possible to lower the
wind generator suﬃciently in order to cover the entire rotor area. One solution would be to tilt
the wind generator forward, but due to the size of the wind generator this was not an option.
Consequently this misaligned is tolerated in the experiments. The complete setup is shown in
ﬁgure 6.3
6.1.1
Mooring conﬁguration
The three mooring conﬁgurations being tested are shown in ﬁgure 6.4, where the tendons are
indexes. A thorough description of the three conﬁguration is conducted in section 4.4.1.
