6.1.2
Instrumentation
Apart from monitoring the surface elevation in 11 locations, it was decided to measure the six
modes of motion, the accelerations in the ﬂoater and the nacelle, the tendon tension and the
thrust force of the model.
Wave gauges
The eleven wave gauges used in the experimental setup (WG 1 - 11) are sketched in ﬁgure 6.1.
A wave gauge is a simple monitoring device which consists of two thin rods of stainless steel in
parallel. When submerged in water an electric circuit is established, and the voltage drop in
the circuit is proportional to the level of submergence.
The wave gauge is calibrated by submerging it to two known depths and a linear relationship
between voltage and submergence/surface elevation can be extracted.
As depicted in ﬁgure 6.1 eleven wave gauges measures the surface elevation in the basin. The
5-gauge setup in front of the model facilitates an analysis of the wave reﬂection in the basin.
However such an analysis is not conducted in this report.
Wave gauge number 11 (WG 11) is located at the exact position where the model is intended
to be installed. Hence the surface elevation measurements from WG 11 will serve as reference
for all subsequent experiments with the model. Five tripods were placed in the water and from
aluminum beams connecting the tripods the wave gauges were hung. In this way the diﬀracted
waves from the tripods did not impact the surface elevation measurements.
Z gauges
The tendon tension is captured by Z-gauges. This type of gauge is Z-shaped, made of stainless
steel and when the Z-gauge is elongated a strain gauge monitors the deformation which through
103
==PAGE:122==
CHAPTER 6. EXPERIMENTAL SETUP AND EXECUTION
4m
3m
Wind generator
Wave paddle
Wave absorber
Wind direction
Wave direction
Water line
Figure 6.2: Side view of the experimental setup
Hooks law is converted to tension force. Eyebolts at either ends of the Z-gauge renders the
possibility of inserting the Z-gauge as a link in the mooring line. A disadvantage of the Z-gauges
is the mass of 250g each, which indisputable will have an eﬀect on the dynamics of the model.
According to [59] the accuracy of the Z-gauges is 1 % of the measured value and the resolution
is 0.1 % of the force range.
The calibration of the Z-gauges was conducted by suspending the Z-gauges one at a time and
gradually suspend a given number of weights with known masses. An upper limit of around
200N was chosen, which guaranteed a suﬃcient safety margin. In this way a linear relationship
between loading and output voltage was obtained. It was tested that leaving the Z-gauges in
the wave basin overnight did not change the readings.
Strain gauge
A two component strain gauge links the tower and the nacelle. The top and bottom of the
strain gauge is connected by four pillars, and each pillar is ﬁtted with two uniaxial ﬁlm strain
gauges. This setup enables measurements of the shear force between the nacelle and the tower,
which can be interpreted as the thrust force on the rotor and transverse forces arising from
unsteadiness in the rotor rotation.
In order to properly calibrate the nacelle strain gauge, the tower was aligned horizontally and
calibrated by suspending known weights in the lateral and transverse direction, respectively,
104

resulting in a linear description of the loading and output voltage.
During the experiments the thrust measurements had a tendency of being contaminated by
an oﬀset of around 0.2 N even though the strain gauge was reset before the tests. One very
reasonable explanation for this is a heterogeneous cooling of the pillars and strain gauge due to
the wind ﬁeld. To reduce the possibility of this uneven cooling the strain gauge was encircled
by a piece of paper.
105
==PAGE:124==
CHAPTER 6. EXPERIMENTAL SETUP AND EXECUTION
Accelerometers
Five single axis accelerometers are used for monitoring accelerations sensed by the model. Three
accelerometers are mounted perpendicular to each other in the ﬂoater at the top of the ﬂoater
comprising a 3 axis system. Two accelerometers are attached underneath the nacelle measuring
the acceleration in the x and y direction.
Measurements of the acceleration in the nacelle and ﬂoater in the x and y direction enables an
estimation of the tower frequency. Moreover the accelerations of the ﬂoater is comparable to
the Qualisys displacement output, since the transition piece is very stiﬀcompared to the tower.
The accelerometers weighs 29.1g each, and hence the mass of the nacelle is increased by around
2 %.
A static gravitational calibration was carried out in order to obtain a three point reading at 0
and ±1g. The relationship between acceleration and voltage is linear and the range was set to
cover ±2g.
Qualisys
The Qualisys system extracts the surge, sway, heave, roll, pitch and yaw motion of the TLPWT.
It consists of two infrared cameras that track the motion of the TLPWT and a piece of software
that processes the camera readings. At the top of the transition piece an aluminum frame
parallel to the water surface makes up a platform on which three white spheres are mounted at
diﬀerent heights. An additional sphere is ﬁtted to the lower part of the tower, and these four
spheres constitutes a frame of reference. The spheres cannot move independently and hence the
motion of the spheres is directly linked to the motion of the model. A certain spacing between
the spheres is necessary in order to separate the surge, sway, heave, roll, pitch and yaw motion
of the model in the Qualisys system, and likewise the spheres must at all time be visible to
both cameras. At the same time the spheres must be within sight of the camera and neither
the spheres or the supporting structure must be in the way of the rotor. Moreover the size of
the supporting structure should be minimized in size and weight in order to corrupt the motion
of the model as little as possible. The chosen sphere-setup is a compromise of these conditions.
The Qualisys software outputs the motion which is then sampled.
The calibration is carried out by ﬁxing a right-angled frame to the top of the transition piece
with the tower unmounted. The frame is aligned with the x and y direction and spheres are
located along the edges, such that the origin, the x, y and z axis of the frame of reference can
be established. Subsequently the four spheres and wind turbine are installed.
According to [59], the accuracy of the positioning system is generally within 1mm, which with
the applied spacing of the tracking objects corresponds to an accuracy in the order of 0.1 deg
for rotary motion.
Data Acquisition
The output signals from the measuring instruments are collected through the analog gates of a
DHI Standard Cabinet 101E. The input voltage must be in the range from ± 10 V. In order to
maximize the resolution of the signal the gain of the incoming analog signal can be adjusted,
such that expected minimum/maximum readings correspond to -10/10 V. Moreover the zero is
manually set on the cabinet.
The cabinet outputs digital signals which are transferred to a nearby computer. A piece of DHI
in-house software "Wave Synthesizer(c)" (WS) reads these digital signals and stores them in a
mat.-ﬁle which is compatible with the data handling tool Matlab.
The calibration of all instruments is conducted in Wave Synthesizer.
All instruments are
consistently reset every time a series of tests is initiated.
106
==PAGE:125==
CHAPTER 6. EXPERIMENTAL SETUP AND EXECUTION
Sampling frequency
A sampling frequency of 160 Hz was chosen in order to ensure a proper resolution of the input
data.
This is a relative high sampling frequency, since no important or physical frequencies are ex-
pected to occur above 31.0Hz which is the rotational speed of the servomotor at the maximum
rotational speed of the rotor. However the frequency of the alternating current is 50 Hz, and
it is recommendable to have this frequency resolved. The Nyquist criterion must be obeyed,
which states the the sampling frequency must be twice the magnitude of the frequency present
in the signal, in order to avoid aliasing. Furthermore the sampling frequency should not be
divisible with the frequency of the alternating current. Hence a sampling frequency of 120 Hz
is decent, but Wave Synthesizer did not allow this sampling rate, neither 130 Hz, and therefore
the selected sampling frequency ended at 160 Hz.
Air Velocity Transducers
At DHI the hot spheres which were used during the construction phase of the wind generator
were not available. Instead four TSI 8455 air velocity transducers (AVT), which are suitable for
low speed measurements, were employed. An AVT consists of a rugged ceramic pin protected
by a casing which only allows a 2D ﬂow to pass the probe. The rest of the sensor is basically
a thin metal cylinder intended to disturb the incoming ﬂow as little as possible. A maximum
velocity of not more than 2 m/s were expected, and consequently the lowest range of 0 m/s
- 5 m/s were chosen. The accuracy of the AVT is ±2% of the reading or ±0.5% of full scale
of selected range. In other words the accuracy is ±0.025 m/s below 1.25 m/s and increases
linearly from ±0.025 m/s to ±0.04 m/s in the interval from 1.25 m/s to 2 m/s. The AVT
samples at a frequency of 5 Hz and the repeatability is within ±1%. The AVT’s did not need
any calibration.
Paddle movement
The feedback signal from wave paddle nr. 31 is sampled. This allows for a comparison of
the actual movements of the wave paddle and the corresponding surface elevation, in case the
target wave climate behaves much diﬀerent from what is expected.
Wave Generation
The WS software also controls the wave generation in the basin. A custom ﬁle format is loaded
into the system, which contains the pattern of movement for the individual wave paddles. It
was attempted to generate all wave paddle signals in a Matlab-script, which utilizes a transfer
function to transform the speciﬁed wave climate into a signal that prescribes the paddle motion.
At DHI irregular wave series signals are usually generated in a DOS-program, and for some sea
state it was necessary to use this program,see 7.1.1.
The wave maker has certain limitations, with respect to stroke length, speed of paddle displace-
ment and relative diﬀerence between adjacent wave paddles. Moreover instant wave breaking at
the wave paddles puts a limit on the obtainable waves. This is outlined in ﬁgure 6.5, which also
includes the sea states. From ﬁgure 6.5 reﬂections from the backwall is not an issue according
to ﬁgure 6.5, and the waves do not break at the wave generator.
In spite of a missing extension of the curve for the general mechanical overload below H = 0.1,
it looks as if the lower sea states could conﬂict with this criterion.

==PAGE:126==
CHAPTER 6. EXPERIMENTAL SETUP AND EXECUTION

and peak period of the chosen sea states. Handed out by DHI

6.2
Test programme
The test programme included the generation of regular waves, irregular sea states, focused
waves and white noise sea states, as detailed in the following. These were all based on the
environmental conditions (EC) deﬁned in table 2.1.
Regular waves are not representative of real sea states, but they are easy to analyse and
valuable for system identiﬁcation. For this reason, regular waves with wave heights and
periods corresponding to the signiﬁcant wave heights and peak periods of the ECs, re-
spectively, were included in the test programme with a heading of both 0 degrees and 30
degrees to the global x-axis. The waves with a heading angle of 0 degrees were denoted
R01, R02,..., R10, with wave heights and periods corresponding to EC1,EC2,...,EC10,
while the waves with a heading angle of 30 degrees were denoted R11, R12,...,R20. All
tests with regular waves were given a full scale duration of 30 min, corresponding to 4 min
in model scale.
Irregular sea states are the most representative of real sea conditions, but their analysis
is also more involved. It was decided to include both long-crested irregular waves with
heading angles of 0 and 30 degrees to the global x-axis as well as spread waves. The
long-crested waves are simpler to analyse, and further this facilitates the comparison of
two dimensional and three dimensional forcing. Irregular sea states were deﬁned from
Piearson-Moskowitz spectra with the signiﬁcant wave heights and peak periods deﬁned in
the environmental conditions, table 2.1. For the spread waves the spreading was described
by the cosine-power distribution as given in (2.14) with s = 10. It was found that the
largest irregular sea states exceeded the limits on stroke and velocity of the wave paddle
and could therefore not be generated. This was the case for the long-crested waves based
on EC9 and EC10, and the spread waves based on EC10. It was decided to run 1 h
realizations of all irregular sea states, and in addition to run 3 h realizations based on
EC8 parameters, which represent harsh sea states that could be generated both with
long-crested and spread waves, to get larger extreme waves. The 1 h realizations of the
long-crested waves were denoted I01, I02,...,I08 and I11,I12,...,I18 for headings of 0 and
30 degrees to the x-axis respectively, with parameters corresponding to EC1,EC2,...,EC8.
The 1 h realizations of the spread waves were denoted I21,I22,...,I29 corresponding to
EC1,EC2,...,EC9. The 3 h realizations of EC8, were denoted I38, I48 and I58, for the
long-crested 0 degree, 30-degree and spread waves respectively.
Focused waves were in the present work used to study the eﬀect of a single large wave. It
was attempted to produce focused waves of the New Year Wave type, at the position of
the WT, but this attempt was unsuccessful, since the surface elevation was not symmet-
ric in time. The New Year Wave is created by waves with many frequency components
which are generated by the wave paddle, such that they all meet at the WT, with a
resulting excitation of the structure over a broad range of frequencies. The focused waves
were created with wave heights corresponding to the expected maximum wave height
approximated by (2.6) in irregular sea states with the parameters of the environmental
conditions. As for the long-crested irregular waves, it was not possible to generate fo-
cused waves based on EC9 and EC10 due to limitations on the wave paddles. The focused
waves were generated in a long-crested form with both 0 degree heading and 30 degree
heading, denoted F01,F02,...,F08, and F11,F12,...,F18, and with directional spread, de-
noted F21,F22,...,F28, with the expected maximum wave heights of sea states described
by EC1,EC2,...,EC8, respectively.
White noise wave series were used to determine Response Amplitude Operators (RAO) for
the structures. These waves were generated as a 1 h realization of an irregular long-
crested sea state with a heading of 0 degrees, based on a rectangular spectrum. The low-
109
==PAGE:128==
CHAPTER 6. EXPERIMENTAL SETUP AND EXECUTION
and high cut frequencies were chosen as 0.5 Hz and 2.0 Hz respectively, and the spectrum
area determined as to give Hs of EC2, by equation (2.8). Hence, the white noise wave
was denoted W02.
First, the undisturbed wave ﬁeld for all waves was measured without the structure in the water,
and these tests were given as description the abbreviation for the tested wave, e.g. R01, I23,
F14, W02, etc.
Next, the structures were mounted in the basin an subjected to the waves without wind, and
these tests were identiﬁed by S1, S2 or S3 in addition to the wave abbreviation, e.g. S1R02,
S2I13, S3F07 etc.
Finally, the three structures were tested in simultaneous wind and waves, which in the test
identiﬁcation was marked by adding a W after the letter in the wave abbreviation, e.g. S1IW04,
S2FW02, S3RW18, S2WW02 etc. The wind speed was chosen in accordance with the wind
speed of the EC from which the wave is derived. As documented in section 7.2, the maximum
mean wind speed that could be generated in the experimental setup was 1.7m/s, meaning that
the wind speeds of EC6-EC10 could not be modelled. It was decided to run these experiments
at the maximum obtainable wind speed, and for EC6 and EC7 to operate the model WT as
for this wind speed, while for EC8-EC10 to pitch out the blades since the desired wind speeds
for these ECs exceed the cut-out wind speed.
It was managed to cover almost all tests for the three structures, and a complete overview of
the conducted tests is found in the test matrix, in appendix D. Completed tests are assigned
with a check mark in the ﬁrst column of the test matrix, whereas unﬁnished tests are indicated
by a red cell in the ﬁrst column
6.3
Dataprocessing
6.3.1
Removal of transients
In the execution of the experiments, it was chosen to log all data from the instant the wave
maker was started, to a point after the wave maker was stopped for the irregular waves, and
for the regular waves the data logging and wave maker were stopped at the same time. This
procedure, was followed to be able to see in the data if also the transient behaviour especially
at the beginning of the experiment proceeded normally. However, for a major part of the data
analysis it was desired to analyse only the stationary part of the experiment, which calls for a
method to remove the transient parts.
Figure 6.6 shows the surge response of structure 1 to regular waves 1 and 10, which are the
smallest and largest regular waves respectively, from the start of logging and 90 s into the
measurement. In the ﬁrst 15 s of the measurement, the ﬁrst waves arrive at the TLP model,
and the model is brought from rest into periodic motion. For the smallest wave, the response
does not seem to attain a constant amplitude, like it is the case for the response to the largest
wave. It is noted, however, that the variation in response amplitude is in the order of the
expected accuracy of the optical tracking system, and further the regular waves at this size
were also seen to vary in size. The transients in both plots is judged to have died signiﬁcantly
out at t = 30 s and this was used as the starting point for all regular waves, since equivalent
observations were done for the response to oblique waves and in the case of superimposed wind.
Since the wave maker, for the regular waves, was operated until the end of the logging of data,
there was no need to remove transients at the end of the measurement. This reduced the total
duration of the measurements to 210 s.
For the irregular sea states, the idea was to conduct 1 h realizations of all sea states, and 3 h
realizations of the most harsh sea states, which in model scale corresponds to 465 s and 1394 s,
respectively. The durations of the corresponding wave generation ﬁles were 500 s-550 s and
1500 s respectively, and it was chosen to cut oﬀthe measurements at the instant the operation

The removal of the ﬁrst part of the signal was adjusted such
that the total duration of the signal matched the 1 h and 3 h realizations, meaning that as a
minimum the ﬁrst 35 s were removed from the 1 h realizations, and 106 s were removed from
the 3 h realizations. Based on the analysis of the response to regular waves, this is regarded
suﬃcient to remove the initial transients.
A more thorough analysis of the transients would include an investigation of build up of re-
ﬂections in the basin and possible excitation of the basins eigenmodes, but this was considered
out of scope of the present data analysis, though this can be done based on the acquired
measurements.
6.3.2
Butterworth ﬁlter
A Butterworth lowpass ﬁlter was applied to all measurements with a highcut frequency of 20 Hz,
to ﬁlter out high frequency components in the signal that cannot be related to the physics of
the scale model and the experimental environment. E.g. a signiﬁcant peak was observed at the
grid frequency at 50 Hz. The highcut frequency was chosen as high as possible, whilst having
a reasonable smoothing eﬀect in the time domain. This lead to a value of 20 Hz. For selected
analyses it was found necessary to lower the highcut frequency to 5 Hz.
6.3.3
Median ﬁlter
In the post-processing of the measurements from the Qualisys system, distinct spikes were
frequently observed in the heave, roll and pitch signals for test with wind. This is exempliﬁed
in ﬁgure 6.7a where ﬁve evident peaks are observed in the pitch signal. A closer investigation
reveals that the peak value is obtained from one measurement to the next, which with the chosen
sampling frequency is over
1
160Hz = 0.0063s, which is clearly not physical. The explanation to
these peaks is likely the passing of a turbine blade, which shades the tracking sphere placed on
the tower when the rotor is running, such that its position is momentary lost by the optical
tracking system, Qualisys. In eﬀect, this gives a clear fault signal in heave, roll and pitch,
but not in surge, sway and yaw. This hypothesis is backed up by considering the distances
between the spikes in ﬁgure 6.7a, which approximately are 0.56 s, 0.57 s, 0.56 s and 0.86 s,
respectively. For this example, the rotor was operated at rated speed conditions meaning that
the a blade passes the tower every 0.28 s. It is clearly seen that the time between the spikes
is approximately two times this period between the ﬁrst four spikes, and between the last two
spikes the time is approximately three times this period.
111
==PAGE:130==
CHAPTER 6. EXPERIMENTAL SETUP AND EXECUTION
It was ﬁrst attempted to ﬁlter out these spikes using a Butterworth ﬁlter, but even with a high
cut frequency as low as 5 Hz, it was not possible to remove the eﬀect of the spikes, as shown
in ﬁgure 6.7a.
In stead it was decided to apply the meridian ﬁlter medfilt1 which is a built-in Matlab tool.
This ﬁlter replaces every point in the data set by the median of the numbers in a centred window
with a width deﬁned by the user. It was attempted to have as small width of the window as
possible, but it turned out that a width of 11 points for the heave signal and 19 points for
the roll and pitch signals were needed to ﬁlter out all spikes. These window widths correspond
to a duration of 0.069 s and 0.11 s respectively, given the sampling frequency of 160 Hz. The
ﬁltered signal is shown in ﬁgure 6.7b. The unfortunate consequence of this ﬁlter is that some of
the higher frequency components, which indeed are physical, are ﬁltered out, as seen in ﬁgure
6.7b. This ﬁgure shows that the energy on the 3P and and 6P frequencies, located at 3.54 Hz
and 7.08 Hz respectively, is signiﬁcantly reduced by the median ﬁlter, which is naturally not
desirable. These frequencies, however, will still be signiﬁcant in the acceleration signals from
the nacelle accelerometers, which are not ﬁltered in this manner.
Hence, it was chosen to
apply this ﬁlter to all heave, roll and pitch signals from Qualisys, since the cost of having the
nonphysical spikes exceeds the beneﬁt of being able to detect the 3P and 6P frequencies in the
pitch signal.

6.3.4
Variance spectra
For the determination of variance spectra and spectral quantities, the present work applies
the WAFO toolbox version 2.5 for Matlab, which is documented in [60] and [61]. The WAFO
(Wave Analysis for Fatigue and Oceanography) toolbox, developed primarily at the University
of Lund, Sweden, in the years from 1993 to 2011, was created for the analysis and simulation of
random waves and random loads [60]. The use of WAFO was based on advice from DHI staﬀ,
and a thorough study into the eﬀects of the diﬀerent settings of the toolbox was not carried
out. For the spectra mainly two functions were used:
dat2spec computes a variance spectrum which can be smoothed by a variety of diﬀerent methods.
In the present study, the default Parzen window frequency smoothing is applied with a
window widths between 2·1024 and 8·1024. For this method, the smoothing is increased
when the window width is increased. The window width was by DHI staﬀadvised to be
112
==PAGE:131==
CHAPTER 6. EXPERIMENTAL SETUP AND EXECUTION
an integer of 1024, and the speciﬁc widths were chosen to give a signiﬁcant smoothing
eﬀect without smearing out important details in the spectra.
spec2mom computes the moments of the spectrum, where the zero’th moment was used to determine
the signiﬁcant wave height, as given in section 2.5.
6.3.5
Identiﬁcation of uncorrelated response peaks
A large part of the data analysis of the present work is concentrated on the presentation of
extreme displacements, accelerations and tendon tensions.
When treating these quantities
statistically, it is important that the observations are uncorrelated. This is regarded the case,
when the observations are the results of the forcing from diﬀerent waves. In this work, a wave
is deﬁned between two down-crossings, in line with [62]. Since the ﬂow is inertia dominated the
hydrodynamic force is in phase with the water particle acceleration du/dt and from linear wave
theory it is well known that the particle acceleration is in phase with the time derivative of the
surface elevation ηt. Hence the ﬂoater is expected to experience the maximum acceleration at
the event where the slope between the trough and crest is greatest, which naturally is within
the respective downcrossing period being considered. This is also evident from ﬁgure 6.8a which
shows the surface elevation of a regular wave and the corresponding acceleration of the ﬂoater
in the x direction for structure 1. The maximum displacement in the six modes of motion is

not expected to occur the exact moment the wave impact, but in stead a short moment after
as shown in ﬁgure 6.8b, where the peak of the surge is identiﬁed behind the crest of the wave.
A similar behavior is expected for sway and since the remaining modes of motion is closely
linked to the surge and sway motion the peak of all six modes of motion are determined as
the peaks falling within a period that is shifted half a period forward in time in relation to the
downcrossing period.
The tendon tension is very much dependent on the motion of the model in particular the surge
motion, and in ﬁgure 6.8c the tension in the upwind tendon (T2) and in the downwind tendon
(T4) is shown in relation to the surface elevation.
From ﬁgure 6.8c it can be deduced that the occurrence of maximum tension in the upwind
tendon and minimum tension in the downwind tendon is associated with the maximum dis-
placement in surge while the opposite incident occurs almost as the subsequent wave is ap-
113
==PAGE:132==
CHAPTER 6. EXPERIMENTAL SETUP AND EXECUTION
proaching. Hence the maximum tension in the upwind tendon and the minimum tension in
the downwind tendon is identiﬁed in a period shifted half a period forward in time, while the
minimum tension in the upwind tendon and the maximum tension in the downwind tendon is
identiﬁed in a period shifted a full period forward in time.
It should be noted that in the irregular wave climates the situation might not always be as
explained above, but in order to develop a tool that is applicable to a large amount of test runs
this is the chosen approach.

Chapter 7
Validation of waves and wind
7.1
Wave climates
7.1.1
Target wave climates
In this section it is investigated to what extend the generated waves resembles the target wave
climates.
It quickly turned out that the wave generator had some diﬃculties generating the speciﬁed
wave climate. The main technique for adjustment of any dissatisfactory wave signals, is to
multiply the wave paddle signal by a constant factor, an ampliﬁcation factor. This leads to a
direct adjustment of the wave height, whereas the period and distribution of the energy calls for
the need for a more sophisticated adjustment of the wave signal, which was not implemented.
Accordingly the wave height is the primary frame of reference.
For the regular waves the wave height and period is compared to the target values. Similarly the
signiﬁcant wave height and peak period must be correct for the irregular waves, and moreover
the wave energy must be distributed as speciﬁed by the Pierson-Moskowitz wave spectrum.
The white noise signal is applicable if the wave energy is distributed evenly across the wave
frequency range from 0.5 Hz to 2 Hz. For the focused waves the maximum wave height is used
for comparison.
Table 7.1, 7.2 and 7.3 presents the deviation between target values and measured values for the
applied wave series. A positive value indicate a larger wave than prescribed and vice versa. All
measured wave height and wave periods are listed in appendix A.
The period for the regular waves with the direction of 0◦only varies in the order of 0.1% or less
from the target period, whereas the regular waves with the direction of 30◦varies in the order

The wave height of the regular waves are a little oﬀthe target values. However, prior to tuning
of the regular waves they were consistently around 30% higher than speciﬁed, and therefore
the obtained margin of error is acceptable.
Unfortunately an erroneous adjustment of the signiﬁcant wave height was conducted, because
the entire sampling period of 600 s formed the basis of the Hs estimation, which naturally re-
sults in a lower Hs, since a greater number of small waves is included in the analysis. Therefore
all measured unidirectional irregular waves are too large compared to the target. Furthermore
ﬁgure 7.3a and 7.3b clearly shows how the amount of energy is too high across all wave fre-
quencies. A window size of 2x1024 has carefully been chosen for the WAFO toolbox, such that
the wave spectra are readable, but at the same time no physical incidents are left/smeared out.
The white noise signal in ﬁgure 7.1c is not completely uniform in the speciﬁed frequency range
from 0.5 Hz to 2 Hz, but almost all energy is distributed in this range, and hence this signal is
suitable for determination of the system RAO.
The heights of the focused waves are also a bit diﬀerent from the target values, but nevertheless
within acceptable limits. Wave breaking, which is a highly non linear phenomena, occurred
frequently for the higher irregular and focused seastates and if the waves break ahead of wave
gauge 11, the monitored wave height and wave spectrum will not be as speciﬁed, and might
be be an contributory explanation for why the majority of the focused waves are smaller than
their target value.

heading
The steepness of the waves is a decisive parameter for linearity. With reservations for the dis-
crepancies between target and measured sea states, this steepness is assumed to be maintained
for the regular waves. The steepness of the irregular waves will vary due to the superposition
of the individual wave trains. This steepness has been determined from the maximum wave
height, H between two downcrossings and the period T as the time between two downcrossings.
This procedure ensures that the waves are independent. Under the assumption of dispersive
and deep water waves the steepness is computed from
S = H
L0
= 2πH
gT 2
(7.1)
where L0 is the deep water wave length. In ﬁgure 7.2 a probability exceedance plot of the wave
steepness of the white noise signal.
According to [32] linear wave theory is considered valid as long as H/L < 0.05. However this
criterion is only fulﬁlled up til the 70 % quantile for the white noise signal. A similar trend
is evident in sea state I01 to I08. Consequently linearity is not conclusive and the method
of adjusting the wave height by a constant factor is not a very solid solution, and could be a
contributory explanation for the diﬃculties of reaching the target values, in particular for the
focused waves.

7.1.2
Repeatability
To ensure that signiﬁcant diﬀerences between comparable tests are solely due to the parameters
being investigated, e.g. wind damping, mooring conﬁguration, any discrepancies between wave
identical wave climates are surveyed.
Additionally wave spectrum plots for irregular wave
series and time series plots of the regular and focused wave series are inspected for assessment
of repeatability.
Obviously wave gauge 11 cannot constitute as the reference gauge. Waves monitored by wave
gauge 9 will be compared for most of the cases, but for the spread and misaligned focused wave,
wave gauge 7 is more appropriate as reference, since, in particular the spread waves, will only
be sensed very little by wave gauge 9. The discrepancies in wave height and period is listed in
table 7.4, 7.5 and 7.6.
The period for the regular waves with the direction of 0◦does not vary more than 0.2 % from
the reference run and for the regular waves with the direction of 30◦the ﬁgure is 0.3%. The
wave generation system is fed with the exact same input signal, and consequently the variation
between identical wave climates is expected to be small. However as table 7.4 and 7.5 shows
there is a variation among identical runs.
Particularly the smaller seastates are quite sensitive to initial disturbances of the water, and if
the surface is not calm at the beginning of a test this will inﬂuence the readings. However by
only evaluating the last 465 second of a wave signal, this eﬀect has tried to be minimized.

The discrepancy between individual runs is clear from ﬁgure 7.4a and 7.4b. Figure 7.4b of the
focused wave train shows that the intended focused wave is not generated completely. In theory
the focused wave should be symmetric about the peak wave.
Taking these variations between identical runs into account, the discrepancies present between
the target sea states and some of the actual sea states is not that obscure, and a comparison
between the target sea states with a dataset for the wave climates collected at a later point of
time, might lead to diﬀerent discrepancies in table 7.1 and 7.2.
Concluding the discrepancies between equal sea states must be kept in mind when examining
the eﬀect of varying a given parameter.
7.2
Wind climates
The wind climate delivered from the wind generator, whos design and construction is described
in section 5.2, was measured to be able to deliver the right wind speeds for the model experi-
ments. The measurements were conducted from the wind measuring unit described in section
6.1, giving measurements in a plane approximately 0.5 m downwind from the rotor’s position in
a no wind condition. It is assumed that the wind ﬁeld does not develop signiﬁcantly over this
distance as it is small compared to the distance of 4.0 m from the rotor to the wind generator.
The wind speed was measured by means of TSI 8455 air velocity transducers borrowed from
DHI, at a range of frequency levels on the fans, to be able to correlate the frequency and the
mean wind speed. For details on the TSI probes, see section 6.1.2.
Before commencing the model tests, the wind ﬁeld was measured in a few discrete points, as only
a single air velocity probe was available. Based on these measurements, the wind generator
settings that were applied throughout the entire range of experiments, were deﬁned. These
measurements are documented in section 7.2.1.
Later, three additional probes were bought, giving the possibility for more thorough measure-
ments of the wind ﬁeld within a reasonable time frame. Theses measurements were conducted
in sweeps across the wind ﬁeld, and are documented in 7.2.2.
120
==PAGE:139==
CHAPTER 7. VALIDATION OF WAVES AND WIND
7.2.1
Point measurements
Having only a single air velocity probe, it was decided to measure in 16 points evenly distributed
across the wind generator, as sketched in ﬁgure 7.5. A sampling time of 30s was chosen to be
able to determine the turbulence intensity from the largest scales which are assumed to be the
same order of magnitude as the wind generator. Given a minimum wind speed in the order
of 1m/s, this sampling time corresponds to the passing of 1m/s·30s
4m
= 7.5 of the largest eddy
structures.
Wind generator
Sweep measurements
Point measurements
0.33 m
1.00 m
Rotor position for thrust
Rotor position for model
curve measurements
experiments
Figure 7.5: Location of wind point and sweep measurements and the wind turbine rotor relative
to the wind generator.
It was decided to base the overall mean wind speed and the overall turbulence intensity estimate
on the mean of the measurements in the circled points in ﬁgure 7.5, even though the rotor in
the model experiments was not centred in jet. For the model experiments the rotor was centred
approximately 2.0 m above the MWL, which corresponds to 1.35 m above the bottom of the
wind generator. These are shown in ﬁgure 7.6, in the legend denoted "point", for a range of fan
frequencies from 24 Hz to 47 Hz, which turned out to be the maximum frequency that did not
lead to overload on the fans. As it appears from this ﬁgure, the maximum obtainable mean
wind speed was 1.7 m/s, meaning that environmental states 6-10 could not be established with
the correctly scaled wind speeds. I was decided to apply the maximum wind speed at these
environmental states as it is shown in table 7.7.
In ﬁgure 7.6 it is further observed that the mean turbulence intensity is around 5%, for all wind
speeds, and that the mean wind speed seems to depend linearly on the fan frequency. A linear
correlation between fan frequency and mean wind speed was estimated:
U ≃0.035ffan + 0.058,
(7.2)
where U [m/s] is the mean wind speed, and ffan [Hz] is the fan frequency.This correlation was
used to deﬁne the frequency converter settings that were applied for the ten environmental
states for all the model experiments, and are given in table 7.7.
7.2.2
Sweep measurements
With four probes at hand, it was decided to measure while sweeping across the wind ﬁeld in 12
levels, as sketched by the grey horizontal lines in ﬁgure 7.5. In the ﬁrst sweep, the probes were
mounted in levels 1,4,7 and 10, in the second sweep in levels 2,5,8 and 11 and in the third sweep
in the remaining levels. In this manner, a maximum distance was kept between the probes,
such that the ﬂow disturbance caused by the presence of the probes, had a minimum eﬀect

on the measurements. The measurement unit was operated at a constant speed of 0.023 m/s
across the wind ﬁeld, corresponding to 40 RPM on the step motor pulling the measurement
unit. This speed was found to have an insigniﬁcant eﬀect on the measured wind speed in the
hot-sphere measurements that were part of the design proof study in the construction phase,
see section 5.2.3.
The mean wind speed and turbulence intensity in a point were then determined by a centred
window function, with a width of 15s corresponding to 0.34 m. The results are presented as
contour plots in ﬁgures 7.7 to 7.12 for the six fan frequency levels given in table 7.7. In these
plots the six units of the wind generator are indicated by gray lines, and position of the rotor
is sketched by a dot-dashed circle. The mean wind speed and mean turbulence intensity over
the rotor, U and TU, can be determined as
U = 1
A
Z
A
U dS,
TU = 1
A
Z
A
TU dS,
(7.3)
where A is the rotor area. As shown in ﬁgure 7.5, the measurements do not cover the entire rotor
area. It is is likely that the wind speed drops signiﬁcantly and the turbulence intensity increases
at the bottom of the rotor where measurements are missing. However, as the area outside the
measurement area is only ∼6% of the total rotor area, it was chosen to neglect this variation,
and in stead it was assumed that U and TU based on the area of the rotor that is covered by
measurements, are representative of the same quantities for the entire rotor. To evaluate U and
TU numerically, U and TU are ﬁrst interpolated to a ﬁnely meshed circular grid, covering the
rotor area. Next, the surface integrals (7.3) are approximated using the trapezoidal rule. The
values of U and TU are plotted in ﬁgure 7.6 together with the corresponding quantities from the
point measurements. It is evident that the two series of measurements show very equal results
both for the mean wind speed and the turbulence intensity. Hence, the sweep measurements
conﬁrm that the applied settings of the fan frequency give mean wind speeds over the rotor, as
speciﬁed in the environmental states, however, with the aforementioned upper limit of 1.7 m/s.
122
==PAGE:141==
CHAPTER 7. VALIDATION OF WAVES AND WIND
The contour plots in ﬁgures 7.7 to 7.12 show that for the a large part of the rotor area, the wind
ﬁeld is relatively uniform and low turbulent. As seen in ﬁgure 7.6, the mean turbulence intensity
is in the order of 5-6% while it can be observed in the contour plots that for the majority of the
rotor area, turbulence intensity is below 5%. The performance of the wind generator is in all
very satisfactory, given the compact design and the fact that the design phase did not include
any ﬂow simulations for optimization of the construction. It is observed that the uniformity
of the ﬂow over the rotor would be enhanced if the center of the jet was lowered to the rotor
center. However, this was not possible due to the presence of the wave maker. Further, it
is observed that the wind generator unit in the lower left corner, underperforms in the range
−2 < y < −1. Here the wind speed is remarkably low and the turbulence intensity is high for
all mean wind speeds. There are likely two explanations to this behaviour: One, is presence of
the measuring bridge as sketched in ﬁgure 6.1, which could have aﬀected and limited the inﬂow
to the fan. The other is that the guide vanes of this unit could have been installed at a larger
angle, directing the ﬂow to the center of the jet in stead of making it uniform. In addition an
area of remarkably higher wind speeds for all mean wind speeds is observed in the center left
unit.
These deviations from uniformity in the wind ﬁeld are likely to cause 3p frequencies in the
response of the WT model response, because for each of the blades passing the an area with
deviation from the more uniform part of the ﬂow, the rotor will be loaded diﬀerently.

7.3
Rotor Thrust
To validate the rotor design aerodynamically it must be shown that the aerodynamic eﬀects
on the rotor in model scale, resembles the eﬀects in full scale, when operated as the full scale
turbine. With the WT model design, it was possible to measure the shear force between the
tower and the nacelle, which under steady conditions is a good measure of the mean thrust. It
was not possible to measure the aerodynamic torque, and thereby to determine the mechanical
power of the rotor. However, as discussed in section 5.1, the most important parameter to
scale correctly is the aerodynamic thrust, which also makes it the most important parameter to
validate. The thrust was measured for a range of operational combinations of wind speed and
blade pitch angle, as speciﬁed for the DTU 10MW reference turbine, see section 4.2. However,
as described in section 7.2, it was not possible to obtain a wind speed above 1.7m/s. As some
uncertainty was expected on the blade pitch adjustment, it was decided to measure the thrust
at a range of blade pitch angles, θ, given as
θ = θDTU10MW + ∆θ,
∆θ = −2◦, −1◦, 0◦, 1◦, 2◦,
(7.4)
where θDTU10MW is given in section 4.2. For the thrust measurements, the WT model was
mounted in the measurement rail, raising the rotor approximately 0.25 m and moving it ap-
proximately 1.0 m downwind from its position in the model experiments. It was assumed that
the wind ﬁeld did not change signiﬁcantly over this distance, and therefore the correlation,
(7.2), between wind speed and fan frequency was used to set the wind speed. The thrust force
was logged for 30s, and the mean values are presented in ﬁgure 7.13. From the ﬁrst measure-
ments of the thrust which are shown in ﬁgure 7.13a, it was experienced that the thrust was
relatively sensitive to small corrections in the blade pitch angle.
For this reason it was chosen to mount a number of vortex generator inboard on the suction
side of the blades, see ﬁgure 5.18d, and the results from these measurements are shown in ﬁgure
7.13b. It is observed that up to rated wind speed, the thrust is less sensitive to a small change in
blade pitch angle, and for this reason it was chosen to apply the vortex generators throughout
the model experiments. The measurements shown in ﬁgure 7.13 and ﬁgure 7.14 show that the
model rotor design performs very equivalent to the full scale DTU 10MW reference turbine.
Due to play in the blade pitch mechanism mentioned in section 5.4, it was not possible to
re-establish the thrust curve with satisfactory accuracy, and therefore it was decided to adjust
the pitch until the right thrust was reached before each of the model experiments.

==PAGE:149==

8.1
Model static validation
When the FWT is loaded by a steady thrust, it takes a new equilibrium position dictated by
the magnitude of the thrust force. Further, the tensions in the upwind tendons increase and
the tensions in the downwind tendons decrease compared to the no-wind state to balance the
overturning moment of the wind. As none of the experiments were dedicated to measure dis-
placements and tensions in the equilibrium positions of the six wind conditions, these quantities
were determined based on the last twenty seconds of the focused wave tests. It is observed that
for this period of time (t = [40s, 60s]), the waves have passed the wind turbine, and the struc-
tural response has died out. The mean displacements in surge, heave and pitch and tendon
tensions for the three structures are plotted in ﬁgures 8.1 to 8.3 against measured values of the
mean thrust force. It is noted that the measurement for wind climate 1 with structure 1 is miss-
ing in ﬁgure 8.1. This measurement was left out due to a missing initialization of the Qualisys
tracking system for this test. The measurements are compared to results from the simple static
3DOF model, derived in section 3.1.8, evaluated based on model data. The ﬁgures both show
the results based on the measured tendon stiﬀness, as well as a reduced tendon stiﬀness that
accounts for the foundation ﬂexibility which is not included in the 3DOF model. The value of
the reduced tendon stiﬀness was chosen such that a match of simulated and measured pitch
natural frequency for structure 1 was obtained, see section 8.2. It is observed that the static
surge displacement of structures 1 and 3 are relatively well predicted by the simplistic static
3DOF model. The measured surge displacements for structure 2, however, are in the order of
25% below the displacement predicted by the 3DOF model.
For structures 1 and 3, the measured set-down in heave is signiﬁcantly smaller than the set-
down predicted by the numerical model. This is likely due to ﬂexibility in the spoke ﬁxture at
the bottom of the ﬂoater, which is not accounted for in the numerical model. For structure 2,
there is no trend in the heave set-down, and the variation within the measurements is within
the measurement uncertainty of the Qualisys system of 1mm as described in section 6.1.2.
This means that the measurements cannot be used to verify the static model in heave for this
structure.
The pitch angle is signiﬁcantly underestimated for structures 1 and 3 and signiﬁcantly overes-
timated for structure 2, when the measured tendon stiﬀness is applied. The reduced tendon
stiﬀness clearly enhances similarity of measured and predicted pitch angles.
Further, the measured heave for structure 3 and the measured pitch for structures 1 and 3 at
fT ≈4.5 N appears to deviate from the general trend of the measurements. This measurement
is from wind state 6, which is above rated wind speed, and this test was conducted after all
the tests at environmental states 1-5. The likely explanation to the deviation is that there has
133
==PAGE:152==
CHAPTER 8. SYSTEM IDENTIFICATION FOR FLOATER MOTION
been a small settlement in the upwind tendons in the order of a few millimetres. For structure
1, a settlement of only 0.6 mm will result in a diﬀerence of 0.1deg in the pitch angle. This may
also be the cause of the increasing trend for pitch for structure 2, which is in contrast to the
decreasing trend of the numerical model.
From ﬁgures 8.1d, 8.1e, 8.2d, 8.2e, 8.3d and 8.3e it is seen and that in the case of four tendons,
the tendons with the dominating tensions are positioned diagonally across the structure (1 and
3, and 2 and 4), see ﬁgure 6.4 for tendon numbers.
For structure 1 this is exempliﬁed by
observing that tendon 1 is the upwind tendon with the highest tension, and tendon 3 is the
downwind tendon with the highest tension, and that tendons 1 and 3 are placed across the
ﬂoater from each other. The bulk of the measured tendon tensions fall 1N-2N below the model
predicted tendon tensions, which indicates that the tendon tension measurements are either
biased by their calibration, or that the buoyancy force is overestimated or that the FWT mass
is overestimated in the model.
When analysing heave, roll and pitch displacements, the settlements shown in these ﬁgures
which are likely related to the mooring system must be taken into account as an error of
the experimental setup. As a guideline the settlement in heave is in the order 1mm, while
it is 0.1deg-0.2deg in pitch. This error could have been reduced, if the mooring system was
adjusted before each test and the Qualisys system initialized. However, this would be very time
consuming and would have resulted in a signiﬁcantly reduced number of conducted tests.

8.2
Model Decay Tests
To facilitate the identiﬁcation of natural frequencies in the model response, both an isolated
tower decay test and a full model decay tests for the three mooring conﬁgurations were carried
out. For the isolated tower decay test, the tower with the RNA was detached from the ﬂoater
and fastened to a plate on the ﬂoor. The tower was pitched in the surge and the sway directions
respectively. The response was measured by the accelerometers in the nacelle and is shown in
ﬁgure 8.4. From the frequency plot in this ﬁgure, the natural frequencies of the isolated tower in
the pitch and surge directions were identiﬁed and tabulated in table 8.1 together with the linear
damping ratios. The damping ratios were estimated by determining the logarithmic decrement
as deﬁned in [39, p. 66]
δ = ln xi
xi+1
(8.1)
where xi is the i’th peak response, between subsequent peaks both in the negative and positive
directions. A ﬁrst estimate of the linear damping, was then given based on the mean of the
logarithmic decrements between peaks, δ.
Assuming that the system is linear, the viscous
damping ratio can be estimated by
ζ =
δ
q
4π2 + δ
2
(8.2)
To evaluate this ﬁrst estimate, the decaying amplitude, Alin, of the corresponding linear single
degree of freedom underdamped response
Alin =
s
( ˙x0 + ζωnx0)2 + (x0ωd)2
ω2
d
e−ζωn(t−t0),
(8.3)
was plotted together with the measured response as shown in ﬁgure 8.4a. Based on this plot,
corrections were made to enhance the ﬁt, resulting in the ﬁnal estimate of the damping ratio.
Here ωn and ωd are the undamped and damped natural frequencies respectively, related by
ωd = ωn
q
1 −ζ2
(8.4)
while x0 and ˙x0 are initial conditions given at t = t0. Figure 8.4a shows Alin for the ﬁnal
estimate of ζ in grey, with a relatively good match between the linear damping and the measured
acceleration.
It is observed that the tower natural frequency in the surge and sway directions diﬀer signif-
icantly. This cannot be explained by the diﬀerence in moment of inertia of the nacelle for
rotation about the x-axis and the y-axis, respectively, and the diﬀerence in stiﬀness is likely
due to material inhomogeneity or the presence of residual stresses within the tower tube.
Model decay tests were carried out for ﬁve of the six degrees of freedom for the model, leaving
out heave, because it was considered impossible to give the model an initial isolated heave
displacement while avoiding slack tendons. For the remaining degrees of freedom model decay
tests were carried out without wind and while operating the WT at rated wind speed.
In
each test it was attempted to give the model an isolated displacement in the tested degree of
freedom and the response was measured in the Qualisys system. For surge and sway a piece

of heavy-duty ﬁshing-line was tied to the point of ﬂotation of the ﬂoater and used to displace
the model approximately 0.11m from the equilibrium position, which for the rated wind speed
conditions is 0.1 m downwind from the no-wind condition for structures 1 and 3 and 0.015 m
for structure 2, see section 8.1. For roll and pitch an additional piece of ﬁshing-line was tied
to the top of the tower and the two lines were pulled in opposite directions. For the lines
being parallel to the y-axis this initiated roll motion, while pitch was initiated by the lines
being parallel to the x-axis. The lines were pulled such that the point of ﬂotation was kept
in the equilibrium position, and such that the model was rotated approximately 1-1.5 degrees.
Subsequently, the lines were let go simultaneously. For the yaw decay test the model was rotated
approximately 10-15 degrees by hand while keeping the model in the equilibrium position from
the wind generator. The damped natural frequencies and damping ratios were approximated
as for the isolated tower decay test, and the results are tabulated in table 8.2. It turned out
not to be possible to determine natural frequencies and damping ratios for roll, pitch and yaw
at rated wind speed as these modes turned out to be either overdamped or too contaminated
by rotor noise.
Along with the measured values, the natural frequencies, determined from
WAMIT simulations with model scale data as input, are shown in brackets in the table. The
ﬁrst number within the brackets is based on the measured tendon stiﬀness of EAt = 8 · 104N,
while the second number is based on a reduced stiﬀness of EAt = 1.5 · 104N, which gives the
right pitch natural frequency for structure 1. In WAMIT the foundation and WT are modelled
as rigid bodies, and the only elasticity is found in the tendons. Hence, the discrepancy in pitch
natural frequency is attributed to the ﬂexibility of the scale model foundation and tower. To
tune the WAMIT model, the stiﬀness of the tendons was decreased to match the pitch natural
frequency for structure 1. The surge, sway and yaw natural frequencies for structure 3 are
higher than those of structure 1, even though the two structures are very similar. This increase
is a consequence of structure 3 having a smaller mass than structure 1 (see table 5.4), which
apart from the eﬀect of a decreased mass in itself, also increases the tendon tension and thereby
the stiﬀness in surge, sway and yaw. The same diﬀerence is observed in the WAMIT based
calculations of the surge natural frequencies.
Mooring conﬁguration 2 has a slightly higher stiﬀness in surge, sway and yaw than conﬁgu-
rations 1 and 3, which can be attributed to the tendons having a component in the x and
y-directions as a consequence of their inclination. An increase in surge natural frequency is also
observed for the WAMIT simulations, although the same value is not obtained.
In addition, the inclination of tendons is observed to decrease the natural frequency in roll

and pitch, when comparing structures 1 and 2. This can be understood by considering the
extreme of inclined tendons, where the anchor points are in the same plane as the spokes. In
this case the structure would be very stiﬀin surge, sway and yaw, while it would be softer in
heave, roll and pitch, which is the exact opposite of the vertical tendon case. The WAMIT
simulations show a similar decrease in pitch natural frequency for structure 2. For structure 3
the measurements show a slight decrease in the pitch natural frequency, while the roll natural
frequency is maintained compared to structure 1. In the WAMIT results signiﬁcantly larger
decreases in pitch natural frequencies are obtained, meaning that the pitch stiﬀness in the
mooring system is signiﬁcantly reduced when the number of tendons is reduced to three. The
reason that the same eﬀect is not observed experimentally, is that a signiﬁcant part of the
ﬂexibility of the foundation and tower, which is unchanged for structures 1 and 3.
For the three mooring conﬁgurations the surge and sway natural frequencies are equal in the
case of no wind, and in the case of rated wind, the sway natural frequency for structures 2
and 3 are slightly lower than the surge natural frequencies. In addition, it is observed that the
surge and sway natural frequencies are either unaﬀected or decrease slightly when the WT is
subjected to rated wind speed.
The damping of a mechanical system is often one of the most diﬃcult parameters to estimate,
which was also the case for the present model. For this reason the damping ratios are only
given with one signiﬁcant digit in table 8.2.
All three conﬁgurations are equally damped in surge and sway in the no wind case. Conﬁgu-
rations 1 and 3 show signiﬁcantly increased damping in surge at rated wind speed, due to the
aerodynamic damping of the rotor, whereas for conﬁguration 2 the damping in surge is only
slightly increased. This can be explained by the rotor of conﬁguration 2 being relatively steady
even though the foundation moves in surge, while for conﬁgurations 1 and 3, the rotor and
140
==PAGE:159==
CHAPTER 8. SYSTEM IDENTIFICATION FOR FLOATER MOTION
foundation move in parallel.
Further, it is observed that the yaw motion of all three structures is quite heavily damped.
8.3
Response Amplitude Operators
In addition to the determination of the natural frequencies of the model, an investigation of the
Response Amplitude Operators (RAO)’s of the diﬀerent structures, reveal useful information
about the motion of the model.
The RAO relates the steady state amplitude of the response of the model to the amplitude
of the incident wave in the frequency domain. The RAO is based on an assumption of linear
wave theory and a linear relation between the wave amplitude and the response amplitude.
Consequently the only frequencies present in the motions of the model should be identical to
the frequencies in the incoming wave train.
The RAO can be extracted from the regular wave climates simply by dividing the amplitude
of the motion by the amplitude of the incident wave, and in its simplest form the RAO is
expressed as [37][p. 308]
RAO = ξi
η
, i = 1, 6
(8.5)
However a regular wave can only excite the model at a single frequency, and hence establish
only a single point on the RAO curve. The irregular waves series on the other hand excite the
model at a whole range of frequencies, and a comprehensive RAO curve can be extracted from
such an irregular wave climate. For the irregular wave series it is more reasonable to consider
the energy spectrum for the response,Sξi and the waves,Sη , which are deﬁned as, [32]
Sξi(f)df = 1/2ξ2
i (f)
(8.6)
Sη(f)df = 1/2η2(f)
(8.7)
Dividing (8.6) by (8.7) disclose how the response and wave spectrum are related by the square
of the RAO:
Sξi(f) =
ξi
η
2
Sη(f)
, i = 1, 6
(8.8)
Sξi(f) = RAO2Sη(f)
, i = 1, 6
(8.9)
and the RAO originates as:
RAO(f) =
s
Sξi(f)
Sη(f)
, i = 1, 6
(8.10)
Ideally a wave signal with waves of the exact same amplitude, but with diﬀerent wave periods,
should form the basis for determination of the RAO, since the wave energy would be equal
across all frequencies. Hence the RAO can be computed directly from the response spectrum
by dividing with a constant.
However this uniformity is violated for the actual white noise signal, see ﬁgure 7.1c, and thus
it is necessary to determine the response spectrum as well as the wave spectrum. A closer look
at ﬁgure 7.1c reveals that the wave energy decreases rapidly below 0.6 Hz and from 1.5 - 2 Hz
a signiﬁcant drop in the wave energy occurs as well. Ideally this should not be a concern, since
the corresponding response at the wave frequencies with lower wave energy would be smaller,
under the assumption of linear waves. However as it was identiﬁed in ﬁgure 7.2 the linearity
limit of H/L < 0.05 is exceeded for 30 % of the incident waves in the white noise signal, and
consequently linearity is not conclusive. Therefore the response at a given frequency might not
solely be related to a wave with that particular frequency, and attention must be given to peaks
141
==PAGE:160==
CHAPTER 8. SYSTEM IDENTIFICATION FOR FLOATER MOTION
a drops in the RAO which could be explained by a dip or peak in the wave energy.
The RAO curves determined from the white noise signal is sharply cut of at the borders of 0.5
Hz and 2 Hz, since the low amount of energy outside this range would most likely distort the
RAO curve. In the post processing of the results from the white noise run, it quickly became
evident that the drop in wave energy around 0.5 Hz, results in a lack of the understanding of
the dynamics of the model, especially in surge. Therefore the severest sea state (SS 8) also
forms the basis for a RAO curve for the surge motion. This signal has been extended to 0.4
Hz.
Despite only having a total number of regular RAO points of ten, the RAO computed from the
regular wave series serves as a strong comparison tool for the RAO’s due to its simple way of
being computed,
It turned out that the response for the sway, heave and roll in the regular wave series did
not consist of one dominating frequency (the wave frequency), complicating the process of
determining the RAO. This is highlighted in ﬁgure 8.5, which shows a sample of the response of
structure 1 in sea state 5 with regular waves and the corresponding response energy spectrum.
The corresponding surface elevation and wave energy spectrum is shown in ﬁgure 8.5. The
wave frequency is very distinct in the energy spectrum plot, but a higher harmonic at twice
the primary wave frequency is also present. Returning to the associated motions of the model
in ﬁgure 8.5, the identiﬁed peaks of the response, forming the basis for the RAO-points, are
marked by spheres. It is evident that not every single peak in the response signals are marked.
This is due to the algorithm used for identiﬁcation of the peaks, which seek to only include the
motions which is related to the surface elevation.

Figure 8.5: Response of structure 1 in sea state 5 for regular waves with Θ = 0◦and no wind
It is apparent that the surge, pitch and yaw motion of the model is comparable to the wave
elevation, and the energy spectra also show that the wave frequency of 1.06 Hz is represented
in these modes of motion. On the contrary the sway and roll motion has clear presence of the
higher harmonic at 2.12 Hz. Moreover the natural frequency for sway at 0.19 Hz is excited
in the sway mode. As a consequence of this mix of frequencies the time series of the sway,
heave and roll motion do not look regular and it becomes complicated to determine an average
amplitude of these responses.
Given that the RAO is determined from unidirectional waves with 0◦heading, only surge, heave
and pitch should in theory be present, since any cross coupling eﬀects only exists between surge
and pitch or sway and roll, due to symmetry of the model. However as ﬁgure 8.5 clearly shows,
the sway, roll and yaw motions are also excited. From this ﬁgure it can be derived that the
magnitude of the sway motion is within 10% of the surge motion, whereas the roll motion is
144
==PAGE:163==
CHAPTER 8. SYSTEM IDENTIFICATION FOR FLOATER MOTION
actually half of that in the pitch mode. This is an indication of a motion where the turbine is
gyrating in a elliptic pattern, which of is not the intention, but it is very reasonable to assume
that even the slightest asymmetry in the model might induce such a motion along with the yaw
motion.
Even though much eﬀort was put into leveling the tendon tension, a discrepancy in the order of
2N could not be avoided. Moreover the anchor platform potentially has a slight misalignment,
since the alignment of the platform was conducted from the bridge, and due to lack of markings
at the bottom of the wave basin, a misalignment of a couple of degrees is not unrealistic. The
cables from the accelerometers, strain gauge and the servo motors also induces some asymmetry,
since the cables were run downwind to the starboard side of the model. Again great care was
taken in order to reduce the impact of the cabling, for instance by supporting the cables as
close as possible to the model, but completely eliminating its eﬀects was impossible.
The cabling to the Z-gauges was run directly from the model to the bridge trough the water. It
was assured that the cables were completely free of any tension, however the weight from the
cables themselves might have tug the model slightly towards the bridge. The deviation from
uniformity in the wind ﬁeld will also excite the sway, roll and yaw motion.
The distortion of the motion of the model from the Z-gauges and wiring could have been
investigated by running tests without monitoring the tendon tension and the acceleration and
disconnecting the servo motors.
Besides the regular response in sea state 5 the surge, pitch and yaw response also appear regular
for all sea states throughout all conﬁgurations and at all regular sea states. As a consequence
of the lack of regularity in the remaining modes, the RAO’s computed from the regular waves
for sway, heave and roll are left out of the analysis.
In ﬁgure 8.6 to 8.8 the RAO curves for the three structure are shown for all modes of motion.

A ﬁrst observation from ﬁgure 8.6a, 8.7a and 8.8a is the ﬁne agreement between the RAO based
148
==PAGE:167==
CHAPTER 8. SYSTEM IDENTIFICATION FOR FLOATER MOTION
on the white noise signal and sea state 8.
Common to all three structures is the behavior in surge. The RAO implies that longer waves
(f →0) induces a relatively larger surge displacement of the model.
If the curves were allowed to continue towards 0 Hz, the response curve would grow towards the
surge natural frequency of 0.2 Hz, whereupon the RAO would drop towards zero. However due
to the absence of wave energy below 0.5 Hz, the corresponding RAO curve in this frequency
range cannot be considered as genuine.
A consistent drop in the surge RAO for all three conﬁgurations at the longest regular wave, is
a clear indicator of a physical limitation of the structure. When a very long wave strikes the
model, the model is displaced in the positive x-direction, and at a certain point the tendons will
pull the ﬂoater downwards, such that the model is further submerged. The fact that the ﬂoater
for large surge displacement is restricted to this pendulum motion, combined with an increased
buoyancy of the ﬂoater and the transition piece, entails this physical limitation of the surge
displacement, independent of tendon conﬁguration. Whether this phenomena emerge is entirely
related to the amplitude of the incident waves. Hence if the waves are very small in amplitude
but very long, this gives rise to a RAO curve with large magnitudes near the surge natural
frequency, while large amplitude waves triggers the limitation from the tendons. Hence the
behavior of the RAO curve will look quite diﬀerent as already indicated by the largest regular
wave, but in this case the validity of the assumptions of linear behavior between structure and
waves is in fact violated.
The surge RAO for structure 2 appears to be completely unaﬀected by the pitch motion, while
the RAOs for structure 1 and 3 have small smooth bumps at the pitch natural frequency.
The nature of the sway motion for all three structures is very similar to the surge motion,
though the RAO is in the order of 10% smaller than the surge response, which was also con-
cluded from the time series in ﬁgure 8.5. Hence longer waves leads to a larger displacement in
the sway direction.
The sway motion is considered as secondary motion since ideally the model should not be ex-
cited in sway for a unidirectional wave with 0◦heading. Hence the response of the sway motion
is to a less extent directly coupled to the wave motion and thus the actual distribution of energy
in the white noise signal has a large impact on the behavior of the sway RAO curve. Therefore
a very reasonable explanation for the increase in the magnitude of the sway RAO curve is the
low amount of wave energy at the boundaries of the frequency span considered.
The sway motion of structure 1 and to some extend structure 2 appears to be aﬀected by the
yaw motion, indicated by peaks near the yaw natural frequency.
As expected the heave motion is very conﬁned for all three structures, which is evident from
the magnitude of the RAO. In fact the response in the heave mode is within the uncertainties
of the Qualisys system and consequently the RAO curves might not be considered as fully re-
liable. Nevertheless it can be deduced that the heave motion increases as f tends towards zero
as for the surge and sway motion. As described in section 4.4.2 the heave natural frequency is
expected to be around 10 Hz and consequently the heave frequency will not be evident from
the RAO curves. The roll/pitch natural frequency is present in the heave signal. Peaks in the
RAO curve are present at around f = 1.0Hz and 1.25Hz for all three structures, but these peaks
are most likely due to a drop in the wave energy at these frequencies, see ﬁgure 7.1c.
The same conclusions that were drawn from the sway RAO plots apply to the roll RAO curves.
However the magnitude of the roll motion, especially for structure 1 and 3, is in the same order
as the pitch motion, which signify that the asymmetry in the setup will have quite a huge
impact on the motion of the nacelle.
149
==PAGE:168==
CHAPTER 8. SYSTEM IDENTIFICATION FOR FLOATER MOTION
The pitch RAO of structure 1 is fairly stable for waves with energy that is not near the pitch
natural frequency. The coherence between the RAO determined from the white noise single and
from the regular waves is decent. The RAO from the regular waves indicates a similar physical
limitation of the pitch motion for long and large amplitude waves as for surge, which is in good
agreement with the reasoning laid out in the discussion of the surge ROAs. Similar ﬁndings
appear for structure 3 in the lower range of the frequency bands. However the RAO points
from the regular waves are poorly representing the response from the white noise signal, which
might be due to the small pitch angles which is almost within the inaccuracy of the Qualisys
system.
No model frequencies should give any rise to the scattering of the RAO points between 1 and
1.5 Hz. Peaks in the RAO curves for structure 1 and 3 around f = 1.0Hz and 1.25Hz are as for
heave most likely due to a drop in wave energy.
The pitch natural frequency is very distinct for conﬁguration 1 and 3 at 1.9 and 1.8, respec-
tively. An interesting observation is that the peak at the pitch natural frequency is more distinct
for structure 1 compared to structure 3 which has a more smooth peak with a smaller peak
magnitude. This indicates that the pitch motion is less damped for structure 1, which is in
accordance with the diﬀerence in damping for the two structures listed in table 8.2
Not surprisingly the inclination of the tendons completely changes the motion in pitch for struc-
ture 2. In stead of a displacement of the nacelle in relation to the ﬂoater, the pitch motion
is now a measure of the displacement of the ﬂoater since the nacelle is held relatively ﬁxed.
Therefore the pitch RAO curve for structure 2 has a pattern very similar to the surge RAO,
and the same response-limitation for large amplitude waves are present from the largest regular
wave. Also for this structure the coherence between the RAO curve and the RAO points is ﬁne.
Zooming in on the pitch natural frequency, which is due to the deformation of the tendons and
not the pendulum motion, it is clear that it is damped considerably compared to structure 1,
and this is ﬁne accordance with the damping ratios in table 8.2.
From the yaw RAO for all structures it is clear that the yaw motion is very sensitive to the
frequency of the incident wave. In particular structure 1 and 3 has a signiﬁcant increase in yaw
motion near the yaw natural frequency. The inclination of the tendons signiﬁcantly reduces
the yaw motion, which can be attributed to the horizontal tendon tension component which is
initially present. On the opposite a certain deﬂection in the yaw motion is necessary in order to
restrict the yaw motion for structure 1 and 3. A comparison of the RAO curves and the RAO
points for structure 1 and 3 reveals that the physical tendons restricts the yaw deﬂection, as for
the surge and pitch motion. The coherence between the RAO curve and the RAO points for
yaw for structure 2 is rather poor, but this could be due to the smaller yaw motion of structure
2, which is more inﬂuenced by the uncertainty of the Qualisys system.
150
==PAGE:169==
Chapter 9
Results
9.1
Behaviour in Irregular Waves
In this section the response of the model in the eight irregular wave climates without wind is
investigated to identify how the response changes as the wave height becomes larger.
Empirical exceedance probability plots enables the presentation of a large amount of data in a
concise and very applicable manner. The exceedance probability P is deﬁned as
P = p(X ≥xi) = 1 −i −1
N
(9.1)
where xi is the i’th peak response sorted in increasing order and N is the number of peaks. The
exceedance probability is presented on a logarithmic scale, such that the extreme events in the
response are magniﬁed.
To ensure that the peaks are not correlated, a downcrossing analysis was conducted, as de-
scribed in section 6.3. For each independent wave the maximum displacement, acceleration
and maximum and minimum tendon tension is determined and the exceedance probability for
the entire wave series is computed.
First the wave height of every independent wave is presented in ﬁgure 9.1. From this ﬁgure it
is clear how the smaller sea (I01 - I05) are not that diﬀerent when it comes to the distribution
in wave heights, which agrees with the aim of having a better resolution of sea states near the
rated wind speed. For sea state (I06 - I08), the wave heights increases signiﬁcantly, reaching
as high as 0.25m in seastate I08. This corresponds to 80 % of the maximum wave height in a
three hour realisation and 56 % of the maximum wave in the 50 years design sea state.
The legend in ﬁgure 9.1 is valid for the plots throughout this section.
Structure 1
The corresponding response to the waves presented in ﬁgure 9.1 for structure 1
is presented in ﬁgure 9.2.
The magnitude of the surge motion is closely linked to the sea state, and it appears that the
individual surge curves are roughly ampliﬁcations of sea state 1. The surge RAO in ﬁgure
8.6a shows that as the waves grow in size (decrease in peak frequency) the RAO increases.
Consequently the surge response for the largest sea states is larger in relation to the wave
height of the incident wave compared to the smaller sea states.
Figure 9.2b shows that sway motion is an order below the surge motion, which is in agreement
with the ﬁndings from section 8.3.
There is clear tendency towards larger sway motion as
the sea state grows, but the probability curves are not as organized as for the surge motion,
especially for the smaller sea states. This behavior is not surprising since the sway motion
for the long-crested waves with 0 heading angle is due to undesired asymmetry in model, the
mooring setup and/or the incoming waves
To some extent the heave motion increases as the sea states grow, see ﬁgure 9.2a. In the light
of the uncertainties of the Qualisys system which is nearly in the order of the maximum heave
151
