Figure 9.1: Distribution of wave heights in the irregular sea states 1 - 8 for Θ = 0◦
displacement for the largest sea states, no solid conclusions can really be drawn, but one thing
to notice is how the diﬀerence in heave for the smallest and largest sea state actually is quite
limited, which is an essential feature of the TLP concept.
The rolling of the model is closely related to the sway motion, and generally it increases with
growing sea state, but also in this case the rolling motion is caused by asymmetry, see ﬁgure9.2d.
The pitch motion in ﬁgure 9.2e also has the same pattern as for surge, but in considerations
of the RAO for pitch, which is relatively constant in the frequency range of which the peak
periods exist, in fact declining as the frequency drops, this is an indicator of the pitch motion
being primarily dependent on the height of the incident wave.
The yaw motion is particular distinct for the larger seastate, which is most likely attributed to
the fact that the yaw natural frequency of 0.63 Hz is relatively close to the peak frequencies of
sea state 6, 7 and 8. The remaining curves are quite steep indicating that the yaw motion is
less sensitive to the wave height of the incoming wave.
In ﬁgure 9.3 the acceleration of the nacelle is presented.
The plots of the acceleration for
structure 1 primarily serve as reference for structure 2, since the acceleration of the nacelle for
structure 1 is expected to be closely linked to the surge and sway motion, given the relatively
small pitch and roll angles and limited deﬂection of the tower. The nacelle acceleration in x
conﬁrms the linkage between the surge motion and the nacelle motion. The acceleration in the
y-direction on the other hand is more disrupted due to the more uncorrelated motion in sway.
The tendon tension is ﬁnally considered. For structure 1 and 2 the tension in tendon 2 and
4 serves as the point of reference. These tendons has been chosen given that the waves with
a heading angle of 30◦most likely will cause the greatest variation in the tension for these
particular tendons, which will be analysed in section 9.4. Hence the minimum and maximum
tension in both tendons is extracted, and ﬁgure 9.4a and 9.4b shows the normalized tendon
tension for tendon 2 and 4, respectively.
To maintain consistency this approach is used independent of wave climate and wave heading
angle.
A comparison of the individual tendon tension across the sea states shows an expected increase
in the maximum tendon tension and a decrease in the minimum tendon tension as the sea states
grow due to greater motion in particular surge and pitch.
For sea state I01-I05 the maximum and minimum values of tension in tendon 2 and 4 acts very
similarly. This indicates a relatively symmetric motion around the point of ﬂotation for pitch
152
==PAGE:171==
CHAPTER 9. RESULTS
and roll at these seastate. As the sea state increases in severity tendon 4 generally experiencing
larger tension. Concurrently the tension in tendon 2 becomes smaller compared to tendon 4
and for sea state 8 about 10% of the waves causes a tension in tendon 2 below half of the initial
tendon tension. In comparison roughly 1 % of the waves causes a similar decrease in the tension
for tendon 4.
Structure 2
Next structure 2 is investigated. Figure 9.5 presents all the modes of motion
for structure 2. The inclination of the tendons has insigniﬁcant impact on the surge motion,
which is very similar to the motion of structure 1, except in seastate 8, where more extreme
surge motion is present for structure 2.
In sway a small reduction is observed for structure 2.
The heave motion also seems to be relatively unaﬀected by the tendon conﬁguration, where the
diﬀerence between structure 1 and 2 nearly is within the inaccuracy of the Qualisys system.
The roll motion is more pronounced for structure 2, especially for the largest sea states. Given
that the sway motion and roll motion is also excited for this structure indicates the presence
of asymmetry in the setup, but the asymmetry excites the rolling motion of structure 2 to a
greater extent.
The pitch motion of structure 2 is signiﬁcantly larger due to the inclination of the tendons, and
tends to grow more as the wave height increases. This is in agreement with the pitch RAO
for structure 2, which increases as the wave frequency decreases, opposed to the relatively ﬂat
pitch RAO for structure 1 in the range of wave frequencies for the irregular wave climates. For
sea state I01 - I05 the pitching angle is approximately twice as large for structure 2, and almost
3 times larger for structure 2 at sea state I08.
By inclining the tendons the yaw motion clearly becomes restricted for large sea states. For sea
state 1-5 the diﬀerences is not that pronounced, but for sea state 6, 7 and 8 the yaw motion is
substantially limited.
In ﬁgure 9.6 the acceleration of the nacelle is presented. Clearly the nacelle of structure 2
undergoes less motion compared to structure 1. This limited accelerations of the nacelle in
the x-direction is caused by the pendulum motion of the model, which keeps the nacelle more
stable.
The nacelle acceleration in the y-direction is also more limited for structure 2, even though it
is by much less compared to the acceleration in the x-direction. An interesting tendency for
structure 2 is that the acceleration in y is in fact lowest for sea state 7 and second lowest for
sea state 8, despite of an increased roll motion and sway motion of the model at these two sea
states.
The maximum tendon tension in tendon 2 and 4 is presented in ﬁgure 9.7.
A signiﬁcant drop in the minimum and maximum values is observed for the tendon tension
of structure 2 compared to structure 1. Moreover tendon 2 and 4 appears to have very equal
behavior, which was not the case for structure 1. Opposed to structure 1, the tension in tendon
4 for structure 2 has the lowest values, though it is only by a small factor.
Structure 3
Structure 3 shares much of its characteristic with structure 1 with respect to the
six modes of motion and the acceleration of the nacelle, and therefore the associated probability
plots are left in appendix B.1. Due to the diﬀerent orientation of the spokes for structure 3 the
tension is presented for all three tendons. The tension in the two downwind is expected to be
quite similar, but for consistency they are both included in ﬁgure 9.8.
As ﬁgure 9.8c shows the single upwind tendon is put on signiﬁcant stress and at the same time
this tendon also experiences very low tension at other instances. This severe loading of tendon
1 in structure 3 will have a crucial inﬂuence on the fatigue life of the tendons and the risk of
snap loads. The tension pattern for the two downwind tendons looks quite similar, in particular
for the maximum tensions, whereas tendon 2 has more extreme peaks for the minimum tension
153
==PAGE:172==
CHAPTER 9. RESULTS
for sea state 7 and 8.
The maximum tension in the downwind tendons of structure 3 is smaller than for the downwind
tendon for structure 1, at least for the three most severe sea states.
9.1.1
Displacement of wing tip
One reasonable consideration that arise from the preliminary analysis is how the complete
change in nacelle motion for structure 2 aﬀects the motion of the blades.
A short analysis of the total displacement of the tip of the blade was conducted for sea state 10
with regular waves with no wind for structure 1 and 2. Disregarding the deﬂection of the tower
and the blade itself the displacement of the blade tip was based solely on the displacement in
x and rotation of the nacelle.
Assuming the blade as being in its "worse position" meaning pointing directly upwards for
structure 1 and directly downwards for structure 2, it was discovered that the largest blade tip
displacement for structure 2 is 0.17m, while it is 0.24m for structure 1. Hence a deviation of
34 %.
Structure 1
Structure 2
s
Figure 9.9: Maximum displacement of wingtip for structure 1 and 2
In this section it has been proven that the surge and pitch motion, the nacelle acceleration in
x and the variation in tendon tension for all three structures increases as the wave height and
wave period increases. The sway, heave, roll and yaw motion and nacelle acceleration in y are
not correlated to the size of the wave in a similar consistent manner, but generally the trend is
an increase in response as the waves increase in size.
Furthermore this analyses has shown that no unexpected phenomena are excited for unidirec-
tional irregular sea with a wave heading of 0◦with no wind.

CHAPTER 9. RESULTS
9.2
Eﬀect of Wind
In this sections the eﬀects of wind and the related operation of the wind turbine are investi-
gated. The response of structure 1 in regular waves and the response in irregular for structure
1 and 2 is presented, whereas the motion of structure 3 is included in the appendix.
Sea state 5 forms the basis for the analysis of wind eﬀects since the thrust force is at a maxi-
mum at this sea state, and therefore the impact from the wind damping is expected to be more
pronounced at this sea state.
9.2.1
Behaviour in Regular Waves
Structure 1
At ﬁrst the motion, the nacelle acceleration and the tension in tendon 2 and
4 is presented for the regular sea state 5. A snapshot of ﬁve seconds is considered, roughly
corresponding to ﬁve wave periods. The plots are simple, but they help to get an quick under-
standing of the eﬀect of wind.
For the modes of motion, focus will be given to the surge, pitch and yaw. The sway, heave and
roll motion is behaving in a more chaotic manner and will be further treated when considering
irregular waves, but the plots are included for consistency. At rated wind speed the thrust force
induces a permanent displacement of the model of 0.1 m, but the presence of wind does not
seem to aﬀect the amplitude of the oscillations in surge.
An oﬀset of the pitch motion of around 0.3◦is also present due to the thrust force.
The yaw motion becomes much more pronounced once wind is applied and the rotor spins.
Six peaks are present in the yaw signal with wind applied, approximately corresponding to a
frequency of 1.2 Hz, equivalent to the 1P frequency. Moreover an oﬀset in yaw of around 0.6◦
arise with wind.
The nacelle acceleration in x does not change signiﬁcantly, but the signal is more corrupted by
frequencies not related to the primary motion of the model caused by the regular wave.
In the y direction the acceleration increases signiﬁcantly once wind is applied. 18 peak are
present in the acceleration signal corresponding to a frequency of 3.6 Hz, which points towards
a large nacelle excitation in the y-direction caused by the 3P frequency.
In the upwind tendon 2, the tension and hence the restoring force is increased roughly 30 %
due to the overturning moment generated by the thrust force. In contrary the loading of the
downwind tendon 4 is reduced around 40 %.

9.2.2
Behaviour in Irregular Waves
In the results for the irregular waves the mean value of the response has been subtracted from
the results to ease the identiﬁcation of discrepancies in the results due to the presence of wind
and not due to diﬀerences in the equilibrium positions, which has been covered in 8.1
For waves with no wind the symmetry of the model implies that the roll and sway motion of
the model should be symmetric as well. However as discussed in 8.3, this is in fact not the
case. Once wind is applied this eﬀect will become more signiﬁcant due to the rotating blades
combined with imperfection in the wind ﬂow. For this reason the peak values of the roll motion
to either side of x axis will be included in this analysis, in order to better identify any changes
in the response.
Structure 1
In ﬁgure 9.13 the six modes of motion is presented as exceedance probability
plots. Throughout this section the black curves represents the response of the model with no
wind present and the blue curve represents the response of the model at rated wind speed. From
this ﬁgure it is evident that the surge motion is only slightly reduced when wind is applied.
The sway motion on the other hand is increased about 50 % when wind is applied. This is
increase is very likely due to rotor imbalances or a skewness in the wind ﬁeld.
Rotor imbalances is probably also the reason why the heave motion increases
Taken the increase in sway motion into account it might come as a surprise that the roll motion
is generally reduced due to the wind, since the damping eﬀect of wind is expected to primarily
have a damping eﬀect in the direction of the wind. Additionally the wind and/or the rotation
of the rotor causes a growing diﬀerence in rolling, such that negative rolling becomes more
distinct. For P<1% the negative rolling becomes even larger with wind.
The largest impact of wind damping is evident in the pitch response, where the pitch motion
is consistently lower for the test with wind. Figure 9.13e indicates that the damping has a
greater eﬀect on the large motions motions, leading to a pitch curve which drops more quickly
compared to the no wind pitch curve. The most extreme pitch angles are decreased by around
40 %.
The yaw motion is signiﬁcantly increased with wind applied. Prior to wave generation it was
observed that the model was yawing solely due to the presence of wind. An explanation for this
could be rotor imbalances and deviation from uniformity in the ﬂow. When the model rolls the
thrust force will give rise to a moment about the z-axis and this could potentially increase the
yaw motion, however the roll motion is quite limited and is hence not expected to contribute.
The yawing motion is also a very likely explanation for the increase in sway, since the rotation
around z will lead to a thrust force component in the y-direction. This eﬀect could also be
explanatory for the extreme peaks for the roll motion.
The acceleration in the nacelle is presented in ﬁgure 9.14.
The acceleration in the nacelle
increases in the x-direction as wind is applied. Previously it was established that the surge and
the pitch motion decreases as wind is applied, and furthermore the model damping ratio for
surge increases from 0.05 to 0.09, which indicates that the increase in accelerations is caused
by the rotation of the rotor. The drastic increase in the acceleration of the nacelle in the y-
direction with wind is also a clear predictor of imbalance in the rotating parts of the nacelle.
The oﬀset of the probability curve for the wind case starting at around 0.5 m/s2 in x and 1
m/s2 in y supports the fact that the nacelle experiences a baseline acceleration solely due to the
rotation of the rotor. Variations in the wind ﬂow could also be contributory to the excitation
of in particular the acceleration in x.
The variations in tendon tension due to the presence of wind is shown in ﬁgure 9.15.
As expected the presence of wind causes in oﬀset in the initial tension T0, such that the upstream
initial tendon tension increases by 24 % and the downstream tendon has a lower initial tendon
tension which is 39 % lower.
For tendon 2 the variation in tension is very similar to the case without wind, whereas for

Structure 2
In ﬁgure 9.16 the six modes of motion is presented as exceedance probability
plots. The wind appears to have the exact same eﬀect on the translation motions (surge, sway
and heave) of structure 1 and 2, causing a decrease in the surge motion and an increase in sway
and heave.
The roll motion on the other hand is generally greater for structure 2 as wind is applied, but the
deviation in whether the turbine rolls to one side or the other is reduced with wind compared
to structure 1, but also compared to the no wind condition for structure 2.
The damping of the pitch motion is much less pronounced for structure 2. This is expected since
the pitch motion of structure 2 is driven by the displacement in surge of the ﬂoater, opposed
to structure 1 which is driven by nacelle displacement in relation to the ﬂoater surge. Since
the nacelle has a greater displacement for structure 1 and hence expected to have a greater

The diﬀerence in nacelle acceleration in x is greater for structure 2. As previously stated the
nacelle motion of structure 2 is smaller, and therefore the eﬀect of the induced vibrations from
the rotor and the transmission mechanism will become more apparent. A common trend in the
acceleration signal in x for structure 1 and 2 is the oﬀset in accelerations between no wind and
with wind situations, indicating that the presence of wind causes a constant ampliﬁcation of
the acceleration.
The presence of wind and rotation of the rotor causes the acceleration in y to become similar
in magnitude to the acceleration for structure 1, in spite of having a lower acceleration in y
without wind.
The tendon tension is presented in ﬁgure 9.18.
A slight decrease in tension variation is evident for both tendons as wind is applied to structure
2. The oﬀset in initial tendon tension is 38 % higher for tendon 2 and 29 % lower for tendon 4
in the presence of wind. Compared to structure 1 there is an increase of 11% in initial tension
in the upwind tendon 2 for structure 2, while the tension in the downstream tendon is 14 %
higher for structure 2 compared to structure 2.
Structure 3
The response of the six modes of motion and the nacelle acceleration for struc-
ture 3 is included in the appendix, and generally the behavior in response of structure 1 and
3 is common, but a diﬀerence is observed in the response in sway which actually reduced for
structure 3 as wind is applied.
The tendon tension in the three tendons are presented in ﬁgure 9.19 The presence of wind also
leads to an oﬀset of the initial tension of all three tendons. The variation of the tension in
the upwind tendon is very similar for the minimum tension, whereas a small decrease in the
extreme maximum values is evident. A similar eﬀect is present in the downwind tendons.
Tendon 1 for structure 3 is experiencing the greatest increase in mean tension to a level which
is 43 % higher than the initial tension. The initial tension in the downwind tendons on the
other hand does not drop as much as for structure 1, and is 23 % lower for tendon 2 and 21 %
lower for tendon 3 in comparison to the no wind case for structure 3.

This section has shown that surge and particularly the pitch motion of all three structures are
reduced due to the presence of wind. Especially the larger displacement in surge and pitch are
damped signiﬁcantly, which agrees with the fact that the wind damping is a function of the
nacelle velocity squared. A few events in the pitch motion for structure 2 and a single event in
the surge motion for structure 1 violates this tendency, but a closer inspection of these events
is necessary in order to determine the cause for this.
The sway motion increases in for structure 1 and 2, whereas it is reduced for structure 3 once
wind is applied. An oﬀset of the initial/mean tension in the tendons for all three structures
occurs due to the wind, and generally the pattern in the variation of the tendon in relation to
the oﬀset initial tension is quite similar for the cases with and without wind.

9.3
3D eﬀects
In this section the response of the model in irregular 2D and 3D waves are compared. 2D waves
are more simple to analyse, but ocean waves are in fact 3 dimensional, and therefore it is of
great interest to determine which eﬀect that might be missing, when conducting experiments
in 2D wave climates.
It was decided to let sea state 8 serve as a basis for the 2D-3D analysis, given that the largest
response is expected to occur at this sea state. In order to single out the eﬀect of having 3D
waves the results from the tests with no wind are presented.
Ideally the wave climates are supposed to be comparable in terms of wave height at the location
of the model, however as ﬁgure 9.20 suggests the waves in the 3D wave climate are consistently
smaller in magnitude up to the 97 % quantile, whereupon the two wave climate are comparable,
ending with a couple of extra large waves for the 3D wave climate.
As outlined in section 7.1.1 this discrepancy is due to an excessive wave height of the uni-
directional waves, and it is naturally a factor which must be taken into account in the evaluation
of the diﬀerences in response. The diﬀerence in peak period for these two sea states is within 6
%. Throughout this section the black curves represent the response in the unidirectional wave

Structure 1
Figure 9.21 is the response of the model in the six modes of motion.
The surge motion in ﬁgure 9.21a appears to have the same trend as the distribution for wave
heights, since up to the 98 % quantile the response in surge is higher for the unidirectional
waves.
The 3D wave climate induces an increase in sway motion, which is due to the ﬂuctuating
hydrodynamic force components in the y-direction, which will naturally excite the motion in
sway. The decrease in surge for the 3D waves is not comparable to the increase in sway of the
model, hence indicating that the total motion in the x-y plane increases for 3D waves.
The heave motion is within the limits of inaccuracy, but the 3D waves tends to cause a larger
heave motion.
As for the sway motion the roll motion is also induced to a greater extent in the 3D wave
climate, which probably is a consequence of the transverse wave force components. However
the increase in roll motion is relatively low compared to the increase in sway. Even though not
evident from ﬁgure 9.21d it was investigated that 3D waves did not lead to a dominating roll
direction, as it for instance was the case in the previous section, where a clear dominating roll
direction was evident once wind was applied.
The pitch motion is considerably lower in with spreaded waves. The motion in the x direction
(surge and pitch) being smaller for the 3D waves, but larger in the y direction agrees with the
fact that as the signiﬁcant wave height is supposed to be equal for the unidirectional and the
spreaded waves, the hydrodynamic force in the longitudinal direction will not possibly become
as large for the spreaded waves.
The yaw motion is slightly increased for the 3D waves, which very likely could be due to the
transverse ﬂuctuations of wave loading on the spokes, causing a rotation of the model.
The acceleration of the nacelle is presented in ﬁgure 9.22
The acceleration in x agrees with the decreased pitch motion of the model in 3D waves. Likewise
the increase in acceleration in the y-direction for 3D waves is a result of the increased sway and
roll motion.
The normalised tendon tension in tendon 2 and 4 is presented in ﬁgure 9.23 Given that the
overall motion of the model seems to increase in the 3D wave climate, it is surprising that
the tendons are generally experiencing less stress compared to the unidirectional case. The
consistent decrease in the minimum and maximum tendon tension proves a close link to the pitch
motion, also in 3D waves, since the pitch motion is the only motion with a similar consistent

This section has shown that in a 3D wave climate the pitch motion is reduced signiﬁcantly,
the motion in y increases drastically, while a change in surge is not really evident. Moreover
the ﬂuctuation in the tendon tension for all three structures is lower in the 3D wave climate.
Hence conservative values for the pitch motion and the tendon tension are obtained when
modelling in a 2D wave climate, whereas the yaw and in particular the sway and roll motion
are underestimated.
9.4
Misalignment of wind and waves
In this section the change in response is investigated for the case where the wave direction and
the wind direction are misaligned by an angle of 30◦. This is accomplished by changing the
wave heading angle to Θ = 30◦. Attention will in particular be given to changes in the wind
damping.
To enable an appropriate comparison of the response for diﬀerent wave heading angles it is
175
==PAGE:194==
CHAPTER 9. RESULTS
requisite to consider the motion in a rotated coordinate system which is aligned with the wave
heading direction. In the case of misaligned/oblique waves the surge and sway motion can be
projected onto the rotated coordinate axes (xobl, yobl) by taking the dot product between a unit
vector along the axes of the rotated coordinate system and the surge and sway motion in the
original coordinates.
ξ1,obl =
(
ξ1
ξ2
)
·
(
cos(30◦)
sin(30◦)
)
(9.2)
ξ2,obl =
(

ξ2
)
·
(
−sin(30◦)
cos(30◦)
)
(9.3)
The roll and pitch motion angles are assumed to be small and hence the transformation of the
roll and pitch motion is conducted by the transformation of a reference point at the tower 1
meter above the point of ﬂotation to the oblique coordinate system.
ξ(15),obl =
(
ξ1 + sin(ξ5)
ξ2 −sin(ξ4)
)
·
(

)
(9.4)

(

ξ2 −sin(ξ4)
)
·
(
−sin(30◦)
cos(30◦)
)
(9.5)
The computation is reversed in order to get the pitch and roll motion of the model in the
oblique coordinate system.
ξ4,obl = arcsin(ξ(24),obl −ξ2,obl)
(9.6)
ξ5,obl = arcsin(ξ(15),obl −ξ1,obl)
(9.7)
A similar transformation is conducted for the nacelle acceleration signal, and throughout this
section, the model motion will only be considered in a rotated coordinate system for tests with
oblique waves. The rotation of the coordinates does not eﬀect the heave or yaw motion as well
as the tendon tensions.
To illuminate any discrepancies in the response caused solely by the change of wave heading
angle, the response in the cases of no wind for Θ = 0◦to Θ = 30◦are included in the analysis.
Since the impact from the thrust force is largest for sea state 5, this sea state forms the basis
for the analysis of misalignment of wind and waves. To compare and highlight the diﬀerences
of the response in the six degrees of freedom and the nacelle accelerations for wind and no-wind
tests, the mean is subtracted from the signals before they are presented.
An exceedance probability plot of the wave height is shown in ﬁgure 9.28, such that a potential
diﬀerence in wave heights can be accounted for. From ﬁgure 9.28 it is evident that up to the
70 % quantile the wave heights are roughly equivalent in size. Between the 70 % and the 95 %
quantile the deviation is still small, whereas the largest 5 % of the waves are around 10 - 20 %
larger for the waves with Θ = 0◦. This deviation must be taken in to account when comparing
the response across the diﬀerent wave heading angles.
Structure 1
A comparison of the response in the case of alignment and misalignment of
wind and waves is conducted in ﬁgure 9.29. For completeness the response in all six degrees of
freedom is plotted, but due to the limited heave response which lies within the uncertainties of
the Qualisys system, this motion will not be considered.
First the eﬀect of wave heading angle is investigated by comparing the black (Θ = 0◦) and the
blue (Θ = 30◦) curves, which both represent tests without wind.
The change of the wave heading angle from Θ = 0◦to Θ = 30◦entails a slight decrease in
surge motion but a consistent doubling of the sway motion. Further, the roll motion increases,
176

Figure 9.28: Distribution of wave heights in the irregular sea state 5 for unidirectional waves
with Θ = 0◦and Θ = 30◦
and the pitch motion is generally reduced by 20-30% , while the yaw motion is practically
unchanged. The nacelle accelerations presented in ﬁgure 9.30 show that the change in wave
heading angle entails a corresponding decrease in the x-acceleration, and an increase in the
acceleration in the y-direction
An additional graphical presentation of the change in the surge, sway, roll and pitch motion as a
result of the change in wave heading angle, is shown in ﬁgure 9.32. Here the signiﬁcant increase
in sway, and the more modest reduction in surge for Θ = 30◦, is observed as an increasingly
elliptical motion in the surge-sway plane.
The increase in roll and decrease in pitch is seen as a more circular object in the pitch-roll plane
for Θ = 30◦.
A similar analysis was conducted for a number of regular waves, where the response is more
simple, the results are plotted in ﬁgure 9.33. The motion of the foundation depicted for seastate
6 in ﬁgure 9.33a is roughly following a straight line along the x-axis, while a very distinct elliptic
motion is apparent in sea state 7 and 8. For the regular waves with Θ = 0◦the motion is a
straight line and the eﬀect of ellipticity is not observed. The wave frequencies of regular waves
7 and 8 are 0.77 Hz and 0.63 Hz respectively, which for the latter case is coinciding with the
natural yaw frequency. This leads to the thought that this might be governing for the elliptic
motion, but no signiﬁcant excitation of the yaw mode was observed. At sea state 9 with a wave
frequency of 0.57 the sway motion is limited again.
Despite a very low amount of energy in the irregular sea state 5 at the yaw natural frequency of
0.63 Hz, energy is deﬁnitely present at 0.77 Hz, being the wave frequency of sea state 7, which
in the regular wave form showed signiﬁcant elliptical behaviour. Hence the elliptic motion is
excited as proven in ﬁgure 9.32.
Further insight to this behaviour can be attained through the study of the tendon tension time
series, shown in ﬁgure 9.34 for regular wave number 8. When the waves strike with an oblique
angle of 30◦the two tendons upwind and two tendons downwind are not equally sharing the
loading, and the tendons being more parallel to the wave heading direction take up more of the
loading, at least for structure 1. This change in loading of the tendons, also changes the nature
of the restoring moment generated from the tendons, which is likely to lead to a reduction in
surge and pitch and increase in sway and roll.
In the light of these changes to the motion, the wind damping eﬀects for the two wave heading
angles are compared. Comments will given on changes from the state of aligned wind and waves
(Θ = 0◦) to a state of oblique waves with a wave heading angle of Θ = 30◦.
The damping in surge is insigniﬁcant and hence reduced below the 90% quantile, but is main-

experienced for the co-directional wind and wave test.
For Θ = 30◦, the damping of the surge motion is only observed above the 50% quantile, while
for Θ = 0◦the damping is evident for a larger proportion of the surge motion. This is likely
related to the aforementioned decrease in surge motion.
In the sway mode, the wind appears to have quite diﬀerent impact on the motion for the mis-
aligned case compared to the aligned case. Up to the 95% quantile the diﬀerence between the
wind and no wind case is quite small for the misaligned case compared to the aligned case, and
for P < 0.05 the sway motion is in fact damped.
The damping of roll motion is generally unchanged compared to the unidirectional case of wind
and waves, which implies an evident damping of positive roll while the damping of negative roll
which is against the wind, is less signiﬁcant.
The pitch motion is damped above the 70% quantile, and here it is signiﬁcantly less damped
compared to the case of aligned wind and waves, where wind damping is clearly present for
P < 0.8.
The yaw mode is dominated by a wind excitation, which increases the response signiﬁcantly
compared to the no wind test. Further it is observed that the yaw response is slightly increased
for P < 0.5 compared to the case of aligned wind and waves.
An increase in the nacelle accelerations due the wind and the resulting rotor eﬀects is maintained
when the wave heading angle is Θ = 30◦.
The distribution of minimum and maximum tendon tension, which is presented in ﬁgure 9.31,
appears to be relatively unaﬀected by the wave heading direction, but is instead mainly governed
by the wind.
A simple comparison of the minimum and maximum tension experienced by all four tendons
for the oblique wave with no wind is presented in table 9.1. This table shows that the minimum
and maximum tension occur in tendons 2 and 4, which justiﬁes the choice of primarily focusing
on the tension in these tendons.
As stated earlier there is a tendency of exciting an elliptic motion of the model when Θ = 30◦.
This is conﬁrmed by compared the surge-sway and pitch-roll motion for the waves with Θ = 0◦
and 30◦, respectively, which is done in ﬁgure 9.32. Due to the small angle for roll and pitch, the
angular displacement can also be interpreted as a translatory motion of the nacelle in relation
to the foundation.
Structure 2
The response of structure 2 to irregular wave number 5 with headings of 0◦and
30◦and with and without wind is shown in ﬁgure 9.35.
First the eﬀect isolated eﬀect of the oblique waves is studied for this structure, by comparison
of the black (Θ = 0◦) and the blue (Θ = 30◦) curves.
In surge the the response is slightly reduced when the wave heading is changed to Θ = 30◦, an
in sway the response is signiﬁcantly increased, which was also observed for structure 1. The roll
motion for structure 2 is diﬀerent from structure 1 in the sense that up to the 60 % quantile
the trend is equal for the 0◦and 30◦heading. The behavior in pitch shares some characteristics

Hence the eﬀect of the wave heading angle on the ﬂoater motion is very similar to the eﬀect on
the ﬂoater motion for structure 1. An analysis equivalent to the one in presented in ﬁgure9.33
showed that regular wave number 6 in addition to 7 and 8, also excites the elliptical motion.
Again a coincidence of the yaw natural frequency which now is raised to 0.81 Hz and the wave
frequency of 0.87 Hz for regular wave number 6 is observed.
The wind damping eﬀects for structure 2 in the two wave heading angles are compared. Com-
ments will given on changes from the state of aligned wind and waves (Θ = 0◦) to a state of
oblique waves with a wave heading angle of Θ = 30◦.
The damping of surge is very limited and similar to the damping of surge for structure 1. The
eﬀect of wind in the sway motion is marginal part from a few extreme observations above the
99% quantile, and hence the wind does not have the same excitation eﬀect when the waves are
oblique ad in the aligned wind and wave case.
The presence of wind has a little eﬀect on the roll motion, but for pitch, the observations above
the 90% quantile are increased compared to the no wind case, whereas for the co-directional
waves and wind, these are damped by the wind.
As for structure 1, the yaw motion for structure 2 is mainly governed the wind load and the
heading angle is clearly secondary.
The nacelle acceleration is presented in ﬁgure 9.36. The diﬀerence in acceleration in x is quite
signiﬁcant for structure 2 compared to structure 1, whereas the acceleration in y does not change
remarkably. This is the case with and without wind, but the change in nacelle acceleration
as wind is applied is greater for the oblique waves. These acceleration results shows that the
nacelle is kept even more ﬁxed, when the tendons become more parallel and perpendicular to
the wave heading.
The tendon tension is sketched in ﬁgure 9.37. Apart from keeping the nacelle more at rest the
tendon tension is actually also signiﬁcantly reduced for structure 2, compared to structure 1,
which had a very similar distribution in tendon tension.
Again a comparison of the minimum and maximum tension experienced by all four tendons
for the oblique wave without wind is conducted in table 9.2. In relation to structure 1 the
distribution of minimum and maximum tension is quite diﬀerent. The upwind tendons are
loaded less than the downwind tendons, and the diﬀerence between the pair of tendon 2 and 4
and the pair of tendon 1 and 3 is insigniﬁcant.
Structure 3
The response of the six modes of motion and the nacelle acceleration for struc-
ture 3 is included in the appendix. Generally the response is quite similar to structure 1, but
a key diﬀerence is the sway response, which only diﬀerentiate between the 0◦and 30◦cases for
P < 0.05, which is quite unique compared to structure 1 and 2.
The tendon tension in all three tendons are presented in ﬁgure 9.38 Due to the change of
heading angle the y-axis becomes parallel with spoke/tendon number 2. As a consequence the
distribution in tension in tendon 1 and 3 becomes very similar independent of the presence of
wind.
179
==PAGE:198==
CHAPTER 9. RESULTS
In this section it has been shown that the damping of the pitch motion is signiﬁcantly re-
duced for structure 1, 2 and 3 when the thrust force and hydrodynamic force are misaligned
by an angle of 30◦. In fact an increase in the pitch motion is observed for structure 2 for the
largest 10 % of the pitch displacements.
However the change of wave heading angle, and hence change in orientation of the mooring sys-
tem in relation to the wave heading direction lead to a phase shifted tensioning in the tendons.
This resulted in an elliptic motion of the model, which implies an increase in the sway and roll
motion and a reduction in surge and in particular pitch. This eﬀect is more pronounced at
wave frequencies that are coincident with the yaw natural frequency, but since the yaw motion
is not excited signiﬁcantly, it can not be directly demonstrated that the elliptic motion is a
consequence of the yaw natural frequency.

9.5
Wave Steepness Dependency
Inspired by the work of [62], a down-crossing analysis was conducted for all three structures in
the severest sea state, SS 8 with wind wave heading of 0◦for the 3 hours full scale realisation.
For each wave the maximum ﬂoater acceleration in surge, the maximum upwind tendon tension
and the minimum downwind tendon tension are represented as a colored dot according to their
magnitude as a function of the depth parameter h/L0 and the deep water wave steepness H/L0,
where L0 is the deep water waves length determined from the deep water dispersion relation:
L0 = g
2πT 2
(9.8)
Figure 9.39c shows the maximum acceleration in surge for the ﬂoater. Two lines are added
to the plots to indicate the wave breaking criteria of (9.9). For a ﬂat seabed the critical deep
water wave steepness can be expressed as.
Hb
L0
= A

1 −e−1.5π h
L0

(9.9)
where Hb is the breaking wave height. Wave breaking usually occurs for 0.12 < A < 0.18, and
the limits of A represent the two dashed lines.

For structure 3 the main part of the accelerations are larger compared to structure 1 and 2,
and the occurrence of extreme accelerations is more frequent for structure 3. The acceleration
for structure 2 is generally lowest, especially for the most extreme accelerations. This is an
indication of a more gentle excitation of the ﬂoater when the tendons are inclined. Maximum
peak accelerations for the three structures are shown in table 9.3.
It is noticeable how some waves are in the wave breaking domain, but clearly are too short to
induce any signiﬁcant forcing. Likewise some waves are indeed very long, but since they are not
very steep the acceleration experience by the ﬂoater is actually quite small. The inclination of
the diﬀerent colored bands is quite close to 1, which indicate that the maximum acceleration
has equal dependency on the wave steepness and the wave length.

downwind tendons for all three structures. The tendon tensions are normalized by the initial
tendon tension τ0.

From ﬁgure 9.40a,9.40c and 9.40e it is evident that for especially structure 1 and 3 the long and

values of maximum acceleration seen in ﬁgure 9.40a and 9.40c.
What really stands out from the maximum tension plots is the uniformity of the tendon tension
for structure 2, which does not at any instance exceed 50 % of the initial tension.

It is evident that the single upwind tendon of structure 3 is loaded to a much greater extent
than the upwind tendons of structure 1, and structure 2, with a signiﬁcantly larger number
of extreme tension, distributed relatively widely. In fact the maximum tendon tension of the
upwind tendon of structure 3 becomes more than twice the initial tension, as listed in table 9.3.
The minimum tension presented in ﬁgure 9.40b,9.40d and 9.40f occurs for waves in a rather
broad span covering long and ﬂat waves along with short and steep waves, as it was the case
for the maximum tendon tension. The minimum tension in the downwind tendon of structure
2 is less critical compared to the two other structures and does not at any point become less
than 50 % of the initial tension. It is interesting how the minimum tensions in the downwind
tendon of structure 3 is less frequent compared to structure 1, given that the loading is only
shared by three tendons.
The wide distribution of the maximum and minimum tension questions the method of correlat-
ing the tendon tension to a wave steepness, but for the ﬂoater surge the analysis is appropriate.
9.6
Focused Waves
This section presents the measurements of selected focused wave tests, with and without wind.
As mentioned in section 6.2, it was attempted to produce a focused wave of the New Year wave
type, but this was unsuccessful. Nevertheless these tests still represent the impact of a single
large wave group on the FWT, and for the largest focused waves they have the potential of
giving insight to extreme wave eﬀects. All measurements are presented in their time series to
the left and with the corresponding power spectrum to the right.
9.6.1
Focused wave 5 (F05)
Figure 9.41 shows the surface elevation at the location of the FWT for focused wave number
5, which has a height of 0.12 m, see table A.
Structure 1
The response of structure 1 to this wave is shown in ﬁgures 9.42 and 9.43,
which present surge, pitch and nacelle x-acceleration and tendon tensions respectively.
An
initial oscillation of the FWT model in surge for this test is observed in ﬁgure 9.42a. It is
clear that this motion is not built up during the ﬁrst seconds of the test, and therefore the
explanation to this motion must be that the FWT model had not come completely to standstill
before this test was started. It is noted, however, that the amplitude of the oscillation is in the
order of 2mm, which is very small compared to the diameter of the foundation.
When the wave group impacts the FWT, the surge peak displacements build up, such that the
largest surge actually is observed after the wave that follows the largest peak surface elevation,
and not as a direct result of the largest wave. This is due to the rather transient behaviour of
the excitation force amplitude. After the impact of the wave group, the FWT model performs
free surge motion with a slowly decaying amplitude.
The response in pitch is very limited and seems to be within the level of expected uncertainty
part from during the impact of the wave group. Here, a closer look reveals that the largest
191

positive pitch displacement is obtained right after the largest peak in surface elevation. The
limited pitch response means that the proportion of noise in the measured signal is larger,
which is identiﬁed as more peaks and a more even distribution of energy in the power spectrum.
However, peaks at the surge, roll and pitch natural frequencies and at the wave peak frequency
are clearly identiﬁed. It is observed that the pitch mean and nacelle x-acceleration mean both
are oﬀset from zero. This is naturally not intended, but for the pitch measurement this is likely
due to a settlement in the mooring system, which in the order of only 0.6 mm will result in a
pitch oﬀset of 0.1deg.
The nacelle x-acceleration, anac,x, in ﬁgure 9.42c shows that the FWT model starts to vibrate
at a frequency close to the roll and pitch natural frequencies right before the primary wave
group arrives. Due to the spatial resolution on the Qualisys system, these very small amplitude
vibrations were not detected in the pitch signal shown in ﬁgure 9.42b, but are easily captured
in the acceleration measurement. A possible source to these vibrations is found by analysing
the surface elevation before the arrival of the primary wave group, see ﬁgure 9.41b. For this
part of the measurement, a dominant proportion of the energy is situated near the roll and
pitch natural frequencies, and therefore model excitation in these modes is likely to be caused
by these waves. It is surprising, however, that no distinct change in the surface elevation can
be observed from the time series, which could suddenly initiate the structural vibrations. This
behaviour was also observed for the tests of the other structures in this wave, but it was not
observed for the tests of the remaining focused waves.
From ﬁgure 9.43 it is evident that the tendon tensions are not equal in mean, but are paired
192
==PAGE:211==
CHAPTER 9. RESULTS
diagonally such that tendons 1 and 3 are nearly equal in tension and tendons 2 and 4 are nearly
equal in tension. Further, it is observed that none of the tendon tensions reach the critical
design values of 0 or 2τ0. The appearance of the time series resemble that of the nacelle x-
acceleration, and no signiﬁcant reaction to the surge motion is observed. The spectrum conﬁrms
this by showing very little energy at the surge natural frequency but signiﬁcant energy at the
roll and pitch natural frequencies.
This clearly shows how the tendon tensions are mainly
governed by pitch rather than surge motions. The mentioned model pitch and roll vibrations
before the arrival of the wave group is also felt in the tendon tensions.
Structure 2
For structure 2 the response to focused wave number 5, F05, is equivalently
presented in ﬁgures 9.44 and 9.45 respectively.
The ﬁrst ﬁgure shows that surge performance of this structure is very similar to that of structure
1.
The pitch motion, however, observed in ﬁgure 9.44b for structure 2, is increased signiﬁcantly
compared to structure 1, with a peak to peak value that has gone up from ∼0.5 deg to
∼1deg. An increase in pitch motion was expected as a consequence of the inclined tendons
that induce a combined surge/pitch motion in which the pitch and surge motions are 180deg
out of phase. This eﬀect was also observed in the simulations based on the 3DOF numerical
model, see sections 4.4.4 and 4.4.5. After the passing of the primary wave group, the pitch
signal is dominated by a strong surge frequency component, which is also a sign of the very
strong coupling between surge and pitch. In the spectrum this component is represented by
a signiﬁcant peak at the surge natural frequency. Further, a peak at approximately twice the
surge frequency is observed and since the surge and pitch motions for this structure are strongly
coupled, this indicates second harmonic response of the structure in pitch. Mathematically, the
second harmonic response can arise as a consequence of of the quadratic terms that show up
when evaluating the particle acceleration by the chain rule, the so-called convective terms, and
these contributions result in a forcing on the structure which is described by the inertia terms
in the Morison equation. For linear wave theory, the ﬂuid particle velocities are given to the
MWL, meaning that the Morison load based on linear wave theory is determined by integration
only to this level. The eﬀect of integrating the Morison load to the actual surface is second
order and could lead to second harmonic excitation forcing. However, as the forcing is inertia
dominated, the surface elevation is zero when the force in the x-direction is maximum, which
means that this eﬀect is likely small.
The nacelle accelerations in the x-direction before the arrival of the primary wave group are
also observed for structure 2, and the accelerations are of the same magnitude as for structure
1. The discussion on the cause of these accelerations for structure 1 also applies for structure
2.
During the primary wave train, the nacelle x-accelerations are reduced signiﬁcantly for
structure 2 compared to structure 1. For structure 1, the maximum acceleration magnitude is
∼1.5m/s2, while this number for structure 2 is reduced to ∼0.3m/s2, which is a reduction of
approximately 80%. This reduction is a consequence of the coupled surge/pitch motion which
keeps the nacelle more steady.
The frequency spectrum shows a high frequency content of the nacelle acceleration in the range
1 −2 Hz at 6 distinct peaks. The ﬁrst one from the left is likely to be related to the peak
frequency of the wave spectrum while number 3 and 4 might be related to the roll and pitch
frequencies of the structure. The second peak is situated approximately at the sum of the surge
frequency and the wave peak frequency, and the sixth peak is situated approximately at the
double peak wave frequency, meaning that they could be a result of second harmonic excitation.
Another explanation to these peaks could be an occurrence of so-called artiﬁcial higher har-
monics which in [63] are observed for the discontinuous onset of a high frequency oscillation
to an oscillation of lower frequency. They are denoted artiﬁcial because they have no direct
physical meaning, but occur as a result of the Fourier transformation. For the acceleration
193
==PAGE:212==
CHAPTER 9. RESULTS
measurement, a couple of response groups with relatively sharp cut-oﬀs are observed, which
makes the signal resemble that in [63], and hence add to the likelihood that these peaks are
artiﬁcial higher harmonics. A more thorough analysis, possibly with the aid of numerical tools,
should be conducted to determine the origin of these peaks.
For the tendon tensions, presented in ﬁgure 9.45, the same pattern as for the nacelle x-
acceleration is observed. The tendon tensions prior to the arrival of the primary wave group
is of the same magnitude as for structure 1, but at the impact of the large waves, the ten-
sion is signiﬁcantly reduced compared to structure 1. Here the tensions are within the range
∼[0.9, 1.1]τ0 compared to ∼[0.7, 1.3]τ0 for structure 1. The spectra of the tendon tensions
show some of the same characteristics as the spectrum for the nacelle acceleration, and since
the time series also are similar the same discussion of second harmonics and artiﬁcial harmonics
applies.
Structure 3
The response of structure 3 to focused wave number 5 is generally similar to
that of structure 1, and therefore the results have been included in the appendix, ﬁgures B.9
and B.10. However, structure 3 shows a slight reduction in the peak to peak pitch response
from ∼0.5 deg to ∼0.4 deg but includes a more distinct surge component.
The tendon tension of the upstream tendon has peaks at ∼1.5τ0 and ∼0.6τ0, whereas the
downstream tendons have peaks at ∼1.2τ0 and ∼0.8τ0. Hence the upstream tendon comes
closer to the design loads than it is the case for the tendons of structure 1.
Eﬀect of wind
The changes in the response of the three structures when subjected the
focused wave number 5 and rated wind speed will be described in the following, based on the
results presented in ﬁgures B.15 to B.20 in the appendix. The three structures all experience
a mean displacement downwind in surge, but the direct response to the primary wave train is
similar to the no wind case, when seen relative to this new equilibrium point. For structures
1 and 3, the wind seems to be capable of exciting surge motion at the surge frequency even
before arrival of the wave train. For structure 2, the free surge motion after wave impact is
evidently more damped than the no wind case, despite the insigniﬁcant wind damping at the
primary wave group.
The pitch response of the three structures at the impact of the primary waves is evidently
damped, such that the maximum peak to peak pitch is reduced by 40% for structure 1, 20% for
structure 2 and 25% for structure 3 . Further, a superimposed rotor 1P frequency at 1.18 Hz is
especially evident for structures 1 and 3. The surge component in the pitch signal for structure
2 is observed to be heavier damped than in the no wind case.
The rotation of the rotor induces a vibrations at a number of distinct frequencies, which are of
small amplitude and cannot be observed in the output from Qualisys, but are clearly observed
in the nacelle acceleration measurements for all three structures. In addition to a peak at the
3P rotor frequency, peaks are observed at ∼4.0 Hz, 5.2 Hz, 6.4 Hz and 7.6 Hz. The origin of
the peak frequency at ∼4.0 Hz was not identiﬁed, but it is clear that it is related to the rotor
dynamics, since this eﬀect was not observed in the no wind version of the same experiment.
Suggestions such as the structural natural frequencies of the blades in the ﬂapwise mode and
the the natural frequency of the generator and rotor suspension, were brought up, but no tests
to verify or reject these suspicions were conducted. This spacing between the peaks corresponds
to the 1P rotor frequency, meaning that the measured vibrations likely are a result of a coupling
between a structural natural frequency at 4.0Hz, tower shadow and rotor imbalance eﬀects.
The wind induced rotor thrust induces an overturning moment which is balanced by a higher
mean tension in the upwind tendons and a reduced mean tension in the downwind tendons,
which is evidently observed in these tests. This results in higher peak tensions for the upwind
tendons that reach ∼1.7τ0, ∼1.5τ0 and ∼1.8τ0, and a reduced peak tensions for the downwind
tendons of ∼0.4τ0, ∼0.5τ0 and ∼0.6τ0 for structures 1, 2 and 3 respectively. Hence in the
194
==PAGE:213==
CHAPTER 9. RESULTS
wind case, the tendon tensions are closer to the design limits. Further, the 1P rotor frequency
is evident in the tendon tensions, especially for structure 2.

Figure 9.46: Surface elevation at TLP location for focused wave number 8 (F08).
Structure 1
The surge response of the FWT model to this wave is shown in ﬁgure 9.47a, and
as for F05 it is observed that the maximum surge peak response is obtained after the passing
of the wave that follows the largest peak surface elevation.
The pitch response, which is presented in ﬁgure 9.47b, shows that as for F05 the largest peak
pitch response is obtained right after the largest wave. The largest peak to peak pitch displace-
ment has gone up by a factor of 2.5 from F05 to F08, which is a little below the ratio of the
wave heights. After the passing of the primary wave group, a weak surge frequency component
is evident in the pitch response, and a corresponding peak in the spectrum is observed. Here it
is also observed that the FWT model pitch frequency is not excited by this wave, as opposed to
focused wave number 5, F05, which is explained by a vanishing level of energy at this frequency
in the wave spectrum, ﬁgure 9.46. However, a small peak is observed at ∼1.2 Hz, to which
the most likely explanation is that it lies within the peak of the excitation force computed in
WAMIT, see ﬁgure 4.7, where 1.2Hz corresponds to 0.15Hz in the prototype WAMIT results.
With a large wave excitation force, noticeable response can be observed despite a modest level
of wave energy at this frequency.
This eﬀect is also felt in the nacelle x-acceleration that shows a peak at ∼1.2 Hz, see ﬁgure
9.47c.
Figure 9.48 shows the tension in the four tendons. As the FWT model pitches in the positive
direction upon the passing of the largest wave, the tension in the upstream tendons (1 and
2) increases to ∼1.7τ0, while the tensions in the downstream tendons (3 and 4) decreases to
∼0.2τ0. Next, the model moves upstream and when it obtains its peak negative pitch the
upstream and downstream tendons obtain peak values of ∼0.5τ0 and ∼1.9τ0 respectively. It is
evident that the downstream tendons experience the largest peak to peak diﬀerence in tendon
tension and come close to the design criterion for maximum tendon tension given in section
4.3.1.
As it was seen for focused wave number 5, F05, surge motion is not evident in the tendon
time series, although the spectra reveal a weak component. The spectra further show a peak
at ∼1.2 Hz which was also observed for pitch and nacelle acceleration in ﬁgure 9.47, and is
attributed to aforementioned magnitude of the wave excitation force at this frequency.
Structure 2
The response of structure 2 to focused wave number 8 is included in the ap-
pendix, ﬁgures B.11 and B.12, and will be commented in brief. The ﬂoater surge is very similar
200
==PAGE:219==
CHAPTER 9. RESULTS
to the one seen for structure 1 in ﬁgure 9.47a, and the pitch peak to peak value is ∼5 deg,
which signiﬁcantly larger than the value of ∼1.3 deg observed for structure 1.
As it was observed for focused wave number 5, the nacelle acceleration also for focused wave
number 8 is reduced by approximately 80% for structure 2 compared to structure 1.
The
peaks of the tendon tension for structure 2 lie within ∼0.7τ0 −1.4τ0 which is is a signiﬁcant
improvement compared to structure 1, which reaches values of ∼0.2τ0 and ∼1.9τ0 and therefore
is closer to the design limit.
Structure 3
The ﬁgures presenting the response of structure 3 to focused wave number 8, is
included in the appendix, ﬁgures B.11 and B.12. The surge and nacelle acceleration of structure
3 is very similar to that of structure 1, but structure 3 experiences decreased pitch motion as a
result of the wave impact by approximately 30% compared to structure 1. The upwind tendon
of structure 3 reaches peak tensions of ∼1.9τ0 and ∼0.2τ0, and thereby comes close to the
design requirements without exceeding them.
Eﬀect of wind
As the rotor is parked and the blades pitched in environmental state number
8, the aerodynamic eﬀect of the wind is insigniﬁcant to most of the measured parameters. It
was observed, however, that for structure 1 the pitch peak response was slightly reduced and
the downwind tendons reached minimum values of 0.1τ0. The response for the three structures
is found in ﬁgures B.21 to B.26. in the appendix.
The presented focused wave tests have on a time series basis given insight to the response
of the diﬀerent structures. It was conﬁrmed that surge and pitch are in phase for structures 1
and 3 with the vertical tendons, while they are in antiphase for structure 2 as a result of the
inclined tendons. In addition it was observed that the maximum surge occurs after the wave
that follows the largest surface elevation, and not immediately after the peak surface elevation.
Further observations included a likely second harmonic excitation of the pitch mode for struc-
ture 2, and the eﬀect of a large wave diﬀraction force which increased the pitch response at a
frequency with limited energy for F08. Previously described eﬀects such as wind damping in
pitch, limited nacelle acceleration for structure 2 and the eﬀect of the mooring system on the
tendon tensions were also observed. However, undesirable eﬀects such as ringing, slack tendons
and resulting snap loads were not observed, but it is possible that these would be present for
focused waves with the 50-year design wave height.

Chapter 10
Conclusions
In this thesis, the design for a compact TLP foundation to support the DTU 10MW reference
wind turbine is suggested. The ﬂoating wind turbine (FWT) was constructed in scale 1/60 and
tested under a large number of conditions with simultaneous exposure to wind and waves. These
included regular waves, long-crested and directionally spread irregular sea states, long-crested
and directionally spread focused waves as well as white noise waves and a steady wind ﬁeld. The
tests were carried out in the oﬀshore-basin at Danish Hydraulic Institute (DHI), and the wind
ﬁeld was formed by a wind generator, which was designed and constructed as part of the project.
For the purpose of the design, a simple dynamic rigid body model with surge, pitch and heave
degrees of freedom of the ﬂoating WT was set up with a hydrodynamic load modelled by the
Morrison equation, and an aerodynamic load modelled from the mean wind speed at hub height.
In addition, WAMIT simulations of the submerged part of the foundation were conducted, from
which added mass and damping could be determined and included in the dynamic model. The
WAMIT simulations also enabled an estimation of response amplitude operators, and diﬀrac-
tion excitation forces. A simple foundation geometry was chosen, consisting of a cylindrical
and fully submerged ﬂoater section connected to a cylindrical transition piece, which extended
to the tower base.
In the selection of the foundation dimensions, the overall goal was to construct a compact
foundation, which eventually is likely to be cheaper in production and thereby will lower the
cost of energy. Prototype diameters of 18 m and 9 m were chosen for the ﬂoater and transition
piece, respectively; and a freeboard of 20 m was chosen for the transition piece to prevent the
maximum wave in the 50-year sea state from reaching the platform at the tower base. A ﬂoater
submergence of 12 m was chosen as the smallest dimension to maintain full submergence under
the same wave, giving the transition piece a total length of 32 m. A ﬂoater height of 25 m and a
spoke length of 31.2 m were chosen based on dynamic model simulations of two load cases and
by requiring a maximum angle of the tendon with vertical of 10 deg and requiring an avoidance
of slack tendons, respectively.
In line with the tendency on the market to move towards larger turbines, it was chosen to apply
the DTU 10MW reference wind turbine in the present work. It was decided to maintain hub
height of 119 m for this turbine, which, with the chosen transition piece freeboard, meant that
the lower 20 m of the speciﬁed tower were replaced by the foundation. A ﬁnite element model
was set up to evaluate the eﬀect of this change on the natural frequency for the ﬁrst bending
modes of the tower, and it was found that the coupled pitch/tower bending natural frequency
was right below the 3P frequency band for the turbine.
A 1/60 model of the prototype foundation and WT was constructed, resulting in a model
with a rotor diameter of 3 m and a hub height of 2 m. The deﬁned prototype foundation
and associated environment were Froude-scaled, while the WT rotor was performance-scaled
204
==PAGE:223==
CHAPTER 10. CONCLUSIONS
to account for the diﬀerence in Reynolds numbers and to give the right thrust in model scale.
This was done by increasing he chord by 80% and by applying the slim airfoils SD7003, SD7032
and SD7062 that perform well at low Reynolds numbers, which in model scale are in the order
of 20-50%. However, the lift/drag ratio of the prototype blades cannot be obtained for the
model scale rotor, which makes it impossible to scale the mechanical power correctly, while
matching the thrust. The foundation was constructed in PVC and acrylic glass, with steel
spokes, which resulted in a construction with signiﬁcant ﬂexibility. Hence, the resulting model
natural frequencies in pitch were approximately half the magnitude of the WAMIT predictions
which assumed a rigid foundation but tendon ﬂexibility.
Further, a nacelle with a collective pitch mechanism was designed from scratch, and equipped
with servo motors that could be operated by remote control to adjust the rotational speed of the
rotor and the pitch angle. The servomotors caused the nacelle to be 40% heavier than dictated
by the Froude scaling of the DTU 10MW nacelle. The nacelle, however, was the only component
with a signiﬁcant deviation in mass from the Froude speciﬁed value. The performance-scaled
blades were constructed in carbon ﬁber reinforced epoxy, using a custom-made mould.
The WT model was instrumented with tendon force gauges, a strain gauge between the tower
and the nacelle, accelerometers in the ﬂoater and nacelle, and an infra-red optical tracing system
to determine the rigid body motions of the model. Further, ten wave gauges were mounted in
the basin to strategically map the surface elevation.
Finally, a wind generator with an exit cross-sectional area of 4 m × 4m was designed and con-
structed to provide a wind ﬁeld for the experimental setup. To obtain a compact construction,
a design without a dedicated diﬀuser was chosen and the ﬂow expansion was instead carried out
over a number of guide vanes that turn the ﬂow 90 deg. In addition, four screen sections were
mounted to reduce large-scale turbulence. The resulting wind ﬁeld turned out to be reasonably
uniform with a maximum mean wind speed over the rotor area of 1.7 m/s, corresponding to 13
m/s in the prototype scale. For the environmental conditions that required higher mean wind
speeds, it was chosen to execute the experiment with the maximum wind speed and to operate
the WT as speciﬁed for this wind speed. The turbulence intensity for the wind ﬁeld was in the
order of 5%, which matches the level of many similar experiments.
The isolated wind turbine was tested in this Froude scaled wind climate, and it was shown
that when the turbine was operated as speciﬁed for the DTU 10MW prototype, it delivered
the right thrust in model scale.
The isolated wind turbine was tested in this Froude-scaled wind climate, and it was shown
that when the turbine was operated as speciﬁed for the DTU 10MW prototype, it delivered
the right thrust in model scale. The waves were generated by a hinged wave maker, capable of
generating oblique and spread waves. The discrepancy in the wave height for identical regular
sea states across diﬀerent tests was within 13% while the wave period was within 0.3%. For
the irregular waves the ﬁgure was 5.6% for the signiﬁcant wave height and 6.7% for the peak
period. The maximum wave height was reproduced within 10.5% for the focused waves.
Three mooring conﬁgurations for the ﬂoater were tested in the full test programme, com-
prising a conﬁguration with four spokes and vertical tendons, a conﬁguration with four spokes
and tendons inclined at an angle of 9deg towards the tower, and a conﬁguration with three
spokes and vertical tendons. These are referred to as structure 1, structure 2 and structure 3,
respectively.
It was shown both numerically and experimentally that for the chosen tendon inclination,
the surge and sway natural frequencies are increased by approximately 10%, while the pitch
and roll natural frequencies are decreased by approximately 10-15% compared to the vertical
tendon case.
Further, it was shown that the ﬂoater and nacelle displacements for the dominant part of the
205
==PAGE:224==
CHAPTER 10. CONCLUSIONS
response are in phase. For structure 2, these are approximately 180deg out of phase by virtue of
the inclined tendons, meaning that a positive surge displacement is accompanied by a negative
pitch angle. This strong coupling between surge and pitch increases the pitch motion by a factor
of 2 - 3 depending on the wind and wave climates. However, this also leads to a reduction of
the acceleration of the nacelle by a factor of 2 - 4 compared to the vertical tendon case.
It was shown that in a 2D wave climate the surge and pitch motion, the in-line nacelle accel-
eration, and the variation in tendon tension for all three structures increases gradually as the
wave height and wave period increases. The sway, heave, roll and yaw motion and transverse
nacelle acceleration are not correlated to the size of the wave in a similarly consistent manner,
but generally the trend is an increase in response as the waves increase in size.
The experiments show that in a 3D wave climate the pitch motion is reduced signiﬁcantly, while
the sway and roll motion is increased remarkably with factors in the order of 2-5 depending
on the mooring conﬁguration. No signiﬁcant changes in the surge motion are evident, and
the minimum and maximum tendon tensions are less critical in the 3D wave climate. Hence
conservative values for the pitch motion and the tendon tension are obtained in a 2D wave
climate, whereas the motions in yaw, and particularly in sway and roll, are underestimated
compared to the 3D wave tests.
Under the eﬀect of wind, the surge and particularly the pitch response of all three structures, is
dampened, but the damping is especially signiﬁcant for structure 1 and 3, due to a signiﬁcantly
smaller nacelle motion for structure 2. Further, the eﬀect of wind increases sway motion for
structures 1 and 2, and yaw motion for all structures, which most likely is due to rotor excitation.
The presence of an aerodynamic thrust entails an increase in the mean upwind tendon tension
and a decrease in the mean downwind tendon tension. This eﬀect increases the severity of the
peak tensions, since the aerodynamic damping in pitch is not large enough to account for the
shift in mean tension.
The misalignment of wind and waves by an angle of 30◦leads to a signiﬁcant reduction in the
damping of the pitch, and in fact an increase in the pitch motion was observed for structure
2 for the largest 10% of the pitch displacements. However, it was found that the dominating
eﬀect here was the change in mean wave heading angle to the foundation, which was shown to
lead to elliptic motion of the structure in the water plane, hence entailing an increase in sway
and roll motion, while reducing surge and pitch.
The acceleration of the foundation was shown to be strongly correlated with the wave steepness
and the wave length, where the largest accelerations were observed for waves with steepness
between 0.05 and 0.15 and a ratio of water depth to wave length between 0.7 and 2. However,
the accelerations of structure 2 were generally smaller than those of structures 1 and 3.
Finally, the impact of focused waves at diﬀerent wave heights were investigated on a time series
and spectral basis to examine possible undesirable extreme wave eﬀects such as ringing and
tendon snap loads. Notably, such undesirable eﬀects were not observed for the setup used in
the present studies, thereby supporting the functionality of the foundation design.
The tests have shown that the inclination of the tendons reduces nacelle motion, extreme ﬂoater
acceleration and variations in tendon tension, which have not been observed below 0.5 of the
initial tension and above 1.7 of the initial tension. In turn, pitch motion is increased which
leads to higher bending loads on the rotor shaft compared to the vertical tendon structures.
However, it was shown that the absolute displacement of the blade tip is reduced in the inclined
tendon case.
The ability to experimentally model the complex dynamic system of the ﬂoating wind turbine
is essential for the validation of numerical models, based on which prototype designs can be
deﬁned. However, the model structure and experimental environment, must be scaled correctly
to ensure model and prototype similarity. The present work has demonstrated a successful per-
formance scaling of the DTU 10MW rotor and has proven the ability of a compact tension leg
206
==PAGE:225==
CHAPTER 10. CONCLUSIONS
platform of simple geometry to support the DTU 10MW wind turbine in a Froude-scaled model
environment. The results have provided insight into the eﬀects of plane and spread waves, sin-
gle large waves, wind dampening when aligned and misaligned with the wave direction, as well
as the eﬀects of the mooring conﬁguration and its orientation.
For future studies it is recommended to
• Reduce the nacelle mass to enhance model and prototype similarity
• Implement an active blade pitch control mechanism to facilitate dynamic interaction
between the wind turbine and the experimental wind climate.
Further, tests in a more sophisticated and dynamic wind ﬁeld should be considered, which
enables the tests of
• Shear layer eﬀects
• Increased mean wind speed to better model post rated wind speed conditions
• Extreme turbulence and gusts
• Abrupt changes in the wind direction
• Fault conditions
• Shut down conditions
Finally, it is also recommended to test the model in the 50-year sea state, which could not be
generated in the present setup, and especially to look into the eﬀects of extreme waves of this
sea state.
In conclusion, the present work has contributed to the development and understanding of future
ﬂoating wind turbines through the design and test of a simple and compact TLP foundation for
a 10MW wind turbine, and through a successful design for a performance-scaled DTU 10MW
rotor. Future challenges include the development of a control system, and an improvement of
the wind ﬁeld.

==PAGE:315==
 
 
DTU Wind Energy is a department of the Technical University of Denmark with a unique integration of research, education, innovation and 
public/private sector consulting in the field of wind energy. Our activities develop new opportunities and technology for the global and Danish 
exploitation of wind energy. Research focuses on key technical-scientific fields, which are central for the development, innovation and use of wind 
energy and provides the basis for advanced education at the education. 
 
We have more than 240 staff members of which approximately 60 are PhD students. Research is conducted within nine research programmes 
organized into three main topics: Wind energy systems, Wind turbine technology and Basics for wind energy. 
 
Technical University of Denmark 
Department of Wind Energy 
Frederiksborgvej 399 
Building 118 
4000 Roskilde 
Denmark 
Telephone +45 46 77 50 85 
 
info@vindenergi.dtu.dk 
www.vindenergi.dtu.dk
